Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Zelman Cowen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, one of Her Majesty's Counsel learned in the law, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: SECOND PERIOD)
Advisory Council for Inter-government Relations Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.7 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Advisory Councilfor Inter-government Relations Act1976.
Airlines Agreement Act 1981 (Act No. 75 of 1981 )An Act to approve the execution of an agreement relating to air transport, and for purposes connected therewith.
Airlines Equipment Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 77 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Airlines Equipment Act 1958.
Airlines Equipment (Loan Guarantee) Act 1981 (Act No. 38 of 1981). An Act relating to guarantees of certain borrowings by a domestic airline.
Antarctic Marine Living Resources Conservation Act 1981 (Act -No. 30 of 1981 ) An Act relating to the conservation of marine living resources of the Antarctic and its surrounding seas.
Apple and Pear Export Underwriting Act 1981 (Act No. 14of 1981 )An Act relating to the underwriting of returns from the export from Australia of apples and pears.
Apple and Pear Stabilization Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 15 of 1981)An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Stabilization Act 1971, to repeal certain related Acts, and for other purposes.
Appropriation Act (No.3) 1980-81 (Act No. 55 of 1981 )An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No. I) 1980-81, for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1981.
Appropriation Act (No.4) 1980-81 (Act No. 56 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No.2) 1980-81 ,for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1961.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 16 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Gaming and Liquor Authority Act 1981 (Act No. 116of 1981)An Act to establish a Gaming and Liquor Authority for the Australian Capital Territory and for related purposes.
Australian Federal Police Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 22 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Federal Police Act1979.
Australian National Airlines Repeal Act 1981 (Act No. 78 of 1981)An Act to repeal the Australian National Airlines Actl945 and for related purposes.
Australian National University Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 106 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian National University Act 1946.
Australian Tourist Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 27 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Tourist Commission Act 1967.
Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 49 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Act 1977.
Bounty (Non-adjustable Wrenches) Act 1981 (Act No. 18 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain wrenches.
Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 17 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Act 1978, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Printed Fabrics) Act 1981 (Act No. 102of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain printed fabrics.
Bounty (Textile Yarns) Act 1981 (Act No. 103 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain textile yarns.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 113 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 198) (Act No. 114 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964 to extend it to certain licences.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 48 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905.
Christmas Island Agreement Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 107 of 1981)- An Act relating to the Christmas Island Agreement.
Coal Excise Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 19 of 1981)An Act to amend the Coal Excise Act 1949, and for other purposes.
Commonwealth Banks Act 1981 (Act No. 29of 1981).An Act to amend the Commonwealth Banks Act 1959, and for related purposes.
Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 26 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Act 1979.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued

Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Act 1981 (Act No. 74 ofl981 ) An Act to implement certain changes in Commonwealth functions.

Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 62 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Act1977.

Commonwealth Teaching Service Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.5 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commonwealth Teaching Service Act 1972, and for purposes connected therewith.

Companies Act 1981 (Act No. 89 of 1981 ) An Act to make provision for the government of the Australian Capital Territory in relation to the formation of companies, the regulation of companies formed in that Territory, the registration in that Territory of certain other bodies and certain other matters.
Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.2 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 94 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 95 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 4 of 1981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act (No. 2) of 1981 (Act No. 98ofl981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securit-ies (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies (Fees) Actl981 (Act No. 90of 1981 ) An Act relating to fees payable for the purposes of the Companies Actl98l.

Companies (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 92 of 1981 ) -

.

An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981 and for other

purposes.

Companies (Transitional Provisions) Act 1981 (Act No. 91 of 1981 )An Act to enact transitional provisions consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981.

Complaints (Australian Federal Police) Act 1981 (Act No. 21 of 1981)An Act relating to complaints made in respect of members of the Australian Federal Police, and for related purposes.

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 71 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.

Crown Debts (Priority) Act 1981 (Act No. 93 of 1981)- An Act relating to the priority of Crown debts.

Currency Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. II of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Currency Act 1965.

Customs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 64of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901.

Customs Amendment (Securities) Act 1981 (Act No. 67 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Customs Actl901 in relation to securities.

Customs Amendment (Tenders) Act 1981 (Act No. 45 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 to provide for tenders for rights to import goods at concessional rates of duly, and for related purposes.

Customs Securities (Penalties) Act 1981 (A.:t No. 46 of 1981 )An Act to provide for penalties for refusal or failure to give certain securities with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Customs Tariff Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 68 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Tariff Act1966.

Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 66of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Customs Tari.ff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975.

Customs TarilfValidation Act 1981 (Act No. 87 of 1981 ) An Act to provide for the validation of certain collections of duty of Customs.

Customs Undertakings (Penalties) Act 1981 (Act No. 47 of 1981)An Act to provide for penalties for breaches of undertakings with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Dairying Research Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 12 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Dairying Research Act 1972, and for related purposes.

Designs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 42 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Designs Act 1906, and for related purposes.

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2861

Tuesday, 2 June 1981
Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 2.15 p.m., and read prayers.
PETITIONS
The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Trans-Australia Airlines
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
Many Australians depend almost exclusively upon Trans-Australia Airlines for the provision of essential air services.
The Federal Liberal Party policy objective of selling Trans-Australia Airlines within 4 years is contrary to the national interest.
Sale of Trans-Australia Airlines would inevitably result in-
(i) the creation of a private monopoly of major airline services in Australia;
(ii) higher domestic air fares.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject outright any proposal to sell Trans-Australia Airlines.
by Mr Beazley, Mr Charles Jones, Mr Kent, Mr Les McMahon, Mr Morris and Mr Scott.
Petitions received.
Excise on Beer
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
1. The hotel industry in Australia is long-established and a valuable part of the community of this nation, providing places where the people may partake of the three basic needs of life: a place to eat, a place to drink, a place to sleep.
2. The hotel industry is accepted throughout the nation as occupying an important place in the social and economic activities of Australia.
3. The hotel industry is almost entirely within the private sector and makes a large contribution to the government in taxes of various kinds.
4. The hotel industry has a need to sustain an appropriate level of turnover and growth, to make profit, and to sustain and improve the level of employment.
5. The hotel industry of Australia is gravely concerned at published reports that the Government intends to increase excise charges on beer in the forthcoming Budget.
6. Sales of beer constitute the major source of income of most of the industry's 5,811 hotels in Australia.
7. The very steep rises in beer excise in 1975 and 1978 severely retarded the growth of the Australian hotel industry.
8. Any further increases in excise on beer in the 1981 Budget would further retard the industry at a time when growth is essential to maintaining economic viability and the ability to serve the constantly growing needs of the community.

9. Such is its significance as a community requirement, beer occupies a place on the Consumer Price Index, and any alteration to its cost to the community would have an inflationary effect.
10. Any increase in excise would result in and force the hotel industry to pass on such increases to the consumer by way of higher prices for beer.
11. Higher excise would be only the starting point for an upward spiral in beer prices because of the presence of other factors, such as State Licence Fee taxes, which are directly linked to excise rates.
12. The hotel industry of Australia already is gravely handicapped by heavy excise and other tax imposts, its growth is at a standstill; and, inevitably must decline if beer excise is increased.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that honourable members of the House of Representatives:
1. Recognise the importance and place of the Australian Hotel industry in the social and economic life of the nation.
2. Accept that the viability of the hotel industry is essential to the maintenance of employment opportunities and stability of the industry is essential to enable it to continue to provide for the hotel needs of the nation in this period of growing population and increasing tourism.
And further, that honourable members of the House of Representatives resist and reject any move to increase excise on beer.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Bourchier, Mr Carlton, Mr Fisher and Mr Les McMahon.
Petitions received.
Soviet Passenger Cruise Vessels
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives of the Australian Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
Owing to an overwhelming and continuous demand by the Australian people for a cruise service within the economic scope of the working man and his family, we request the Government to lift the ban placed on Soviet Passenger Cruise Vessels entering Australian Ports.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Robert Brown and Mr Mountford.
Petitions received.
Moreton Island: Sand Mining
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
1. That sandmining on Moreton Island poses a threat to the natural ecology or environment.
2. That Moreton Island is included on the list of the Australian Heritage Commission, and the Federal Government is urged and indeed it must, under the Australian Heritage Commission Act 1975, take into consideration the protection of the island.
3. That the Queensland Government has recently renewed mining leases against the advice of conservationists and a State Government sponsored public inquiry.

2862 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Petitions

Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House will request the Government to refuse requests for export permits covering mineral sand products.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling and Mr Humphreys.
Petitions received.
Tertia~y Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
1. The proposal to introduce fees for higher and second degrees will mean that access to post-graduate and recurrent education will be limited to the rich; Australian research and ability to cope with technological change will suffer; this proposal must be seen as a step in the direction of the introduction of fees for all tertiary students.
2. The proposed introduction of a loans scheme for students will mean graduating with a heavy debt or being denied a tertiary education ifloans are refused because a student is seen as a 'poor risk' (particularly affecting women and those doing non-vocational courses); only the rich will be able to get a tertiary education without course restrictions and the burden of indebtedness.
3. In regard to the proposal to restrict eligibility for TEAS and the foreshadowing of reviewing TEAS levels only in the light of the availability of loans, it should be noted that any further downgrading of TEAS, which is already inadequate, can only further limit access to education.
4. The proposed restriction of Adult Secondary Assistance to those needing qualifications for employment will severely restrict access to a full high school education and necessary bridging education to tertiary education for those who left school early.
5. The proposal not to fund 30 colleges of advanced education if they are not rationalised is an attack on teacher education. This attack will create teacher shortages in the mid 1980s, restrict access to education now and endanger the future quality of teaching available to Australian children.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:
(i) post-secondary tuition fees not be introduced for any courses or for any category ofstudents;
(ii) that the TEAS scheme be extended so as to more fully meet the real financial needs of students.
(iii) that a comprehensive loans scheme not be introduced. (iv) that there be no restriction of Adult Secondary
Assistance. (v) that the proposal not to fund 30 CAEs unless they are
rationalised be rescinded.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling and Mr Scholes.
Petitions received.
Health Care
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That many Australians will be financially disadvantaged by the health changes proposed by the Federal Government, especially the withdrawal ofCommonwealth medical benefits for the uninsured, and the introduction of a means test for free public hospital care;

That the health insurance changes promote inequity, inefficiency and lack of universal coverage;
That the public hospital system and community health programs will be financially disadvantaged by the abdication of Commonwealth responsibility and funding.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will take all steps necessary to reintroduce a system of health care based on ability to pay, and access to health care based on medical need.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Howe and MI' Les McMabon.
Petitions received.
Plant Variety Rights
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. We, the undersigned citizens of the Commonwealth, do humbly pray that the Commonwealth Government:
1. Note that legislation establishing plant breeders' rights in other countries has had serious adverse efl'ects, namely:
a. Virtual monopoly control of seed production has passed into the hands of a few large international corporations seeking to profit from the exclusive rights over plant genetic materials created by such legislation.
b. The varieties of seeds available have been restricted mainly to hybrids which will not reproduce truly and will not grow without the aid of artificial fertilisers and pesticides, thus maximising corporate profits without regard for the interests of growers and consumers.
c. The genetic diversity of crops has been eroded, rendering them vulnerable to disease and other environmental threats.
2. Recognise that maintenance of the genetic diversity of plant varieties is crucial to the continued well-being of the Australian nation, and take all necessary steps to preserve and promote such genetic diversity as a public resource and to prevent exclusive control over plant genetic materials from falling into private hands.
3. Defend the vital interests of Australian farmers and gardeners, independent Australian seed companies and their employees, and consumers of Australian [arm and garden produce, by rejecting any proposal to legislate for the establishment of plant breeders' rights in Australia.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Dr Jenkins and Mr Willis.
Petitions received.
Taxation: Child Care Expenses
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
A substantial contrihution to Australia's economy by the payment of taxation and to the quality of life of fellow Australians is made by parents in the work-force who have preschool children.
Taxation relief for adequate child care is necessary to enable these parents to avail themselves of child care facilities in order to take paid employment.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that taxpayers who incur child care expenses in order to earn income should be able to have those expenses exempt from income taxation in

the same way as other taxpayers can deduct business expenses from their assessable income.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Beazley.

The decisions of the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Government functions to discontinue the professional employment offices, to 'rationalise' and reduce occupational information services, to rationalise and reduce vocational psychology services, to discontinue the waterfront employment offices, to terminate the former regular servicemen's vocational training scheme, to discontinue the regular collection and publication of CES statistics of persons registered for unemployment and of unfilled vacancies, because such publication is embarrassing to the Government, are blatantly political and will in the long term prove not to be cost effective.
Your petitioners therefore pray that the Government will reverse these decisions.

Great Barrier Reef: Oil Exploration

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Tertiary Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That public investment in tertiary education in the form of Federal Government funding is an investment in Australia's future and enables Australia to meet the demands of technological innovation.
That grave concern has been generated as a result of the Government's decisions to:
(a) Abolish the Education Research and Development Committee,
(b) terminate special support for educational research, (c) scale down in-house and contracted research activities
of the Department of Education, (d) scale down participation in international education
activities, particularly UNESCO and OECD, (e) further tighten already restrictive eligibility guidelines
for TEAS,

(f) supplement TEAS assistance by means of a loan scheme,
(g) phase in tuition fees for students undertaking degrees and diplomas subsequent to an initial tertiary qualification,
(h) reduce grants to universities and colleges to take account of fee income,
(i) completely abolish 30 colleges of advanced education and
U) scale down various engineering schools in colleges of advanced education around Australia.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will rescind these regressive decisions and desist from obvious attempts to pave the way for a complete reintroduction of tertiary fees.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling.

Service Pensions
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That, as ex-servicemen are eligible to receive a service pension five years earlier than other Australian males are eligible to receive an age pension, subject to certain conditions, so too should the age at which the means test is applied to pension increases be reduced for service pensioners by five years to age 65.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will, in recognition of the service they have performed for their country, and because of reduced life expectancy as a result of that service, reduce the so-called means test free age limit for service pensioners from 70 years to 65.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling. Petition received.

Department of Social Security

To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned in the Northern Territory respectfully showeth:
That the proposed use of Darwin Airport as a refuelling and Rest and Recreation base of American B52 bombers:
Jeopardises the welfare of the citizens of Darwin and of all Australians, in that it establishes a stronger potential of Darwin being a nuclear target in any conflict between the USA and another power.

(a) Carried nuclear weapons.

Jeopardises the welfare of the citizens of Darwin, in that noise, pollution and the threat of accidential radioactive fallout will increase substantially to the residents.
Negates the potential of the Australian nation to establish itself as a nation of peace in the Pacific region and violates Australia's commitment to the Nuclear NonProliferation Treaty.

DEATH OF PRESIDENT ZIAUR RAHMAN
Mr MALCOLM FRASER (Wannon-Prime Minister)-I move:
That this House expresses its deep regret at the death on 30 May of His Excellency President Ziaur Rahman, President of Bangladesh since 1977, and tenders its profound sympathy to his family and to the people of Bangladesh.
With the tragic assassination of President Ziaur, Bangladesh has lost a leader of zeal and, I believe, integrity. Bangladesh can ill afford his loss. His party, the Bangladesh National Party, won a substantial majority in democratic elections in 1979. One of the principal reasons for the success and popularity of the late President.was his dedication to the development of rural Bangladesh and its immense population. President Ziaur worked hard to overcome the crushing problems of food shortages and poverty. Bangladesh, as many honourable gentlemen would know, has been high on Australia's list as a recipient of food aid and other assistance in recent years. President Ziaur was able to announce this year that food production in Bangladesh doubled during the previous 12 months. That was a remarkable record by any standard. In many respects this had become the primary objective of his Government. He personally spared no efforts either within his country or abroad to achieve that objective.
In the international context, President Ziaur had established himself as a leader of the least

developed countries. The late President was highly respected by everyone for his views within the North-South debate. Within the non-aligned movement and the Islamic Conference he had established himself as an independent and moderate leader. President Ziaur was a strong supporter of the Commonwealth. I had many contacts with him through that Commonwealth link. It was especially gratifying that President Ziaur attended the first Commonwealth Heads of Government Regional Meeting held in Sydney in 1978. Australia was one of the first countries to recognise the newly independent nation of Bangladesh in 1971 and we sponsored its membership of the United Nations and of the Commonwealth. In the past 10 years the friendship between our two countries has strengthened. In the past year Bangladesh was the third largest recipient of Australian bilateral development aid and an increasingly important trade partner. The late President Ziaur was aware of these links. We discussed them together on many different occasions. We had discussed on many occasions how Australian aid could best assist Bangladesh and the achieving of objectives that he had for the people of Bangladesh.
The Australian Government and the Australian people were particularly shocked by the circumstance of President Ziaur's assassination. This is the third occasion in a matter of months on which a world leader has been the subject of political violence. Words cannot express the abhorrence that Australians must feel about these events. They deserve the strongest condemnation by all people. I am sure that all honourable members of this House and all Australians will join me in condemning this brutal act and in extending sympathy to the President's family and to the people of Bangladesh.
There is one other matter which I would like to add before asking the House to support this motion. When one hears of an assassination or an attempted assassination one feels, in intellectual terms, that it is an abhorrent and brutal thing and it attracts our total condemnation. But when at the same time one knows and has developed some degree of friendship with the person involved it takes on a dimension which is obviously more immediate. It is more personal and the feelings that one has are more compelling. It becomes to an extent a personal thing as well as an intellectual condemnation of the act. I had met the President on a large number of occasions. I had come to like and respect him. I am sure that all honourable members will support the strongest possible condemnation of this act being expressed by this House.

Death ofPresident Ziaur Rahman

Dr BLEWETT (Bonython)-As a member of

Restrictions on freedom of the Press and on political activities had all been present during President Ziaur's rule. But, at the same time, he made major if halting steps towards civilian rule and democracy. He ended the one-party rule of Shiek Mujibur, held presidential elections in 1977, parliamentary elections in 1979 and local elections in 1977. He felt compelled to abolish the strict principle of secularism both as a reflection of his own commitment to Islam and in consequence of the importance to his position of the Islamic-based political groups. Yet, for all that, he did not impose the sort of extremist theocracy or religious exclusivity of other Muslim states. In foreign policy he withdrew from the very close relationship between Shiek Mujibur and India, but he did not adopt the extreme anti-Indian posture many of his supporters wanted. Indeed, his refusal to indulge in xenophobic anti-Indianism seems to have been an important element in his assassination. More generally, he pursued a policy of firm non-alignment, with an openness to the West which allowed Australia, the first Western country to recognise the new state in 1971, to build a close relationship.

a parliamentary delegation from this Parliament to Bangladesh, where we were welcomed by the late President of Bangladesh, I wish to add a few words on this motion. President Ziaur Rahman was, for all practical purposes, an authoritarian military leader. But to leave assessment there without regard for the tumultuous history of his country or for the inappropriateness of Western values and classifications in an environment vastly different from that of our own would be to do great injustice to the man and to his work.
Ambitious as the President undoubtedly was, it was an ambition not for himself but for his people and, above all, for the peasants of Bangladesh. The President led a completely unostentatious lifestyle, continuing to live with his wife and two sons in a modest apartment in the military cantonment in Dacca. In a society frequently damaged by political corruption, his personal integrity and honesty were never impugned by his critics or by his enemies. He could be ruthless, which was a virtual necessity for survival in the politics of Bangladesh but in nearly all cases his firmness was tempered by justice and magnanimity. In the last years of his rule there had been a distinct improvement in the respect for political

President Ziaur will be remembered most of all for his rejection of the economic philosophy summarised in the phrase 'trickle-down effect', a theory which has few credible adherents these

and human rights in Bangladesh. In the election of 1979, for example, opposition parties scored a greater representation than in any previous Bangladeshi or East Pakistani parliament.

days. Briefly, this philosophy was that as long as President Ziaur Rahman dedicated himself to

the rich of a developing country got richer, as long the alleviation of the poverty of the rural masses

as an industrial, bureaucratic and technological through a massive village development program.

strata of society. President Ziaur, himself injeep, and on foot throughout the villages of

corruptible, believed in grass roots development. Bangladesh. Indeed, it has been argued that no

2872 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Questions Without Notice

contemporary Asian ruler had so intimate a contact as ruler with the masses of his people. It is a tragedy for Bangladesh that it should have lost a President with so much of his potential unfulfilled. It would be an even greater tragedy if his death opened a further bloodstained chapter in the troubled history of that country.

Mrs CHILD-My question is addressed to the Minister for Communications. Mr SINCLAIR-Yes. PRIVATE AIRCRAFT: NAVIGATION
FEES Mr PORTER-Is the Minister for Transport .ware of the concern being expressed by aircraft owners and the Aircraft Owners and Pilots Association of Australia in particular about increases in private aircraft navigation fees? Are the suggestions of increases of up to 300 per cent for private aircraft and of up to 700 per cent for charter aircraft navigation fees correct? Can the Minister inform the House of the current position in relation to the proposals for fee increases?

Mr HUNT-I am aware of the concern of the general aviation industry at the general aviation study that was commissioned in 1978 to report on cost recovery in the general aviation sector. At present there is more than a 100 per cent cost recovery for the international airlines. Under legislation before the House the domestic carriers will have to meet approximately 100 per cent of cost recovery. The regional airlines are currently meeting about 30 per cent and general aviation about 14 per cent. However, the recommendations contained in that study, are a matter of great concern to the general aviation industry which is fundamental to aviation in this country.
I announced to this House not very long ago that we would be extending the period until 30 June to enable the general aviation industry to report formally to the Government on the recommendations contained in that study. At that time. I said-I repeat-that the Government will not be taking any decisions that will undermine the viability of this very important section of the aviation industry in Australia.
In due course I will be discussing this matter with my colleagues in the Government in the light of replies that have been received from various sectors of the general aviation industry. I know that the honourable member for Mitchell addressed a large gathering of the general aviation industry in Sydney recently. He made it quite clear to that gathering that the Government had not committed itself to the recommendations in that report.

COFFS HARBOUR: FM BROADCASTING Mr IAN ROBINSON-I refer the Minister for
Communications to a Press statement issued today to members of this House and another pl&':!e regarding FM broadcasting proposals for the Coffs Harbour area. I ask the Minister to clarify the action necessary under the Broadcasting and Television Act regarding the calling for and issue of FM public broadcasting licences. Was a preliminary planning proposal for broadcastingboth FM and AM-on the mid-North Coast issued late last year? Was time allowed for further submissions on the proposal, as provided for in the Broadcasting and Television Act? Are these submissions currently under consideration? Mr SINCLAIR-It is certainly true that radio station CHY is anxious to go on the air. I can understand that. It has made a series of representations to me, as it did to my predecessor. Indeed, as I understand it, the draft planning proposals were first discussed between Mr Staley and representatives of the station in September 1979. There are, however, quite real problems in developing a comprehensive plan for both amplitude modulation and frequency modulation broadcasting in the north coast area. I hope that it will be possible to come forward with final proposals shortly.
INDIAN OCEAN PEACE ZONE Mr BEAZLEY-I draw the attention of the Minister for Foreign Affairs to the fact that the United Nations ad hoc committee on an Indian Ocean peace zone will meet in New York this week to determine whether a conference at ministerial or heads of government level will take place this year on a peace zone proposal. I ask the Minister: What instructions has he given to the Australian delegation on the conference issue? Does the Australian Government intend to continue to oppose a conference in 1981 as it did at a similar meeting last March? If so, why? Does the Government remain committed to the Indian Ocean peace zone proposal? If so, will it entertain participation in a conference on this matter should it be held this year despite any opposition from the Australian Government?
Mr STREET-It is quite true that the Australian Government shares the objective of the zone of peace concept for the Indian Ocean. Recent events-first and foremost, of course, the Soviet invasion of Afghanistan-have made that extremely difficult to achieve. We believe that should a conference be held it would best be held in an atmosphere in which it had some chance of success. We do not come to the judgment that the present circumstances are likely to result in a successful conclusion. We would prefer to wait until that is a possibility.

WELFARE HOUSING PROGRAMS
Mr LEO McLEAY-I have a short question for the Minister for Housing and Construction which I hope will elicit a concise answer. My question relates to an answer which the Minister gave on Tuesday, 26 May, to the honourable member for Bendigo about the net value of Commonwealth payments to the States for housing. I ask: What is the total value of repayments of capital and interest due to be paid to the Commonwealth by the States in 1981? What amount ofrepayments will be incurred under the new arrangements for 1981-82? Is it not a fact that next year's repayments by the States will exceed the value of funds they will receive under the new arrangements for housing assistance next year?
Mr McVEIGH--I have to admit that I do not have the exact figures. The situation is that Commonwealth-State housing agreements have been in existence since 1945. In the first year of the arrangement a sum of slightly in excess of $13m was made available. Under last year's proposals the sum of $285m was made available. Quite obviously, each year when the States are repaying those sums with interest over a term of 53 years they are paying back more than they did the previous year. Consequently, quite obviously as we approach 1998 the repayments will reach their height. It is wrong for the honourable member for Grayndler to try to equate payments of interest and redemption by the States over 53 years with a contribution by the Commonwealth in one year. It must also be remembered that the States do not repay money to the Commonwealth with their own funds; those repayments are made by the ordinary citizens, the Jacks and Jills of Australian society. They come from the ordinary, hard working Australian who pays his or her weekly rent or monthly repayment. They do not come out of the coffers of the State. I am glad I have been given the opportunity of at least educating the honourable member for Grayndler--
Mr Uren-Mr Speaker, I raise a point of order. I inform the Minister that an amount of $200.3m was repaid this year by the States to the Commonwealth.
Mr SPEAKER-There is no point of order. I suggest that the Minister return to the figures.
Mr McVEIGH-These payments are made out of the household savings and income of the ordinary person. It is wrong, therefore, for the honourable member for Grayndler to try to imply that the States are repaying this money. When I look at the figures I am astonished sometimes at the generosity of the present Government.

Mr SPEAKER-I ask the Minister to bring his answer to a conclusion.
Mr McVEIGH-Very generous terms are made available by the Commonwealth. In the legislation which we will be debating later today, we are making moneys available at an interest rate of 4+ per cent, notwithstanding the current rates. Of course, general interest rates under the Labor Government showed an alarming increase from 1972 to 1975. Bank loan interest rates went up from 7 per cent to 11 per cent.
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable gentleman will draw his answer to a conclusion.
Mr McVEIGH-On account of the generous terms made available to the States, the States, by taking the money, repaying it and recycling it, and after meeting all administrative and maintenance costs, are able to show a surplus of $142m this financial year. The honourable member's question was very long and important and I wanted to answer it, unlike my usual style, in some depth.
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable gentleman will bring his answer to a conclusion or I will require him to resume his seat.
Mr McVEIGH-I conclude on this note: The sum total of funds made available for welfare housing in Australia is made up of the very significant contribution madc by the Commonwealth, plus those surpluses plus input by the States. I would only hope that when the State Premiers come to---
Mr SPEAKER-Order! The Minister will resume his seat.

2878 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Questions Without Notice

soon as possible so that we could get to an agreed position, if that is possible. In relation to health insurance, the Commonwealth's position and the position of these parties is very well established. We believe it is incumbent upon people who can afford to do so to make some provision for their health care costs. AUSTRALIAN WOOL TESTING AUTHORITY
Mr BUNGEY-I direct my question to the Acting Minister for Primary Industry. What savings and/or benefits will result, firstly, to the Government, secondly, to the Australian Wool Corporation, thirdly, to wool growers, and fourthly, to wool users, by the disposal of the Australian Wool Testing Authority?
Mr SINCLAIR-I cannot quantify the exact sums that will be saved in accordance with the matter the honourable gentleman put forward, but I think a couple of things need to be recognised. The first is that to date, the Australian Wool Testing Authority has been run entirely under the embrace of the Australian Wool Corporation. Whilst it is true that the Australian Wool Corporation is funded, essentially, from levies that result from legislation passed through this House, those funds do represent growers' funds. They represent money that at the moment is set aside in a way which covers the operation of the reserve scheme and the degree to which funds are necessary to buy wool and to maintain the level of the general reserve established by the industry each year. They also cover various expenses relating to the administration of the wool stores and so on. The constitution of the Australian Wool Testing Authority, as a new body, will enable the freeing of those moneys that are presently used through the Australian Wool Corporation's account. It is hoped that, by the involvement of all those concerned in the industry, growers' money can henceforth be used for other purposes of the Australian Wool Corporation rather than for the Australian Wool Testing Authority.
Secondly, the Australian Wool Testing Authority operates in a commercial area. It is a body which gives certain undertakings. As a result of

discussion between the Minister for Primary Industry and the Prime Minister, agreement has been reached on the basis of the continuity of the necessary guarantees to ensure that the certificates provided in that area are maintained and the guarantees required by the trade continued. Indeed, there has been an understanding that there will be no hiatus in the change from the present constitution to the new constitution to ensure that there is no interruption to wool selling procedures. It is true that, as it is a body that is operating in the commercial market place, there seems to be a very real reason for it to be constituted, therefore, in the market place. The exact basis on which it is to be constituted, however, is a matter which will be finally concluded between my colleague the Minister for Primary Industry and the industry to ensure that all the requirements of the industry are met. However, I believe that the freeing of the money from the Australian Wool Corporation's account which will result from its being separately constituted will ultimately mean a saving of money to the wool grower.
CONTROL OF NUCLEAR ACTIVITIES Mr WEST-I direct my question to the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment. To what extent will regulation and control of nuclear activities be devolved on the State and Northern Territory governments, as was so vaguely outlined in the Review of Commonwealth Functions? Does the Government intend eventually leaving environmental assessment of uranium projects, waste disposal techniques, mining safety procedures and production levels to the decision of competing State governments? Will this proposed reduced Federal responsibility involve amendments to the Atomic Energy Act and other Federa11egislation? If it is not intended to proceed on these matters, will the Minister now tell us why these outrageous proposals were included in the razor gang's report in the first place?
Mr WILSON-It was made quite clear in the Prime Minister's announcement of the decisions arising out of the Review of Commonwealth Functions that the Commonwealth's essential interests in this matter would be maintained. The extent to which authority and responsibility are handed over to the Territory in respect of environmental matters will be against that background of the retention by the Commonwealth of its essential interests. Its concern and interest in relation to the uranium industry are clearly known to members of this House.

UNEMPLOYMENT BENEFIT
Dr THEOPHANOUS-My question, which is directed to the Prime Minister, refers to his reply to my question without notice on 11 March which concerned the proposed removal of the unemployment benefit for people under the age of 18 years. Will the Government end speculation and anxiety on this matter by completely rejecting this proposal now? If not, when will a determination be made on this matter? Further, when will the Government announce plans for financial assistance to people under the age of 18 years so that more young people-especially from low income families-can afford to remain at school and gain reasonable qualifications and skills?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The first part of the honourable gentleman's question cannot be answered until such time as we, in co-operation with the States and the education authorities, have established education and training opportunities for all young Australians, especially those young Australians who now do not have opportunities because there are not the courses that attract their inclinations or meet their talents. Of course, that is something that we have been pursuing for the last several months, very much as a result of a report of the Schools Commission to which the honourable member for Wills drew attention a week or so ago. I understand that my colleagues have made a good deal of progress with the States and the Cabinet has had reports from them in relation to that. Some of the results of these reports will be revealed when the Minister for Education makes a statement to the House at the appropriate time.
Quite plainly, when some tens of thousands of young people under the age of 18 years are presently in receipt of the unemployment benefit, it indicates the great task that has to be undertaken in making sure that the education system has places for all those who otherwise would be in that category. That is the first thing that we have to do, if we can, in co-operation with the States.
The second thing, of course, is a budgetary matter; that is, an examination of the adequacy of allowances. I am sure the honourable gentleman would not be interested to know-I will advise him in any case-that I was Minister for Education when the present secondary allowance scheme was introduced. I was concerned that the

trend towards more and more young Australians staying at school for the last two years of secondary schooling would pose a problem and a difficulty for low income families. Part of this examination is to test the adequacy of the allowances that might be available if young Australians can be attracted into full time training.
It is only when these two things have been done adequately and to the satisfaction of the Government that we would then consider that third question. If one has a situation where there are good and appropriate-appropriate to the talents and needs of individuals--training opportunities available for all people under the age of 18 years and if at the same time one has the situation where the interests of low income families are protected, it is then, but only then, fair enough to ask that other question about the unemployment benefit. So nobody need have any fear about the circumstances that exist at the moment. Indeed, I believe the more the community understands our objectives in these matters the more it will applaud our efforts to make sure that there are adequate training opportunities for all young Australians. That is certainly much better than that which the present system offers them-a school system which is not suited to their needs, that does not attract them, but leaves them with a feeling of frustration and failure which, more often than not, is the fault of the school rather than the students. Then that becomes compounded by a great difficulty for people in this category being able to get a job. We owe those young Australians something much better than that. The Government has set about trying to achieve something better than that. I hope that we would have the support of all honourable members in that objective.

PERSONAL EXPLANATION Mr MORRIS (Shortland)-Mr Speaker, I claim to have been misrepresented. Mr SPEAKER-Does the honourable gentleman wish to make a personal explanation? Mr MORRIS-I do. Mr SPEAKER-He may proceed. Mr MORRIS-In the Daily Mirror of yesterday's date, on page 8, in a feature column entitled 'PS' edited by one Debbie Tarrant, there appears an article headed 'Taking refuge in Hansard'. The article purports to cover the proceedings surrounding last Thursday morning's incident at

Personal Explanation

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2881

Question Time. It states that following the day's proceedings 'MPs and their staff rushed for a copy of Hansard, the official parliamentary transcript, on Friday to examine at what exact point of Mal's grandiose delivery' certain incidents occurred. It states that 'persons noted with extreme interest that a number of not quite-in-order comments' were made by me. Without going into all the text of the article-it is there to be read-the article also notes that Hansard failed to record the interjections that I made. It concludes:
But somehow those normally thorough transcriptions omitted the verbal interruptions.
The article notes that as being peculiar. I find the article malicious. Its inferences are without foundation. It implies that the Hansard record was suppressed by me, by Hansard or by someone else. It implies that my interjections were not a coincidence. They were a coincidence to subsequent events. The article labels as peculiar the Hansard staff not having recorded interjections and questions the propriety of the interjections. For the record, my interjections were 'what about a dollar for the kids'?' and 'This is costing about $12m'.
Mr Speaker, you are aware and I think can confirm that the practice followed by Hansard in recording interjections is that unless the interjections are responded to they are not recorded. That is my understanding of the practice followed by Hansard. Accordingly, the interjections were not suppressed by me. I did not see the greens. In fact I did not read the Hansard of the events until I noticed this article yesterday evening. I do not believe anybody else suppressed the interjections. No member of the Press Gallery rushed around on Friday morning looking for a copy of Hansard or looking for information on the interjections. I was in this building and I saw no members of Parliament or Press reporters gallivanting about in the building. Given the privations being suffered today by thousands of women and children of broken families, I find it regrettable that rather than recognising the plight of these unfortunates, particularly those in women's refuges, the columnist chose instead to impugn my integrity for making the comments which I made, and which I stand by, and to impugn the integrity of Hansard for carrying out its duties in its traditional manner.
Mr SPEAKER-In relation to part of what was said by the honourable member for Shortland, it is the practice of this House, on the direction of the Presiding Officer-I have maintained that practice-that if an interjection is made but is not noticed by the person speaking at the time, it is not recorded in Hansard. The matter raised by the honourable member also gives

me the opportunity to make a personal explanation. Sometimes an interjection heard by members of the House may be offensive but I do not order the interjection to be withdrawn because the person speaking has elected to proceed with his speech. Therefore, the interjection is not included in the Hansard record. This is particularly the case when a second reading speech or some set-piece statement is being delivered to the House.

AUSTRALIAN PARLIAMENTARY DELEGATION TO PERU, VENEZUELA
AND BRAZIL
Mr JARMAN (Deakin)-by leave-I present the official report of the Australian parliamentary delegation to Peru, Venezuela and Brazil. Mr Speaker, I wish to pay a tribute to the leadership which you gave in the early part of the delegation's visit and to the leadership which Senator Young from another place gave during the rest of the visit. I would also like to pay tribute to the work and assistance given by Mr John Dyer, the secretary to the delegation. Without his support

2882 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Australian Parliamentary Delegation

and constant attention to detail, I am sure that the delegation would not have achieved the success which I believe it did. I know I express the views of all members of the delegation in that regard. I believe the delegation did a great deal to further good will between Australia and the South American nations we visited.
Mr LES JOHNSON (Hughes)-by leave-I join with the honourable member for Deakin (Mr Jarman) in paying tribute to the leaders of our delegation to South America and to the staff members who assisted in such an effective and assiduous way. There are very great lessons to learn from South America. We learned a great deal, first of all, about South American hospitality. Mr Speaker, it was an honour for the Parliament that you were accorded something like the freedom of the city of Cuzco when we visited that place. It was an historical visit as it was the occasion when the country was transferring from dictatorship to democracy. It is significant to say that we were treated with the utmost importance.
Peru is one of the most impoverished countries in the world, and it has a very high rate of inflation. It seems to me that the new regime will have an extremely difficult time in overcoming those problems. The new President, Fernando Belaunde Terry, is setting out to create one million jobs through co-operatives and to create state companies. He also aims to reduce the foreign debt to 25 per cent of exports. I understand that the inflation rate was running at approximately 160 per cent, although nobody knew the precise situation and there were various accounts of it. The state debt, for example, was $6 billion.
One could gain the impression in Venezuela only that it was a country on the move. There was a very great sense of pride about the achievements and about projects that were being put in hand. It was very interesting to note the size and success of the state-owned gas-fired steel industry; the iron ore mining company, which also is a state instrumentality; the state aluminium company, which is in partnership with a Japanese concern. Enormous hydro projects are under construction, and pine growing projects have resulted in 160 million trees being planted on 350,000 acres. One of the most impressive industrial developments in Venezuela is that pertaining to the oil industry. The state-owned oil company, Petroven, which has been operating since 1975, has the inside running and is returning enormous profits to the people. We visited the great oil centre of Lake Maracaibo, where over 1,000 oil wells are producing about 900,000 barrels of oil a day, or 80 per

cent of the nation's production. It is very interesting to note that the state-owned company has taken over responsibility for exploration, refining, transportation and marketing. This kind of thing is happening on a very wide scale throughout South America generally.
The literacy rate in Venezuela presents enormous problems, being at a very low level. Onequarter of the adult population is illiterate. There is a very inadequate distribution of the nation's income, with 20 per cent of the population earning two-thirds of the national income and the poorest 20 per cent earning only 8 per cent of the national income. There is a great deal to be learnt from the enterprising spirit in evidence in that country. In Brazil we could be only impressed by the effectiveness of the state oil body. A great deal is being done to develop ethanol as a major fuel. At present the Volkswagen car industry is orientated mainly to the use of ethanol, and of course the Volkswagen car manufactured in that country is the predominant vehicle. I understand that probably 250,000 alcoholic cars, if I could use that term, will be using this fuel by the end of the year. Australia could learn a great deal from this process, which converts sugar cane to a very effective fuel. Apparently, it is 50 per cent cheaper than ordinary petrol but slightly less efficient, and the overall net effect is very advantageous.
A wealth of information is contained in the report which has been tabled today. Regrettably, the report does not mention-it could not mention-the visit which was made to Chile on an unofficial basis as the delegation was returning from its official itinerary. The regime in that country has caused enormous disruption and has subjected many people to very great injustices. It has subdued the trade union movement and, in many ways, has reduced the whole cause of democracy to a most demeaning state. Any member of the delegation who took part in that unofficial visit to Chile would be able to provide information that could be of extremely great value to people who are interested in the pursuit of democracy. I pay tribute to you, Mr Speaker, as the leader of the delegation in Peru, and to Senator Young, who led the delegation for the other parts of the journey. I believe that enormous benefit has been derived as a consequence, certainly for Australia and, I hope, for the people of Brazil, Venezuela and Peru.
Mr SPEAKER-Unfortunately, I could not complete the journey and went only to the installation of President Belaunde in Peru. I regret that I could not carry forward because the delegation

Proposed Stifting Hours

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2883

was an excellent team. Its members applied themselves most assiduously to finding out information, which was the purpose of the journey. I make a comment in passing about Peru, which has experienced the most extraordinary set of circumstances. President Belaunde was elected by democratic process and overthrown by what was termed a left wing military coup. It must have been the most unique military coup in the world. Shortly afterwards, there was a subsequent military coup on the persons who themselves had formed government by military coup. After 10 or 12 years-I have forgotten exactly-the military government got into such a mess that it decided to turn government over to the democratic process once again. Lo and behold, who should win the election but President Belaunde, who had been overthrown by a military coup a decade earlier. The return to democracy was very much appreciated by the people, and we were very glad to have participated in the triumphant return to democracy of one of the great South American states.

The different hours proposed after some discussions were quite different, in the sense that at the present we are locked into two situations. One is not to sit during meal hours. I believe it is not necessary for us to rise during meal hours. I add that if we sit during meal hours the debate would not want to be interrupted by calls for quorums or divisions. There would have to be an understanding between all honourable members that quorums and divisions should not be called during the meal break. There is no reason why the business of the House cannot go forward during that time. That is a point that can be discovered whether it works or not. The other point that must be made. I think is that we have been locked in by a second factor, which is that under the Standing Orders the proceedings commence with prayers, petitions, et cetera, and, most importantly, questions without notice. In other parliaments of the world questions without notice, or the particular variation of that procedure in that parliament, do not necessarily follow immediately after prayers and petitions.

PROPOSED SITTING HOURS Mr COHEN (Robertson)-I seek your indulgence, Mr Speaker, to raise a matter. Some time last year I approached you and also spoke in this House about revised sitting hours. This matter has been discussed by the parties and throughout the Parliament for a period of 12 months. I understand that you are proposing to take some action regarding the sitting hours. Can you inform the House what you have in mind?
Mr SPEAKER-I will be happy to do that. In the time. I have been in this chamber there have been many occasions on which members have wished to change the sitting hours to suit contemporary requirements. Experiments have been carried out on a variety of times, but after a short period each of those experiments has failed and we have returned to the sitting hours which I have known throughout the period I have been here, except for very minor variations. The honourable member for Robertson (Mr Cohen) raised this matter with me. Generally, it was apparent that the time had arrived when a great number of honourable members wished the issue of sitting hours to be looked at again. Therefore, I felt that it was a responsibility in that I had to do what I could to bring the matter forward for determination. Consideration was made by certain members of the Government parties who then approached me for my response. I thought the most convenient way to determine the matter was to reduce it to two alternatives, that is, to continue with the present hours or with different hours.

I have had discussions with party leaders. It has been indicated to me by party leaders on both sides of the House that before a response is given they would wish to have the matter discussed in the party rooms, that is, the Caucus or Government party rooms. What I had in mind is that if there is a consensus established by the party room meetings we could formulate the provision to go ahead. Otherwise it is my intention, unless there is specific request to me from the parties not to do so, to ask the Clerk to conduct a ballot amongst honourable members in which they would be able to opt between two alternatives-the present sitting hours or a new set of sitting hours.
Mr COHEN (Robertson)-Mr Speaker, thank you very much for that answer. Very briefly, as this matter has now occupied the time of honourable members, both in the House and out of the House, for over a year and as we are coming to the end of the session it seems to me that as we are grown adults it is about time we made a decision. I hope that we will have discussions in our party room tomorrow as I hope discussions will occur in the Government party room so that the ballot can be conducted this week or next week. Then some provision can be made for the next sitting of parliament.
Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Leader of the House)-Mr Speaker, I seek your indulgence to address a few words on this same matter.
Mr SPEAKER-The right honourable gentleman may proceed.

2884 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Proposed Stiffing Hours

Mr SINCLAIR-A paper has been distributed by the honourable member for Robertson (Mr Cohen) and by Senator Martin. Unfortunately I did not hear the full text of your comments to the House, Mr Speaker, but quite obviously there is a shared concern that there is a basis on which the present sitting hours can be modified in any way to meet the requirements of honourable members and yet still maintain the general available opportunity for Government Business and private members' business. The Government has had some discussions about the matter. It believes it is necessary perhaps to develop further propositions than those now before us. For example, on other occasions there have been propositions relating to two-week back-to-back Tuesday to Friday, Monday to Thursday proposition, with two weeks up. Some honourable members are attracted to that type of proposal.
I am aware, Mr Speaker, that the group of which you have been chairman has been looking at some alternative proposals. The Government feels that perhaps still more work is needed to be done, not just for the purpose of looking at those proposals before us but perhaps to look at some of the other dimensions as to how we can accommodate adequately party committees, parliamentary committees and those other very important parts of a member's conduct of parliamentary business such as the interviewing of constituents and so on. Therefore, it will be a matter which will be discussed in the Joint Party Room tomorrow on the basis of looking at perhaps a wider range of options than is included just within the proposal that you, Mr Speaker, commented on a few momentsago.
The honourable member for Robertson suggested that the matter is urgent. In agreeing with that I still think it is necessary to determine a proposal that is workable. Whatever proposition is put forward I think we need to test it in relation both to the demands of the front bench Executive and the Opposition parties as well as the need for Cabinet to meet and to debate the wide range of matters that seem to come up for consideration both for it and in consultation with various groups.
We are anxious in that whatever proposals go forward that we try to meet generally the wishes of honourable members. We do not wish to do that without consultation with the Opposition or with members of Parliament as a whole. I hope that through party room discussion tomorrow we might be able to progress the matter a little further. I throw some caution into the arena as far as our being necessarily able to resolve the proposition finally at that time.

Mr SPEAKER-Unfortunately, I say to the right honourable Leader of the House (Mr Sinclair) that everything he has put is predicated on a decision by the Executive; whereas it has been put to me by honourable members who have approached me that it is a parliamentary matter. As I see it and as I have been informed by party leaders, they wish the matters to be discussed at party meetings. It is well known that at the request of members from both chambers the President and I had drawn up some ballot papers which could be used to ascertain the wishes of honourable members and honourable senators.

Discussion of Matter of Public Importance

The refusal of the Government to provide for public funding of political parties for election campaigns.

These questions ought to be asked: How was the $5m or $6m that was spent by the Liberal and Country parties in the 1980 election campaign obtained? Who were the donors? Were any agreements reached with the donors? Have any favours been granted by the Government to the donors? All these qllestions have to be answered to satisfy the Australian people. The allegations that public funding is corrupt, that it is unacceptable, without any serious substantive argument in support of them are not acceptable either in the Parliament or outside the Parliament among members of the public.
Let us look at some of the things that are on record. I was campaign director for the Labor Party in 1972. Mr Murdoch has gone on recordthis is not something I would have made public because the agreements between political parties and donors are such that the parties try to keep everything secret-since 1972 and said that he made a donation of $74,000 to the Labor Party in 1972. In 1972 Mr Murdoch had one television station, in Adelaide in one of the smaller States, and he had a number of newspapers around Australia. But let us look at what has happened to Mr Murdoch's empire since 1972. Let there be no mistake about it; Mr Murdoch now stands 100 per cent behind this Government-101 per cent, if one likes. He not only donates to the Government parties but also uses his newspapers regularly to support this Government, to do everything possible to see that the Liberal Party is in control both at the national level and the State level. We had the disgusting display of Press bias in South Australia two years ago when the Corcoran Government was overturned partially by the Murdoch empire. Now, Mr Murdoch wants television stations in Sydney and Melbourne. He wants the airways. What additional things would Mr Murdoch do for the Liberal Party that he would not even have thought of doing for the Labor Party in 1972? The answer must have come as a bit of a shock to him in 1972 when he asked Mr Whitlam to make him high commissioner to the United Kingdom. Mr Whitlam said: 'You must be joking'. But Mr Murdoch said: 'But I have made a big donation'. It was not on.
What is MrMurdoch getting now? Many questions are being asked in the Parliament about the draft of the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal legislation? The Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair) says: 'Certain discussions have been going on with Mr Murdoch'. Everybody in Australia believes that substantial donations are coming from the business people in this country, including Mr Murdoch, to the Liberal and Country parties. This is one of the reasons why

the Liberal and Country parties are absolutely terrified of the legislation which has been introduced in New South Wales, which will give us a decent e1ectorallaw at last. It will not be long before every other Parliament follows. Every other State will follow and so will the Commonwealth, because the Labor Party, the largest single political party in this country, is pledged to bring about this reform. Reform in this area we will have. People opposite say that the business world has no influence in the Liberal Party. Those opposite should ask Dick Hamer how much influence business has in the Liberal Party. He has been on television for the last four days telling us that it was not his colleagues who sacked him; it was big business in Victoria that sacked him. That is the influence that big business has on the parties that sit opposite. Those parties carryon with so much nonsense about the system proposed by the Labor Party.
If the Prime Minister leaves Australia in two or three weeks time and wanders around the world he will meet and shake hands with some very prominent citizens in the other Western countries. Mr Fraser is on record in Australia--

Part of the test of the capacity of a political party in a democracy to sustain itself over the years is its capacity to attract a degree of financial support. The failure of the Labor Party in this area is part and parcel of its failure as a major party in our system. It is utterly wrong and an utter perversion of proper principles of parliamentary democracy to fall back upon the taxpayers and to force a contribution from every taxpayer in this country towards the activities of a political party that he does not support. That is what this legislation involves. It is not democratic to force a member of the Labor Party to pay, through taxes, to help finance the activities of the Liberal Party. The legislation is a cynical exercise designed to serve the political ends of the Australian Labor Party. The opposition of the Liberal Party in this place and in parliaments throughout Australia is based upon principle. It is based upon the soundly based proposition that part and parcel of the role of a political party is to gather financial as well as physical and electoral support.
Public funding is a long cherished objective of the Labor Party, not only in New South Wales but all over Australia. I remember the maiden speech of the honourable member for Port Adelaide in 1974 when he spoke on that subject in this Parliament. He has spoken on that subject at regular intervals ever since. He is committed to the view that taxpayers' money ought to be used to finance the activities of political parties in this country. I put it to the honourable member for Port Adelaide and to the House that not only does the Labor Party want the New South Wales taxpayers to pay off the campaign debts of the ALP in that State, which were incurred not only in fighting State elections but also in fighting Federal elections, but it also wants that practice to be duplicated all over Australia. The fight that is being waged in New South Wales now by the Liberal and National Country parties is not a fight in respect of New South Wales politics alone. It has implications that go far beyond the State of New South Wales. It has implications all over

Not only does this legislation strike at the fundamental principle that taxpayers' money should not be used to pay off the campaign debts of the Labor Party or, indeed, of any other political party in Australia; it also strikes at another very important principle in a democracy, that is, the right of privacy and the right of anonymity of political support. In supporting this legislation the Labor Party is saying to anybody who makes only a modest contribution to a political party that that person has no right to preserve the privacy of his support for that political party. The thing that really matters in the legislation that has been passed by the New South Wales Parliament is the enormous power it gives to inspectors appointed by the Electoral Commission to examine the books of political parties and to destroy a basic right of anonymity and of privacy, which I believe is a very important principle of democracy. That view also happens to be the view of 65 per cent of people surveyed in a poll conducted by the Age newspaper on this subject, which included a very large number of Labor Party and Australian Democrat supporters. So the opposition is to be found not only in the ranks of the Liberal Party and the National Country Party but also amongst many supporters of the other side of politics in Australia. The legislation that has been passed by the Wran Government was passed very quickly.

Mr COHEN-The Treasurer did not mention that once. All parties participating in an election campaign in Germany are entitled to public funding provided they meet a threshold. Approximately $1.75 is provided by the Bundestag for each person voting for the political parties. Mr Wran is talking about 22c for each person. In West Germany parties must disclose their sources of revenue. Donors of $10,000 or more must be named regardless of whether they are individuals or corporations. Austria has a fund that gives a basic grant to each party with five members in parliament and apportions the remainder of the fund on the basis of votes polled in the national elections. That system has operated since 1975. Is the system operating in Austria a corrupt system?
We know of the many scandals that have oc-- I curred in Italy over the years. One of the means of
keeping the Italian system honest-which is recognised by the Italian Parliament--is to pro-. vide political funding. Sweden probably leads the way in introducing legislation of this nature because it introduced the payment of subsidies to political parties in 1965. Norway soon followed the Swedish system, as did Denmark, Finland, Israel, Canada and the United States. All those countries are highly respected democracies. What an absurdity it is to suggest that all these countries that have had democratic systems for such a long period would introduce a system that is corrupt. They have introduced a system of public funding for the obvious reason that this gets rid of corruption. Once people are nominated and the amount of money that has been paid in is made known to the general public, that clearly ends the sort of corruption that has gone on before.

The Government has decided to introduce statute law revision bills into the Parliament on a regular basis, at least once in each year and, if required, once in each sitting. This will enable the prompt correction of mistakes and errors and removal from the statute book of expired laws. In the absence of the regular passage of Bills of this kind, the correction of an error in an Act can be achieved only by having a special Bill passed for that purpose or waiting until the Act needs to be amended for some other more important purpose. An objection sometimes made to omnibus amending Bills is that they result in so-called 'hidden' amendments. However, the Government Printer now issues monthly tables of Acts passed by the Parliament and these tables indicate clearly whenever an Act is amended by an omnibus Act such as a statute law revision Act.
