2894 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Statute Law Revision Bill

Acts Interpretation Act 1901
The Bill provides in Schedule I for the insertion in the Acts Interpretation Act of a provision that is relevant to the whole body of statute law. The provision is to the effect that, in the interpretation of a provision of an Act, a construction that would promote the purpose or object underlying the Act, whether that purpose or object is expressly stated in the Act or not, shall be preferred to a construction that would not promote that purpose or object. '

Under our constitutional arrangements it is the function of an independent judiciary to interpret the law and no proposals which we make can or should undermine the freedom that this function requires. Nevertheless the general approach to be adopted to statutory interpretation is something which this Parliament can---and at this stage should-address. The volume and complexity of legislation are assuming daunting proportions and the task of understanding and applying it grows in proportion. Increasingly the more significant decisions our courts are called upon to make turn on a point of statutory interpretation. Our problems are not unique. They are shared, for example, by the British Parliament, to which proposals have been made along the lines now proposed by this Bill. The British proposals, I should add, have been initiated and supported by very distinguished law Lords, including Lord Scarman and Lord Wilberforce.

The effect of the provision to be inserted in the

Acts Interpretation Act will be to confirm that in

interpreting provisions regard is to be had to the

object or purpose underlying the Act in question.

We are not among those who would say that the

general approach of our courts is at present overly

legalistic, but we do think that there is scope for

expressly stating that the statutes this Parliament

makes are to be interpreted in a purposive man-

ner. Tax decisions constitute a topical and import-

ant example that will come readily to all

members' minds, but the matter has wider impli-

cations that extend to many other statutes. I

therefore commend the provision to but before moving on I should invite

the the

HHouosues'e~

attention to some related questions. Explanatory

material is often provided by the Government to

assist senators and members, and subsequent users

of legislation. The material is of particular value

where legislation is complex or specialised in sub-

ject matter or both.

The Government thinks that we need to consider placing this practice on a more regular basis,
even to the extent of having an explanatory memorandum approved by the Parliament and able to

be taken into account by the courts in interpreting difficult provisions in the Act. The memorandum could not and would not be controlling. However, apart from contributing to the ease with which an Act can be understood by the public as well as by senators and members, such a practice offers the hope that the draftsman, when faced with the difficult choice of either using general language which might, however, leave the courts without sufficient guidance, or introducing lengthy detailed provisions, could feel some security in choosing the former course if he knew that a memorandum approved by the Parliament would embody its intent.
A cognate question arises where an Act is based on an expert report tabled in the Parliament, for example by the Law Reform Commission. Courts can at present consider such reports, but the precise way in which they can use them has been the subject of dispute. The fear has been expressed that the use of extraneous materials such as reports and explanatory memoranda will increase the cost and length oflegal proceedings. Certainly the possible disadvantages have to be considered and weighed against the benefits that would flow from the use of such materials. The time has come, however, when the matter should be fully explored, and the Attorney-General (Senator Durack) has announced that he proposes to do so with a view to developing specific proposals that can be brought into the Parliament for further consideration. In the meantime, the present position on the use of extraneous materials is preserved by stating in the proposed new provision that it is not to be construed as authorising the consideration of extraneous materials for any purpose for which they could not be considered if the new provision had not been enacted.
Administrative Appeals Tribunal Act 1975
Part II of the Bill repeals the provisions of the Schedule to the Administrative Appeals Tribunal Act 1975 that confer jurisdiction on the Administrative Appeals Tribunal in matters arising under various other Acts, and other parts of the Bill reenact the provisions concerned as part of those other Acts. In the course of transferring these provisions from one Act to another, Part IV omits the requirement that the Tribunal be constituted by a presidential member sitting alone when a decision is being reviewed under section 20 of the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969. That section empowers the Commissioner of Taxation to revoke a banker's authority to issue cheque forms that are prestamped with the duty payable under that Act if the banker is convicted of an offence against that

The amendments made by Parts III, X and XV and clause 116 give effect to recommendations made by the Millar Committee report on the Citizen Military Forces in 1974 regarding the title and structure of the Army Reserve.

Reserve. The Regular Army Reserve, which is a non-training element of the permanent military forces, is abolished and its members are trans-
ferred to the Inactive Reserve. In the context of reserve forces, 'active' denotes those elements the members of which have specific training obligations. The Bill also removes from the Defence Act reference to the unattached list and the Re-

The Millar Committee recommended that the Citizen Military Forces be retained as the country's immediate reserve of Army units and be renamed the Australian Army Reserve. The Committee also recommended that the members of the Regular Army Reserve, the Regular Army Emergency Reserve and the Reserve Citizen Military Forces be brought into this single Army Reserve organisation. On 4 April 1974, the report was tabled in the House of Representatives by the then Minister for Defence, who announced approval of these two recommendations. It was not appropriate to proceed with formal amendment to the titles and structures of the Reserve Forces until the Government had considered a further

serve of Officers. These relate to the internal administrative arrangements of the active and inactive reserves, respectively, which are matters of administrative detail appropriate to be dealt with by the regulations or by determinations under section 33 of the Defence Act. It has been decided not to incorporate the Regular Army Emergency Reserve into the Australian Army Reserve as recommended by the Millar Committee. The Emergency Reserve comprises former members of the Regular Army who agree to become members on cessation of their Regular Army service. Members may be called out by the Governor-General when he considers it desirable to do so.

Statute Law Revision Bill

Force Reserve becomes the Air Force General Reserve and an additional element, the Air Force Specialist Reserve, is introduced. The Air Force Specialist Reserve will comprise officers and airmen having specialist skills, such as doctors, dentists, lawyers and chaplains. Members of the General Reserve and the Specialist Reserve will not usually have peacetime training obligations. The Bill renames the Citizen Naval Forces as the Australian Naval Reserve. It does not deal with the elements into which that body is divided because this is dealt with in regulations under the Act.
The opportunity has been taken to revise the provisions of the Defence Act, the Air Force Act and the Naval Defence Act with regard to the rendering of service-other than continuous full time service-by members of the Reserve Forces. The revised provisions are more closely tailored to the needs of the reserves and provide more accurately feir the regulations relating to obligatory and voluntary periods of non-continuous service. Clause 116 amends references in various Acts to the former titles of the reserves forces and to 'continuous full time service'.
Federal Court of Australia Act 1976
Schedule 1 amends the Federal Court of Australia Act 1976 so as to permit an affidavit to be sworn before a district registrar and a deputy district registrar of the Court. It also amends that Act so as to enable a reserved judgment to be delivered at any subsequent sitting of the Court, ~hether or not the Court is sitting as a full court.
Historic Shipwrecks Act 1976
Part XIII amends sub-section 3 (5) of the Historic Shipwrecks Act 1976. The amendment relates to the description of the area adjacent to Queensland contained in the Petroleum (Submerged Lands) Act 1967 and is consequential on an alteration that is proposed to be made to that description by another Bill during the present sittings.
Migration Act 1958
The purpose of the amendments made to the Migration Act 1958 by clause 59 is to overcome a problem resulting from the amendments of section 16 of the Act that were made by the Migration Amendment Act 1979. Section 16 deems to be a prohibited immigrant a person who, for the purpose of securing entry into Australia, produces or produced to an officer an official document that was not issued to him or was forged or obtained by false representation. A doubt has arisen as to whether section 16 would apply to a person who produced a visa or return authority to an officer before 1 November 1979, which was the date on

which the amending Act came into force, in a case where the visa or return authority was not a separate document but was stamped in the person's passport. The amendment will make it clear that the section will apply in such a case. Clause 2 will make the amendment retrospective to 1 November 1979.
Navigation Act 1912
Schedule 1 amends the Navigation Act 1912 so as to enable judges of the Federal Court to sit on courts of marine inquiry without the need for special authorisation by the Governor-GeneraL
Pal'liamental-y Counsel Act 1970
Schedule 1 amends the Parliamentary Counsel Act 1970 to remove any doubts as to the validity of the long-standing practice of making dormant acting appointments to the offices of First and Second Parliamentary Counsel. These are appointments that are made in anticipation of any absences from duty of the holders of those offices and are designed to take effect automatically whenever an absence occurs during the currency of the relevant acting appointment.
Pig Industry Research Act 1971
Schedule 1 inserts the word 'Australian' at the commencement of the name of the Pig Industry Research Committee, which is constituted under the Pig Industry Research Act 1971. This will bring the name of the Committee into line with similar committees established under other Acts.
Public Service Act 1922
Clause 79 corrects an obsolete cross-reference in sub-section 82(6B) of the Public Service Act 1922. That sub-section provided that the services of a temporary employee to whom the Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Act 1979 applied could not be dispensed with except on the ground that the employee had been guilty of conduct which, if he were an officer, would constitute an offence referred to in subsection 55(1) of the Public Service Act. However, on 15 March 1981 the provisions of sub-section 55(1) were replaced by the new disciplinary provisions of Division 6 of Part III of the Act. Clause 79 therefore omits the reference to the repealed sub-section 55(1) and replaces it by a reference to specified grounds of dismissal, which are the same grounds as are set out in the new Division 6 of Part III of the Act. Clause 2 provides that clause 79 is to be deemed to have come into operation on 15 March 1981.

Car Parks Behind Parliament House

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2897

Social Sel'Vices Act 1947
Schedule 1 amends the Social Services Act 1947 to enable claims for age, invalid or widows pension, or sheltered employment allowance, to be lodged with any registrar. That Act presently requires such claims to be lodged with the registrar whose office is nearest the place of residence of the claimant.
Trade Union Training Authority Act 1975
In 1979 the Australian Council of Salaried and Professional Associations and the Australian Council of Trade Unions were amalgamated and the former body ceased to exist. Part XXIII provides for the abolition of the offices of the persons representing the former body on the Australian Council for Union Training, and on each of the State Councils for Union Training, established under the Trade Union Training Authority Act 1975, but it increases by one the number of persons representing the ACTU on each of those councils. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by MI' Dawkins) adjourned.
CONSTRUCTION OF CAR PARKS BEHIND PARLIAMENT HOUSE
Mr FIFE (Farrer-Minister for Education) (S.l )-On behalf of the Minister for Industrial Relations (Mr Viner) who is Minister Assisting the Prime Minister, I move:
That, in accordance with section 5 of the Parliament Act 1974, the House of Representatives approves the following proposal: Construction of car parks behind the provisional Parliament House.
The motion I have just moved is required in accordance with the provisions of the Parliament Act 1974 to carry out the following works:
1. Seal the presently gravelled temporary car park behind the provisional Parliament House into a bitumen sealed 160-space car park.
2. Provide an additional bitumen seal for a 12S-space car park to the east of the House with ingress-egress from Queen Victoria Terrace.
3. Provide security lighting in the form of 10-metre high steel columns with 2S0-watt mercury lamps in each car park. The lighting will be extended into the West Block car park to replace the existing post-top lanterns.
4. Disturbance to native flora will be minimised. However, it is expected that three trees and some shrubbery will be removed to enable the car park to function efficiently.

The estimated cost of the works is $109,000. I commend the motion to honourable members.
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (S.2)-1 wish to make one or two comments. The necessity for car parking is an unfortunate scourge which lives in our society. Car parking in the vicinity of Parliament House is quite obviously inadequate. It is unfortunate that the area immediately behind Parliament House has to be disturbed, but that decision, I think, was made some considerable time ago. The area is one of the few natural areas that have been preserved in any of Australia's major cities. That was the result of considerable foresight by earlier generations of Australians. I am not sure that any of us will be delighted with the results of turning a substantial proportion of that area into a concrete jungle, irrespective of the utility the necessity and the convenience for doing so.
I will briefly raise one other matter. That relates to the inadequacy of the current provisions of the Parliament Act. Because of interpretations which have arisen since the drafting of that Act certain alterations to areas within the Parliamentary Triangle can take place without, in fact, reference to this Parliament. I think the Act has been interpreted extraordinarily narrowly to exclude demolition and removal of items that are not structures. 1 hope that at some stage the Government will forgo the convenience of not having the constrictions of the Parliament Act applied and will amend the Act so that it protects totally the area included for coverage within the Schedule of that Act. There will then be no doubt that no alterations, such as the removal of the trees proposed a year ago and deemed not to have been covered by the Act, can take place in the future without the Parliament's being aware of it. I think the national Parliament has a right, a responsibility and an obligation to protect that area in which the Parliament operates and the ceremonial areas surrounding it.
Mr FIFE (Farrer-Minister for Education) (S.S)-in reply-I thank the honourable member for Corio (Mr Scholes) for his remarks. At the same time. I take the opportunity to assure him that the points he has made will be taken into consideration.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
CONCILIATION AND ARBITRATION AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 28 May, on motion by MrViner:
That the Bill be now read a second time.

2898 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Canciliation and Arbitration Amendment Bill

Mr CHARLES (Isaacs) (5.6)-ln the debate, which commenced last Thursday, we have already heard the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke) and the honourable member for Kooyong (Mr Peacock). As usual, the honourable member for Kooyong unfortunately was very long in rhetoric and short in substance in his speech. He did not really refer in great detail to the secret ballot provisions which are the major part of these amendments to the Conciliation and Arbitration Act. The new secret ballot provisions in the Bill can really be summed up in just one word- 'lunatic'. The secret ballot provisions introduced b~ the Minister for Industrial Relations (Mr Vmer) are or were a blatant election gimmick with which the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) hoped to capture some easy votes at last year's federal election. As the old saying goes, you can fool some of the people some of the time but not all the people all the time. Without any hesitation I say again that these secret ballots are and were nothing more than an election gimmick.
Of course the people of Australia want to know the answers to several very important questions: Is there no provision like this in the present Act? How will these new provisions work? Are they practical? If a person goes on strike after a secret ballot has been held how does that person come off strike and go back to work? None of these questions are addressed in any detail in this legislation. I suspect that the Government and, in fact, the Minister have not addressed themselves to these questions either. The answer to the first question is that there is provision now in the Act under section 45 which allows the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission to order a ballot of members to ascertain the attitudes of those members in an industry or work place if the Commission sees fit and considers that it will help in the situation or in the negotiations and settlement of the dispute or disputes. For the benefit of every honourable member and for people listening at home, I will read out part of section 45 of the Act so that we all know that in fact such provisions do exist although this Government keeps burying them slightly. The section states:
(1) Where-
(a) an organisation is a party to or concerned in an industrial dispute with which the Commission or some other tribunal acting in pursuance of a law of the Commonwealth is empowered to deal (whether or not proceedings in relation to the dispute are before the Commission or such a tribunal); and
(b) the Commission thinks that the prevention or settlement of the dispute would or might be encouraged or assisted b) ascertaining the views or attitude of the members, or of a section or class of the members, of the organisation or of a branch of the organisation in relation to a matter the Commission may order that a

vote of those members, or of the members included in that section or class, for the purpose of ascertaining their views or attitude in relation to that matter, be taken by secret ballot.
The honourable member for Wills said on Thursday that the Commission has used that section, although only on a handful of occasions. The section does exist. The power is vested in the Act now for the Commission to use. To quote from the words in the Minister's second reading speech, 'to build on this' we have these wonderful new provisions for secret ballots where only 5 per cent of people in a work place may make application for a secret ballot of all members; that is five people out of 100 or 5 per cent. The practicalities are that while negotiations are taking place, where in a work place 95 out of 100 members are in favour of industrial action of some kind because they feel they have a just cause, five people can make application to the Commission for a secret ballot and completely stifle the negotiations and an early settlement. Let us look at the practical implications that go with this. Negotiations are taking place between an employer and the employees. The rumour is that there could be industrial action of some kind. Five out of 100 members, that is the 5 per cent needed, decide to apply for secret ballot. Now what happens? The Minister's second reading speech states:
The Commission, constituted by a presidential member, will be required to order the conduct of a ballot . . .
So, the presidential member looks into the case and decides whether to conduct a ballot. We will get back to the question of what happens if the presidential member, a Deputy President of the Commission, decides against a ballot. We will assume for a second that he decides for a ballot. Then what happens? We have to draw up the ballot papers, get them all ready, go through the rolls and decide who is eligible to vote and who is not and when and how the ballot will take place. At this stage-it could be several days later-what has happened on the industrial front? Heaven only knows! Negotiations have more than likely become stifled and, in fact, become drawn out by the kerfuffie about the pending secret ballot. Let us get to the crunch. We eventually get around to having the ballot. What happens if the ballot decides that we are all going on strike? The possibility is that it has totally ruined any negotiations that have taken place. The Commission has had to intervene. We are back where we started. How do we get off strike and back to work if the negotiations have reached that stage? I assume that we have another secret ballot. After all, we had a secret ballot to go on strike. I suppose that we now have to have another secret ballot to come off

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2899

strike and to go back to work. All in all this process has probably taken nearly a week and done mote harm than good. The employees will not like it in general. The employers will not like it. It will hinder their operations and the possibilities of good and peaceful settlements much more than it willhelp them. The Commission will find it an absolute nightmare.
One very interesting thing about the Minister's second reading speech is that the Minister does not mention anything on the situation that I have just mentioned. His silence on the matter would suggest-this is how I read his speech-that in the majority of cases employees will vote against industrial action. I quote from the report of the Royal Commission on Trade Unions and Employers Associations 1965-68, from which the honourable member for Wills also quoted. There is some more very interesting information in this report. The Chairman of the Royal Commission was the Right Honourable Lord Donovan. The report was presented to the British Parliament in 1968. The references to 'official' and 'unofficial' in the report relate to the British system and not to the Australian system. In paragraph 426 this famous Donovan Royal Commission stated on the issue of compulsory strike ballots:
A number of witnesses have suggested to us that a secret ballot should be required before a strike can lawfully take place. This proposal is based on the belief that workers are likely to be less militant than their leaders and that, given the opportunity of such a ballot, they would often be likely to vote against strike action.

It seems that our present Prime Minister must have given evidence. In paragraph 427 the report stated:
It is clear that the scope of any legislation to this end, if it were to be effective, would have to be confined to major official strikes. A law forbidding strike action before the holding of a secret ballot could not be enforced in the case of smallscale unofficial stoppages, which make up the overwhelming majority of the total number of strikes.
The honourable member for Wills quoted the following words of this famous Donovan Royal Commission:
There is little justification in the available evidence for the view that workers are less likely to vote for strike action than their leaders; and findings from our workshop relations survey, already cited, confirm this. Experience in the USA has been that strike ballots are overwhelmingly likely to go in favour of strike action. This is also the experience of Canada, where strike ballots, are compulsory in the provinces of Alberta and British Columbia.
Paragraph 429 stated:
There are other objections to such ballots. Once a vote has been taken and has gone in favour of strike action, the resulting stoppage may delay a settlement by restricting union leaders' freedom of action.
This is exactly what I have just said. In Australia from 1975 to 1978 half the number of the strikes recorded by the Australian Bureau of Statistics were of one day's duration or less. I ask leave of the House to incorporate in Hansard tables to that effect.
Leave granted.

Mr CHARLES-I thank the House. It would pay the Government and the Minister for Industrial Relations (Mr Viner) to do some long and hard homework on industrial relations. These secret ballot provisions are absolute lunacy. However, I must say that they are in accord with the Government's record. I ask the Minister to look at clause 6 and proposed new sub-section (3D) of the Bill, which states:
Subject to this section, the Commission shall, upon an application made to it under sub-section (3A), order that a vote of the members to whom the application relates-
Later the proposed new sub-section uses the words 'to which the application relates'. I sincerely ask the Minister: What does that mean? Does that mean that people in one work place can make an application for their work mates in the same work place, or does that mean also that people in one work place can make application for people in another work place on the other side of the city? It is not very clear at all. Of course such questions have to be cleared up by regulations. I would prefer this matter to be cleared up in the Parliament now. If it has to be cleared up with regulations, of course, the regulations have to lie before both the Houses of Parliament. I suggest to this Government that there is quite a possibility that after 1 July those reg'llations will not be allowed.
This Government has a reputation for confrontation and divisiveness. It has a never-ending resolve to set one Australian against another. History will see this Government in that light~--make no mistake about that. These secret ballot provisions will continue this Government's policies of hampering and hindering good industrial relations and once again set Australian against Australian. Talking about that, may I get back to the situation that would obtain if a Deputy President of the Commission refused to grant a secret ballot. The Minister's second reading speech states:
. . . a refusal to order a place of work ballot must be reviewed automatically by the President of the Commission.
In other words, the Deputy Presidents will be watched over by the President, at present Sir John

Moore. What a position to place the President in. The Deputy Presidents of the Commission are all honourable and learned people of high integrity; yet in this case they are unable to make a decision without that decision and them being reviewed by the President. What an outrageous situation. To get one person to look over the shoulder of another is just not the way to help solve and further better industrial relations in any way, shape or form.
Like the honourable member for Wills, I was also outraged that the amendments relating to union amalgamations are not being introduced with the secret ballot amendments, as the Prime Minister and former Minister for Industrial Relations Street promised they would be. But we are used to broken promises from this Government and this Prime Minister. After the election the Minister for Industrial Relations announced that the Government had decided to introduce the legislation during the autumn session this year in order 'to meet the Government's commitment to introduce this legislation as indicated by the Prime Minister in his policy speech'. Despite all the assurances and the Government's alleged commitment to consultation, the legislation relating to union amalgamations has been shoved under the carpet. I do not suppose that this is any news to us, though, because had it been introduced it would have been the first piece of industrial legislation that the Fraser Government introduced with the support of both the employers and the unions. The proposed changes to the law relating to amalgamations were first raised by the Australian Council of Trade Unions at the National Labour Consultative Council meeting in April 1978. The changes were the subject of almost three years consideration by a specialist committee of the NLCC which comprised senior representatives of the Depart-- ment of Industrial Relations, national employers and the union movement. A unanimous proposal for changes to the amalgamation law was endorsed by the NLCC and forwarded to the

Minister-if I may borrow a phrase from the ond is: How are those purposes to be achieved?

honourable member for Kooyong-backed up by The third is: Are those purposes consistent with

Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip tution which gives to this Parliament the power to

Lynch) and the Minister for Communications make laws with respect to the prevention and

2902 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Bill

settlement of disputes of an industrial nature extending beyond anyone State by means of conciliation and arbitration? I now address myselfto the two main purposes of the Bill.
Firstly, the Bill seeks to give to members of an organisation, or of a branch, working for a particular employer and at a particular place of work the right to have their views taken into account by means of a secret ballot when directed or requested by that organisation or branch to engage in industrial action. Secondly, in cases where the Australian Conciliation and Arbitration Commission forms the view that the majority of the members of the organisation or branch who have recorded a valid vote in a ballot ordered to be held under the proposed legislation were not in favour of engaging in the industrial action with which that ballot was concerned, the members are to be freed from compliance with a direction or request made by the organisation to engage in or support that industrial action; that is, the industrial action which is the subject of that ballot.
How are these purposes to be achieved? They are to be achieved by carefully regulated steps which must be understood if the Bill is to be dispassionately examined and not simply emotionally expounded. The steps are these: In the first place, there must be a direction or a request made on behalf of the organisation or branch or made under the rules of the organisation or branch. This Bill is not concerned with unofficial strikes, so we can put that matter to one side. The direction or request to engage in industrial action must be made to members of an organisation or branch employed by a particular employer at a particular place of work. We are not concerned with people across the way, as the honourable member for Isaacs (Mr Charles) seems to think we are. We are concerned with particular employees at a particular place of work. When the direction or request is given, and if the requisite minimum number of members employed by the same employer at the same place wish to do so, they may apply for a secret ballot. For what purpose? For the purpose of ascertaining whether they support the industrial action in question. That is the matter which seems to be so objectionable to honourable members on the other side of the House. What happens then? The next step is that the application is heard by the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission constituted by a presidential member.
When the application is heard the Commission shall order that a ballot be held and give the directions unless it comes to one of four views. These are the matters that were entirely ignored by the

honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke) and the honourable member for Isaacs. Firstly, if the Commission forms the view that cessation or prevention of the industrial action or the settlement of the matters giving rise to the industrial action to which the application for the ballot relates would not be encouraged or assisted by ascertaining the views of the members to whom the application relates-that is, those members at a particular place of work employed by a particular employee-the application is to be refused. In the second case, if the view is come to by the Commission that the industrial action to which the application relates has ceased, then again the application is to be refused. Thirdly, if the cessation of the industrial action is imminent and the Commission forms that view, then once again the application is to be refused. Fourthly, if the Commission forms the view that the industrial action to which the application relates is not likely to take place, again it is to be refused. Of course, this matter has to go ultimately to the President, but that d,oes not matter. That is simply a question of procedure. So there are four bases on which the application can be refused which would exclude the kind of vexatious litigant that the honourable member for Wills sought to draw out of his hat.
Next, if after a secret ballot has been ordered it appears that the dispute has been settled or the industrial action has ceased, or that settlement or cessation is imminent, or that the industrial action concerned is not likely to take place, then after the ordering of a secret ballot but before the vote, when one of those matters appears the Commission shall revoke the order for the secret ballot. That is the way in which the procedures for the hearing of an application and for orders made work. Now, what happens if a ballot is heard and not revoked? If that be the case, notice of the result shall be given to the organisation to which the members belong, the persons eligible to vote, and the employer. That seems perfectly sensible and perfectly straight forward. Next, if the Commission forms the view that the majority of those who recorded valid votes were not in favour of engaging in the industrial action with which the ballot was concerned a statement to that effect shall be given to the organisation, the employer, ,and those eligible to vote. Again, that is perfectly straightforward and perfectly sensible.
What then are the consequences when a ballot has been taken? The consequences for the ongoing process of work and conciliation and arbitration are, first, that the Commission shall have regard to the result. This simply makes clear the

ments were utterly irrelevant. First, I will deal with his coverage. He cited what Sir John Moore had to say, and he quoted this passage from the Age:
Sir John also warned that compulsory ballots for unions contemplating strikes would not be as effective as many people believed in reducing strikes.

What the honourable member said was incomplete and, secondly, it was irrelevant. It was irrelevant because the question to which the authors to whom he referred were addressing their attention was that of compulsory secret ballots. That is not the question before the House. The question before the House is that of ballots at the request of

2904 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Bill

members; ballots which are secret but not compulsory.
Let us recall certain fundamental facts about the affairs of registered organisations. Many of their officials are elected only every four years. Of course, that means that those organisations may get out of touch with the affairs of their members. Such elections are permitted under the Conciliation and Arbitration Act, but it is perfectly plain that the organisations can get out of touch, as the honourable member for Wills well knows. One of the reasons is, that it is perfectly plain that they can get out of touch because of the very high turnover of members in certain areas; for example, employees in shops. The size of organisations alone can put officials out of touch. One looks to the size of some of the unions such as the Amalgamated Metal Workers and Shipwrights Union of 160,000 members; the Shop, Distributive and Allied Employees Association of 145,000 members; and the Australian Workers Union of 130,000 members. How is it that the leaders of these unions will at all times be conscious of what is happening in all places and in the hearts and minds of all their members? Of course they will not! Of course they can get out of touch! Indeed, in many cases union members will have never had the opportunity to participate in the ballot for the very simple reason-I speak now of a ballot for the election of union officials-that they have joined a union between four-year ballots. Of course, in other cases members may feel that undue pressures have been brought to bear on them and they wish to have an opportunity to express a view that is free from pressure. Tn some cases strikes are brought about for purely political purposes-strikes that have nothing to do with the working interests of union members. A case that the honourable member for Wills would well understand is the possibility of directions being given in respect of uranium mining because that is contrary to the policy of the Australian Council of Trade Unions. It might be exceedingly relevant for the Commission to know the views of those members of unions who earn their living from mining. As I have said, that is quite contrary to the views of the ACTU.
I conclude by making the following comment: We have had secret ballot provisions in this country for many years. They are to be found in the Conciliation and Arbitration Act and in the New South Wales industrial and arbitration Act. I believe there is a provision in the Western Australian Act which deals with industrial matters. These provisions have given to a court or to the Commission power to order a secret ballot of their own volition. The provisions before the House are

essentially different. Those provisions open the way to members to initiate the procedure of a secret ballot themselves. I do not suggest for one moment that these provisions represent a panacea. They may be seldom used, but they give individuals a greater opportunity to have a say in their own affairs, something which honourable members on the other side of the House seem to resent. That accords with the intent of the provision in the Constitution which gives to this House power to pass laws in respect of conciliation and arbitration for the prevention and settlement of industrial disputes. How better, when the right time arises, to get a view of what the members really want than to conduct a ballot which is not ordered by a court or by any government but which is requested by the members?
It is right that trade unions should be an essential part of the fabric of our life, but it is right also that since their very reason for existence is to represent the individual interests of their members those members should have the right to vote on questions which directly and immediately concern
their very means oflivelihood.
Mr HOLDING (Melbourne Ports) (5.45)- The attitude expressed by the honourable member for North Sydney (Mr Spender) and his lack of knowledge as to how the trade union movement operates and what occurs in strike situations really indicate all that is wrong with this legislation. The Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Bill is ill-conceived and unnecessary. Rather than make any contribution to the settlement of industrial disputes it will compound the problems faced by the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission in its daily task of trying to resolve issues of industrial confrontation. The principal spokesman for the Opposition, the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke), has already indicated to the House that the Government has failed to introduce legislation to facilitate the amalgamation of unions, despite three years of work by both employer and employee organisations and the endorsement of the National Labour Consultative Council, despite the fact that the Bill is ready and available and despite the agreement of the employer groups and the trade union movement that such legislation is long overdue and vitally necessary to the proper develop-
ment of our industrial relations.
Instead of having a Bill which is designed to facilitate and make industrial relations in this community easier, as requested by employer and trade union organisations, where the work has been done and where the legislation is already drafted, we have this legislation for secret ballots in respect of industrial action in particular places of

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2905

work. Surely the Parliament is entitled to ask: 'What is the origin of this legislation?' There certainly has been no demand for it from the Commission. It has not been seriously argued for by any significant employer group in this nation and certainly no trade union group. When the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) or the Minister for Industrial Relations (Mr Viner) referred this concept to the National Labour Consultative Council the Government was told by that body, which represents trade union and employer interests in this nation, that this concept was not necessary. I would have thought in that situation even the most ardent supporter of the Government might have said: 'If the trade union movement'-whom the Government suspects-'and the employers'- whom the Government loves-'are agreed that legislation of this kind is not necessary, perhaps we ought to heed their advice, or perhaps we ought to seek the advice of those members of the Commission who have to face these problems day after day'. Indeed the Parliament has had quoted to it both the views of academic writers operating full time in the industrial relations field and the views of so eminent a person in that area as Sir John Moore, the President of the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission expressing their doubts as to the effectiveness of legislation of this type. Instead of having before us-as the Parliament is entitled to expect-legislation dealing with union amalgamation we now have this hotchpotch legislation which will not solve industrial problems but, indeed, will compound them.
Where does the Parliament look to find the arguments, the reasons, and the justification for this ill-conceived proposal? The only answer that we have been given by the honourable member for North Sydney was that this was the Prime Minister's promise; that is to say, the whole concept of this legislation is to be found in the Prime Minister's, somewhat narrow view of industrial relations. It is a view in which the Prime Minister's introversion is equalled only by his naivete and his 'macho' need for confrontation. He believes, as do many of his supporters, that every strike is the result of manipulation by radical union bosses-he does not even say communist union bosses-and that the victims of this manipulation, the ordinary, decent Australian male and female workers, will be saved from the results of this continual and ongoing manipUlation only by the intervention of this warm-hearted Prime Minister, and this legislation. He believes, as does the honourable member for North Sydney, a man much experienced in industrial relations-his experience shows when he addresses the Parliament--

Mr McVeigh-He is a good parliamentarian, too.
Mr HOLDING-He is probably modelling himself on the Minister for Housing and Construction, so that instead of one political dunce we will have two political dunces.
Mr McVeighSee what the electors say.
Mr HOLDING-I know what the electors would say about this legislation. We should consider the Government's view of men and women who go on strike. Has the Minister for Housing and Construction ever heard either the Prime Minister or the Minister for Industrial Relations say in this Parliament that there might be some justice in a strike, there might be a view that ought to be considered by the Government as to the rightness or the cause of those unionists resorting to industrial action? I have never heard that, and neither has any member of this Parliament. We are not likely to hear it. We are now asked to adopt this legislation because it is the Prime Minister's view that if we give all the decent, ordinary union members, men and women, who he behoves are being manipulated all the time, the right to this odd and secret ballot then the anonymity of that ballot will give them the courage to say 'no' to industrial action. That, at heart, is the underlying philosophy which is the reason for this legislation.
Apart from the social class condescension that underlines this view, this Parliament is being asked to overlook the fact that on almost every occasion a majority decision of workers in the work place is required before any industrial action is taken. Many industrial actions and stoppages take place without the immediate knowledge of the trade union executives or their full time secretaries and officers. They hear about the problems in the workshop, after the stoppage has taken place, when someone has to sort the matter out. It also overlooks that other essential factor in any strike action--that every worker involved in strike action votes continuously on a daily or weekly basis in respect of that strike, because he votes with his hip pocket. There is no industrial action, no stoppage of work, which does not ultimately involve some loss of wages and some financial hardship for those who take that decision. That being the case, we have to go to examples of this type of legislation being considered in other communities. The honourable member for Wills has already pointed out how the Donovan commission-that was a high-powered commission looking at the whole question of industrial relations-rejected this concept. This concept of strike ballots before legislation has

2906 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Bill

been tried in those communities where the industrial relations system, unlike ours, is based upon the concept of collective bargaining. They do not have the processes of conciliation and arbitration and the intervention of judicial and semi-judicial procedures to try to resolve the industrial issues between the parties.
It is interesting to note that in both the United States and Canada during the war secret ballot strike legislation was introduced. The premises were exactly the same. The governments were in a wartime situation and wanted to minimise industrial strikes. Those governments, quite frankly, took the view that if there were strike ballots then industrial stoppages would be reduced. I wish to quote from an authoritative article which analyses the success of those proposals. It is an article by Mr F. R. Anton entitled 'Work Stoppages: Should Authorisation be Mandatory?' Having examined the origin of that legislation and how it worked, he concluded:
It is reasonable to assume that both the Canadian and United States governments enacted strike vote legislation in wartime to limit a union's right to strike, in the hope of minimizing the number of strikes and thus enabling war production to be maximized. Official statements made at that time support this assumption. But no available evidence indicates that such legislation reduced the number of work stoppages. For what it is worth, many of those who administered the strike vote in the United States held the view that such legislation tended to foster, rather than discourage, strikes. There were indications in Canada too that in no important dispute did workers vote against strike action. Moreover, the impression was gained that whenever strike votes were conducted on federal authority, the public tended to regard such supervised voting as carrying government sanction for a strike.
Here is an objective analysis of this type of legislation in a collective bargaining situation, in both the United States and Canada. What is the conclusion? It is this:
It is therefore significant that in spite of substantial pressures, neither of the central governments saw fit to extend the stroke vote requirement into postwar legislation.
I commend that article to honourable members opposite who obviously do not understand the complexities of our industrial and conciliation system, who do not understand the way in which stroke situations and industrial problems arise or the way in which they are resolved. What we put to this House-I do not think the Government will listen but we are bound to put it-is that this legislation will not make the solution of industrial stoppages any easier. It will compound the problem for the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission. It involves some very naive concepts, including some very naive legal concepts. I do not know what a commissioner is supposed to do. He is required to recognise the result of a ballot. What occurs if a ballot is conducted in a work

place of 1,000 people and 200 people vote against the strike, 400 people vote for it and the others do not vote at all? Is a non-vote to be regarded as a vote in favour or a vote against? What position is a commissioner placed in when he is involved in trying to settle a dispute and there is an overwhelming vote in favour of a strike and its
continuance?
Mr Hawke-It shows the depth of feeling.
Mr HOLDING-It shows the depth of feeling. Will the commissioner say that, having regard to this depth of feeling, he will do nothing to resolve the situation? Supposing people in a work place overstate their case and demand $50 when they really want only $10 or $20. Does the commissioner say that he cannot have regard to the view that $10 or $20 is a proper figure on which to conciliate because the depth of feeling among employees has been shown to be so overwhelming that he must say to them that the only proper thing he can do to resolve the matter is to give them all they require? That is the kind of legal nonsense being perpetrated in this Parliament and heaped on to the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission. In the name of whom? Is it in the name of the employers? No. In the name of the trade union movement? No. Their responsible bodies have said that they do not require this legislation. This legislation is being heaped on to the Commission, creating incredible problems, not merely for those who are involved in the conciliation and arbitration process but also for employers and responsible trade union leaders, to suit the industrial fantasies of this Prime Minister and to satisfy the industrial viciousness of the present Minister for Industrial Relations. That is the reason for this legislation and that is the reason it ought to be rejected by this Parliament.
Sitting suspended from 6 to 8 p.m.
Mr PORTER (Barker) (8.0)-The main provision of this amendment to the Conciliation and Arbitration Act is to enable members of a union at a particular place of work to have a secret ballot in connection with a request that may be made of those members to take part in industrial action. In other words, if the union demands that its members at a factory go on strike the members of the union at that factory can request and take part in a secret ballot to decide whether in fact they want to go on strike.
Before dealing with the details of the legislation I would like to deal in the broad for a moment with the reasons the Government has been prompted to introduce this Bill. We have said many times, and it is worth repeating, that in the industrial relations field it is clear that each

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2907

member of the community has both rights and responsibilities. Further, the rights of the individual ought to be protected whilst at the same time the individual must meet his or her obligations. This legislation is designed to protect the rights of the individual in the work place. It may also be seen in the wider context to be protecting the community against industrial disruption undertaken by members of a union acting under direction rather than in accordance with their wishes.
There is no doubt that one of the most powerful weapons of the unions, and indeed an important right of the individual worker, is the right to strike. My concern, and I believe the concern of the community, is that the right to strike is no longer being used by many unions as a powerful weapon of last resort; rather, in the fragile field of industrial relations, it is being used as an initiating manoeuvre in order to soften up or lower the resistance of the employers to the unions' demands. At the same time, little account appears to be taken of the effects of such action upon those who are not directly involved in the dispute. Further, the right to strike has now been adopted by some unions as a political weapon to be used in political disputes outside the industrial relations area. I quote just one example. On 20 May this year an article in the Australian newspaper stated:
The ACTU last night threatened 'direct industrial action' against companies which moved to buy government enterprises put up for sale to the private sector. The ACTU President, Mr Dolan, is quoted as warning that action, which would involve strikes and possibly union bans on companies' products, could be taken in the areas of operation of any company which considers buying government businesses. This move by the unions is a direct attack on the implementation of decisions announced by this Federal Government in the report on the Review of Commonwealth Functions or, as it is known, the decisions of the razor gang. Those decisions were made by the properly and democratically elected government which was elected only late last year. The Australian Council of Trade Unions is apparently saying that the Australian people can elect whatever government they like but if the ACTU does not like the decisions of that democratically elected government then it, as a minority group in Australia, will use industrial muscle to prevent the implementation of that Government's decisions.
The same newspaper article reports that Mr Dolan said that union action taken against this Government's decisions would involve top level consultation between the Federal Labor Party and ACTU officials. So the Labor Opposition, not satisfied with the results of the election, is trying to stuff its socialist dogma down the throats of the unwilling public through the ACTU.
Not content with the use of its industrial muscle for political purposes, as the Sydney Morning Herald reported the next day:
The ACTU threw down the gauntlet yesterday to the Arbitration Commission and employers by deciding to press on

with its campaign for shorter working hours through productivity bargaining.It--
That is the ACTU-
reiterated its support for centralised wage fixing but rejected the Commission's new guidelines.
The article went on:
After yesterday's executive session, the ACTU President, Mr Dolan forecast that the next few months would see the stormiest period in industrial relations since 1947-49, when the Chifley Government sent soldiers in to work strike-hit coal mines.
The article then stated that Mr Dolan nominated Telecom Australia, Australia Post and the road transport industry as likely trouble spots. So the ACTU has threatened to use the strike weapon on one day against the Government and on the next day against the decisions of the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission. At the same time the unions have refused to take part in the proposed inquiry into wage determination and industrial relations. Instead of adopting a positive role they are embarking on a course of massive industrial unrest and disruption. It is my view that this is not a course of action which is accepted by the general public as being either desirable or correct. Within the Australian context I believe it is an abuse of the strike weapon.
This legislation will provide the opportunity for union members to express their wishes in relation to such demands made on them by their union executive. I believe that the people of Australia, employees, consumers, employers and small businessmen-are fed up with the number of strikes in this country. Employees, especially those working in industry, are sick to death of the constant threat and uncertainty which industrial disruption poses to their weekly pay packet. Employees, especially those with young families and a high level of commitments, are naturally and justifiably concerned about their. ability to meet their commitments in the event of industrial disruption. Their concern relates not just to being involved directly in an industrial dispute but also to being stood down as a result of being employed in a factory or business which is incapable of maintaining their employment because of an industrial dispute in a related area. Employers are loath to stand down workers. However, if there is no work for those employees they cannot keep them on for very long.
Consumers are another group of people who are inevitably affected by industrial disputes. It seems that many unions in their cavalier attitude towards the use of the strike weapon have no thought for their fellow employees or the consumers who may be affected by their decisions. Consumers, of course, have to pay more for goods

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2909

in our view, further demonstrates the attempt by this Government to attack the trade unions and the living standards of workers. The general weaknesses in this Bill have already been aptly demonstrated by other Labor speakers. I shall concentrate my remarks on the values and attitudes on which this Bill is based. Union bashing by this Government and, to some degree, by the media has often taken the form of hysterical and irrational outbursts of the kind we have just heard. Recently, the Fraser Government has engaged in something more ominous-an attempt to undermine, subvert and destroy the union movement and thereby to water down the basic rights of workers to organise, unite and withdraw their labour.
Undoubtedly this Government seeks to take advantage of an atmosphere of antagonism which it has created through its continuous attempts to blame the economic problems of this nation on the trade union movement. Because of the cacophony of abuse being heaped on that movement and its leaders there is danger that the true meaning and real worth of the trade union movement will be forgotten. There is a danger, therefore, that the peopie of Australia will be hoodwinked into supporting attacks on those very organisations, the trade unions, which have been primarily responsible, throughout the history of the present economic system, for improvements in their standards of living and conditions of employment. That point needs to be made strongly to those Australian workers, whether in offices, shops or factories, who have become or are becoming apathetic about it. Without the solidarity of the union movement and the many struggles by workers of previous generations, wages would be much lower than they are at present; the majority of people would be living in dire poverty; hours of employment would be long; and conditions of employment would be atrocious.
Even a cursory look at the historical development of Western economies and the role of unions within them clearly proves this claim. In the nineteenth century, capitalist economies were riddled with crises of over-production of goods and under consumption by workers who could not afford them. In order to survive in that cut--throatworld, business leaders attempted to reduce wages. Where this succeeded it led to increasing poverty and misery for workers. They barely had enough to provide for food and shelter. The only way available to combat this tendency of the capitalist system was the organisation of workers into unions. 'Workers Unite' thus became the rallying call of the working class in its attempts to counter these oppressive conditions. Their major weapon

in that struggle was the strike, which is nothing more than the communal withdrawal by all the members of the union of their labour. The early unionists sought the protection of workers against the attempt to reduce their wages and to make conditions of employment even worse than they were. The corollary to this was to try to improve the wages and conditions of their members.
Historians have recorded the huge struggles that occurred in Australia in the 1880s and 1890s in which employers, supported by the State, by the Government, attempted to crush the development of unionism. In fact, in many cases the Government succeeded in beating workers into submission. These lessons of history have been forgotten by some of those who enjoy the benefits, in terms of wages and conditions, of those struggles and many others that came later.
Mr Barry Jones-They never knew them.
Dr THEOPHANOUS-My colleague said 'they never knew them', but they certainly are finding out more about them at present because they are having to struggle once again, because of the actions of this Government, to retain the kind of standards of living which they achieved before. It was at this time and because of this point that the unions also recognised that they would have to fight through the political and democratic process. I am pleased that this last point regarding the trade union origins of the Australian Labor Party is to be specifically included in the new platform of the party, if the Federal Executive's recommendation is accepted. This point reads:
The ALP has its origins in the recognition by the trade union movement of the necessity for a political voice to take forward the struggle of the working class against the excesses, injustices and inequalities of capitalism.
The present Government in its attitude to unions seeks to return to the nineteenth century situation when workers' organisations were weak and could be crushed by a joint alliance between big business and the government. The Government seeks to use force against the unions so that they cannot achieve any improvements in the wages and conditions of workers. With this secret ballot legislation the Government seeks to castrate the trade union movement. As I shall point out later, it will not succeed. Indeed, there is no doubt that this Government seeks to reverse the gains made by workers in recent years and to reduce the standard of living of workers.
In the last three years we have had constant statements by the Treasurer (Mr Howard) that it is essential to reduce real wages. This claim was echoed by representatives of big business in whose interests this Government continues to act. A paper by Greg Turnidge and Ainsley Jolley

2910 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Bill

published last October by the Victorian Chamber of Manufactures they claimed:
. . . real wage reductions are an essential ingredient of the total economic policy package necessary to return the Australian economy to a completely healthy state.
Attacks of this kind on living standards of workers are the real reason behind this Government's legislation on secret ballots. It believes it can achieve a reduction in the number of strikes and other actions taken by workers in support of their claims through this legislation. Of course, this is based on a myth. The myth that underlines that claim is that workers somehow love to go on strike and that union leaders love to call out their members at the drop of a hat.
Mr Tuckey-You are the second one tonight.
Dr THEOPHANOUS-That kind of remark just goes to show the honourable member's intelligence. This proposition ignores the fact that workers who strike also suffer. They lose wages. The decision to go on strike is a serious matter which is not taken lightly. It is taken out of a sense of desperation that unless workers act to protect their livelihoods they will be victims of the same greedy impulses which reduced workers to absolute poverty in the last century. We still see those greedy impulses alive and kicking in Australia at present. From 1975 to early 1980 record levels of profit were achieved by many large, often foreignowned, corporations. Yet during this period real wages relative to inflation fell and so did the standards of living of workers. It is only in the last year because of determined actions by key unions and negotiations over increased productivity awards-because productivity increased dramatically-that some increase in real wages was possible.
As the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) pointed out, it is only this fact-the actions of the unions-which has allowed for growth in consumer spending in the last few months and growth in the economy as a whole. Thus we have the extraordinary situation where the Treasurer is claiming credit for achieving increased economic growth whereas in fact it is only the actions and the achievements of the union movement in the last year in increasing wages which have been the main catalyst of that growth. Is this Government and its Minister grateful for that assistance, for that achievement? Not on your life. They cannot stand the fact that workers have achieved some distribution of the wealth which is generated from workers' labour. This is the crux of the matter with regard to the conservative attitude to workers and unions. The Government is congenitally incapable of understanding a fundamental fact of economics-that it

is human labour, the mental and physical work of ordinary Australians, which creates wealth and which recreates the world and everything in it. The god of profit which they worship would be non-existent without the contribution of this human labour.
The union movement which is concerned to ensure that labour is adequately rewarded and exploitation is minimised is the movement which the Government seeks to attack. The legislation seeks to destroy the solidarity and unity of purpose of the workers and to prevent the achievement of those goals which I have just mentioned. It seeks to place special powers in the hands of that tiny minority of unionists who, because they have been indoctrinated by the media and others, seek to disrupt the process of negotiation and action to achieve justice. Significantly, none of these individuals ever, by the way, rejects a wage increase resulting from the action and strike activity of their fellows. These attempts further to control the trade union movement will fail not only for the specific reasons given by the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke) but also because of the overriding fact that no legislation, court action or penal provisions or arbitrary use of government powers will prevent the majority of workers from uniting to protect and improve their standards of living and the rewards of labour. It is about time the Liberal Party and other conservative parties recognised that fundamental fact. They cannot continue to attack the living standards of people and expect them to sit down and accept this. If the Government wants workers to behave in accordance with laws then the laws need to be reasonable. The Government does not have reasonable laws in industrial relations at present. This is another stinking law which is going to make industrial relations worse. The sooner the Government comes to accept this fundamental fact the quicker harmonious industrial relations will be created in this country.

For the future, the Government proposes to:

It is important that we reflect on these promises because the Government has reneged on its promise on the amalgamation of unions. No matter how this pathetic Minister for Industrial Relations may try to put a veneer of respectability on this legislation further to facilitate secret ballots in respect of industrial action, it can and will be seen for what it really is. It is a vehicle for pimps, scabs, malcontents and dobbers. It is perhaps typical that this Minister should bring it in. These scabs and pimps that the Government would know much of are there to cause disruption within the democratic trade union organisation. If anyone has doubts about this then he will have to look at the razor gang report and see just how many departments were slashed and note that the IRB, the Industrial Relations Bureau-or the industrial police force as it is is known in the trade union movement-this industrial gestapo, has been retained. It is rather ironic that in the report referring to the cutbacks there was a line to say that this Bureau would not be cut back.
I think it is worthwhile to reflect on the history of industrial relations in this country in particular.

Mr SCOTT-Some clown here says that he is a socialist. In general, I do not think the Australian system can claim much success in preventing industrial disputes. I doubt, however, whether any system would have done much better or that we would have been willing to pay the price of total dispute prevention. This is, largely a matter of the make-up of the social community, of its attitudes and collective personality. Australia has a record of having more industrial stoppages than most other countries. However, despite this record it is not one of the top countries in terms of man days lost. The latest figures available for top international comparison of eighteen countries take us up to the end of 1978. They show that over the previous ten years, which include some pretty torrid years for Australia, the United States, Canada, Italy and India have almost consistently lost more working days per 1,000 employees than Australia.

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Bill

reading speech of the Minister and the promises of the Prime Minister that were referred to. That is what I have gone on from. I come to the present Conciliation and Arbitration Act and the provisions for secret ballots that have been in it for some number of years. Of course, we have heard from the Opposition side an explanation of just how often those provisions have been used and the results that have come from their use. We have heard from the Government side talk of the right to strike.
I will finish on this aspect because it is perfectly relevant. This Bill is further legislation to hamper and interfere with the democratic set-up of the trade union movement. Everyone believes in the right to strike.
Mr Tuckey-I don't.
Mr SCOTT-There is always an exception in every community. The editorial of the capitalist newspaper in Adelaide on 9 November 1974, in fact, was on the very question of the right to strike. Those who do not agree can certainly hold there own opinions. The editorial concerned the perfect strike. Public opinion unquestionably concedes the right of every free man and woman in a free society to withdraw his or her labour. It just draws the line at strikes. Perhaps we should try to design the perfect strike. The first essential is to get the profit motivation out of strikes. There is evidence that the public finds striking for higher pay, shorter hours and better conditions to be a distasteful display of materialism. A strike must not cause any loss of production or affect the country's supply of goods and services. Strikes should be arranged in the workers' own time rather than in the employer's. The strike must not cause any loss or inconvenience to customers. The timing of the perfect strike is very important. It would be disloyal to strike when the country is facing economic competition from overseas or at a time when a rise in wages would tend to increase prices. The strike should not be held under a Liberal-Country Party Government. This would be seen as political mischief-making. The strike should not be held under a Labor Government since it could be said to be stabbing one's own side in the back. It should not be an unofficial strike. The public frowns on unofficial strikes. It should not be an official strike because the public thinks that union officials should not encourage strikes. Therefore, taking all these points into consideration, the nearest we might get to the perfect strike would go something like this: A small, polite, well-behaved strike of company directors during one lunch hour in favour of lower wages for trade union officials. It should take place under an Australian Democrat Government just as soon as

America, the European Economic Community, Japan and our other principal economic competitors happen to sink into the sea.
I think that just about typifies the attitude of the Government. It says it believes in the right to strike. It knows that there is already in existence legislation providing for secret ballots. It must also know that a number of unions have written into their rules a requirement to have a secret ballot before they have a strike. More importantly Government members are not practitioners. They do not know what happens. In the realHfe situation the workers themselves at a factory gate meeting, usually at lunch time, determine whether they will take strike action. Often they will determine whether they will have a secret ballot. They do not have to be coerced. They do not have to have further legislation. They certainly do not have to have vehicles that provide for pimps, dobbers and troublemakers within the trade union movement who try to disrupt a democratic organisation.

industrial disputation. The Act that we now seek to amend has been amended about 70 times already, as I stated at the outset. I wish to quote from the speech made on this Bill by the former Minister for Industrial Relations, the honourable member for Kooyong (Mr Peacock). Page 2827 of Hansard states:
The Conciliation and Arbitration Act has been amended over 70 times since its enactment in 1904-14 times under the present Government. I have to say that this represents lack of direction and lack of consensus over many years on direction over time and the fallibility of legislation. This Act has surely reached saturation point. The cumulative effect of legislation on industrial relations is largely a matter of conjecture and often ill-informed assumption. There is a need for the Act to be simplified into a useful tool for the practitioner in industrial relations-not complicated by further legislation.

Of couse, we are not surprised by the divisive nature of this Bill as it has come from a government led by the most divisive Prime Minister since Federation. The secret ballot provision contained in the Bill is calculated to set one Australian worker against the other. It invites and encourages scabbing at the work places. In fact, it will have the effect of exacerbating industrial disputes instead of solving them. Members opposite are espousing the idea of reducing government involvement in every area where the Government should protect individuals and the community as a whole. The razor gang's proposals will reduce or abolish Federal Government responsibility for health, hospitals, migrant services, housing and so on. Yet the same Government vigorously insists that it should intervene in industrial disputes between workers and bosses. The only areas in which this Government is interested to intervene are those where by its intervention it can enlarge the high profits of corporations and at the same time reduce the ability of the workers to ensure for themselves a fair share of the cake. However, in this instance the Government's intervention is so inept that it is not welcomed even by the employers, 'whose interests it claims to represent. The employers know well that strike ballots will not reduce industrial disputation but will have a contrary effect.
The Government is an uninvited and unwelcome guest in this' area. The workers, the bosses or the Australian Conciliatiori and Arbitration Commission-those who are directly involved in industrial relations in Australia-have not suggested that amendments along the lines proposed by the Government are necessary or even desirable. The Arbitration Commission, as well as the Government, has enough power to deal with any

This Government has yet to learn that industrial action cannot be managed by the bludgeon of legislation but has to be approached with common sense and conciliation. As far as the trade union movement is concerned, it is not opposed to secret ballots. They are used in union elections. The trade union leadership is elected in secret ballots. However, it would be silly to suggest that each decision made by that democratically elected leadership should be submitted to secret ballot. Let us look at who is proposing this. Our Prime Minister would not be keen to put his razor gang proposals to the secret vote of the people. On the contrary, last October he was hiding his intentions from the people. Whilst trade union leaders have a mandate from their members to fight for better wages and working conditions, the Prime Minister had no mandate from the people of Australia to abandon universal health insurance or the Government's responsibilities in areas of hospital financing and health care. He had no mandate to sell out publicly owned companies and land. The man who was sneaking in through a back door at Yarralumla is trying to teach workers democracy. What a hypocrite!
The right to strike is an important human right achieved by the workers in countries such as ours. If that right is taken away or limited by government interference we might as well pack our cases and join President Marcos or some other little dictator friend of honourable members opposite. It will be a sorry day for Australia when the Australian worker is not allowed, freely and without fear, to negotiate and, if necessary, to fight for better wages and working conditions. The Government is trying to tell us that strike ballots will reduce industrial disputation, that unions are not democratic and that their leadership does not consult with membership before and during industrial action. There is nothing further from the truth. Decisions to use the worker's ultimate weapon are
