Mr HAWKE (Wills) (8.58)-Because of the exigencies of time, we are not able to consider this

matter in Committee. However, I want to make a couple of comments at this third reading stage of the Bill which seem to be necessary if there is to be a proper understanding of the inadequacies of the legislation this House has been asked to pass. I draw the attention of the House to a quite basic inadequacy in clause 6, where the fundamental requirement is that the members of an organisation should have been directed or requested to take part in industrial action. One would expect that in this fundamental requirement to trigger the whole mechanism of the Bill, that is, that the members should have been directed or requested to engage in industrial action, there would have been some definition of what a direction or request is. That is something in respect of which the legislation is entirely silent. Much more disturbing is the provision in clause 6, proposed new sub-section (3D), which states:
Subject to this section, the Commission shall, upon an application made to it under sub-section (3A), order that a vote of the members to whom the application relates . . .
So the sub-section goes on. We have a situation where, in respect of the wording as it stands now, a group of members in one place of employment could make an application. It could be that that application would, in fact, relate to other members of the union in other places of employment. Of course, that is a quite ridiculous situation. Yet to clarify that appropriate regulations will have to be brought into being. As one of my fellow members on this side of the House has pointed out, that will be something that the Government cannot look forward to with any confidence in the future.
Perhaps more than anything else the things that indicate the totally one-sided nature of the Government's approach in this matter are the proposed changes to section 45C of the Act. Without going into the exact language of those changes, the substance of what is suggested is that if a ballot is, in fact, undertaken and the majority of those people taking part in the ballot are not in favour of participating in industrial action then, of course, they are not required to do so and the will of the majority in that respect is taken into account. They cannot in any way be forced to take action, and action cannot be taken against them. In other words, the Government presumably is indicating that, in that sense, the will of the majority as ascertained in a ballot should prevail. One would have thought in these circumstances-if one were in fact concerned with the principle of allowing the will of the majority to prevail-there would have been a provision to cover the obverse situation. If, in fact, the ballot were taken and the majority voted in

2916 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Health Acts Amendment Bill

favour of industrial action then there would be a requirement in those circumstances for the will of the majority to prevail. Those who were in the minority would be bound by the will of the majority as expressed in the ballot. Of course, it is indicative of the one-sided approach of this Government that the legislation does not so provide.
Because of the undertaking which my colleagues on this side of the House have arrived at with leaders on the other side of the House and because it is time to come to the end of this debate, it is impossible, therefore, to allude to all the detailed inadequacies of the Bill. As far as I am concerned, as that agreement is like an industrial agreement and is one which will be kept, at this stage I conclude by expressing my regret that the Government has inflicted upon the community a piece of legislation which is not only unnecessary but also will in the future provoke industrial conflict which would not otherwise have arisen.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
Bill read a third time.
HEALTH ACTS AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr MacKellar, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr MacKELLAR (Warringah-Minister for Health) (9.4)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The Bill is an omnibus Bill by which the Government seeks to make wide-ranging changes to four health Acts. These will give effect to the Government's philosphy and goals outlined in my recent statement to the House on health care services. As honourable members will be aware, the Government's overriding goal in the health area is that all Australians should have access to the high quality health care services we now enjoy in this country. But the achievement of this aim must be at a cost the individual and the community can afford. The Government believes that the most equitable and responsible means of achieving these goals is by means of a health policy that:
(i) requires the State and Northern Territory governments to take up their full constitutional responsibilities for the provision of health services;
(ii) ensures that those in genuine need receive special assistance to cover their hospital and medical bills;
(iii) results in other people contributing towards the cost of the health services they receive-either totally out of their own pockets or through health insurance;

(iv) ensures that health insurance is available to all people at a reasonable cost and directs the relevant government subsidies towards those who are willing to help themselves;
(v) recognises the importance of the community rating principle for a viable health insurance industry;
(vi) fully acknowledges the important role of the private sector of the health care delivery system, particularly private hospitals;
and (vii) seeks to ensure that users and providers of
health care services become more aware of the costs they generate.
The Bill now before the House will also:
(i) ensure immediate eligibility to receive basic insurance benefits for people taking out such cover with a registered health fund between I September and 31 October 1981 irrespective of any pre-existing illness
conditions; (ii) ensure that those people who lose their
entitlement to a pensioner health benefit or health care card and then choose to insure with a registered health fund can immediately become eligible for benefits at the basic coverage level; (iii) provide for the Commonwealth to pay all nursing home benefits and, so, take over responsibility for those nursing home benefits currently paid by health funds for insured persons; (iv) provide further protection to people in a nursing home against being unnecessarily charged for extras that would normally be regarded as part of nursing home care and, therefore, included in the approved fee; (v) expand the operation of the isolated patients' travel and accommodation assistance scheme; (vi) give effect to certain recommendations made by the pathology services working party which have been accepted by the Government; (vii) overcome a problem in the existing legislation which relates to the bulk billing of certain pathology services; and (viii) extend the Commonwealth's supervision of the production and supply of certain therapeutic goods.
The House will recall that in my statement of 29 April on the Government's new health policy I outlined new funding arrangements it is proposed to introduce for State health services. They are designed to ensure that the waste, inefficiencies and unnecessary duplication of

Health Acts Amendment Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2919

the means of ensuring that no insured persons are adversely affected by actions of a fund that are contrary to Government policy.
In order to further its aim of ensuring that insurance is available at a reasonable cost-and, at the same time, to provide greater protection for the aged and the chronically ill in relation to the cost of nursing home carethe Government has decided to take over full responsibility for the payment of nursing home benefits. The amendments to the National Health Act provided for in Part III of the Bill, together with complementary amendments to the Nursing Homes Assistance Act, will give effect to this decision. They will operate from 1 September 1981.
Since 1 October 1977 registered hospital benefits organisations have been required to pay, as part of basic hospital insurance, all nursing home benefits for their members. These benefits payable by the funds are equivalent in level to the benefits the Commonwealth pays for all patients in nursing homes who are not insured. The Government has decided that this arrangement is unsatisfactory for two principal reasons, namely: Nursing home patients are, in the strict sense, uninsurable through voluntary insurance in which guarante~d benefits without discrimination or exclusions are made mandatory; and the arrangement represents an inequitable liability for the bulk of people with hospital insurance. The Government believes that this decision will ensure a more equitable distribution of the cost of Commonwealth assistance to those in need of nursing home care. The Government also believes that this measure will further assist in containing the cost of health insurance.
The Government will also be providing additional protection for people receiving nursing home care by new provisions more clearly to prevent nursing homes from imposing a levy or compulsory additional charge on patients. Regrettably some nursing homes attempt to circumvent the present fee control arrangements by such measures. This practice will be prevented by clause 45. I would add that the great bulk of nursing homes do not attempt to pursue such practices, and these new provisions should in no way be taken as a reflection on the industry as a whole.
The Bill now before the House-by virtue of clause 59-also gives effect to the Government's decision to prohibit registered health benefit funds from operating optional benefit tables. These tables undermine the community rating principle under which the cost of health insurance is borne equally by contributors with such cover. The effect of this principle is to reduce the cost of

health insurance to the more needy sections of the community-such as the aged, the chronically ill and families with children. The operation of clause 59 will therefore strengthen the community rating principle. It will also simplify the present health insurance arrangements.
The Commonwealth fully acknowledges the important role of the private sector of the health care delivery system. The Government has received a number of submissions expressing concern at the financial problems facing private hospitals. It recognises that in the medium to longer term its overall health policy will assist in correcting this situation. However, immediate action is necessary to assist private hospitals in meeting the increased cost of servicing surgical beds. Surgical facilities in private hospitals should continue to be available to the community.
To achieve this goal a higher rate of subsidy-of $28 per day--is to be introduced for private hospital patients whose admission to hospital or subsequent treatment is primarily related to a surgical procedure. This is achieved by clause 20. The new subsidy will operate from the date the Bill receives royal assent. The existing bed day subsidy of $16 per day will be retained for non-surgical patients in private hospitals. It is proposed that the medical services in private hospitals that will attract the higher subsidy will be prescribed in regulations. The Bill also provides that the Minister for Health will have a discretionary power to review individual cases, and by regulation, to admit additional schedule items to the approved list.
The Bill now before the House contains a number of provisions related to the Isolated Patients' Travel and Accommodation Scheme, the Therapeutic Goods Act and several machinery matters. By virtue of clauses 41 to 44 inclusive, the IPTAAS Scheme is to be expanded in line with the Government's decision last year to include cleft lip and cleft palate patients under 22 years of age requiring orthodontic and associated treatment and other patients requiring specialist oral surgery. The extension of the scheme is to apply retrospectively from 1 January 1981 to coincide with the date of the inclusion of orthodontic treatment for cleft lip patients in the medical benefits schedule.
Part V of the Bill now before the House would amend the Therapeutic Goods Act. The amendments seek to keep Australia abreast with other developed countries and further exemplifies the Government's belief that it should have the capability of ensuring that the therapeutic goods available in Australia are safe, pure, potent and of

2920 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Health Acts Amendment Bm

good quality. Clause 72 of the Bill would amend the definition of therapeutic use to ensure that health care products and medical devices including contraceptive devices, pacemakers and artificial heart valves clearly come within the scope of the Act.
The Bill would also update the references to British publications which are the source of standards for goods for therapeutic use. Unless this is done, the standards upon which Australia has relied for so long would no'longer have effect. The Bill would authorise the Minister for Health to set standards by references to other published sources. Under the present legislation the means for obtaining essential information about the way biological products are made is not available. The Bill would provide a mechanism to allow the Commonwealth, when necessary, to seek information about the manufacture and testing of biological products. It will also 'provide a mechanism to regulate their release on to the market. As the need for a complete list of the medicines used in Australia has long been felt, the Bill would establish the national register of therapeutic goods and provide the power to acquire information regarding the composition of goods included in the register.
To safeguard the rights of manufacturers, the Bill will include appeal machinery. I draw the attention of honourable members to clause 85 which will provide for appeal to the Administrative Appeals Tribunal against decisions of the Director-General that affect the commercial operations of a manufacturer. In addition, before the Director-General can seek information about medicinal preparations from a manufacturer for inclusion in the proposed register, those preparations must be declared by the regulations to be goods to which the register applies. The type of information must also be prescribed by the regulations. In this way the Parliament will have the opportunity to oversight the information which is being recorded. Similar arrangements will apply in relation to the expansion of the scope of the Therapeutic Goods Act. Before new health care products can be brought within its scope they must be prescribed.
The Bill also contains a number of clauses related to the rendering of pathology services. Clause 12 would correct an anomaly in relation to the assignment of benefits for pathology services. The Health Insurance Act now stipulates that in cases of bulk billing, a patient must assign his benefit at the time or after the service is provided. However, in the case of the majority of pathology services, the service is carried out sometime after a specimen is collected from the patient and the

pathologist has no face-to-face contact with the patient. The amendment will enable benefits to be assigned in advance of the actual rendering of the pathology service.
Clauses 9, 10, 28 and 34 to 37 inclusive of the Bill include a number of changes to the approved pathology practitioner provisions that were recommended by the pathology services working party. This working part was establis~ed by the Government in 1976 principally to review pathology arrangements and schedule fees and, benefits for pathology services. Its members included representatives from the Australian Medical Association, the Royal College of Pathologists of Australia, the Society of Pathologists in Private Practice, the Health Commission of New South Wales and Commonwealth departments.
Under the present arrangements medical practitioners requiring pathology tests to be performed must make their request in writing. The amendment in clause 9 will enable the Minister to approve requests in other forms so as to allow modern methods of data transmission to be used. In addition, the amendment would enable the Minister also to approve the use of alternative methods of modern storage media such as on microfilm and microfiche.
The effect of clause 10 is to require the Minister for Health to keep the Australian Medical Association informed in writing of any changes referred to the medical benefits advisory committee which involved variations to undertakings entered into by pathologists and for the Minister to be aware of the AMA comments before making such changes. The clause also provides for the AMA to be consulted ahead of the implementation of any recommendations by the medical benefits advisory committee which could lead to variations to undertakings.
Clause 28 sets out those practices which were regarded by the pathology services working party as being inconsistent with the generally accepted standards of professional conduct. The effect of this will be to reduce the incidence of the provision of unnecessary practices. In order to clarify for approved pathology practitioners those practices prohibited by clause 28 it will be necessary for them to enter into new undertakings with the Minister for Health. Clauses 34 to 37 provide for the transition from the present undertaking to the new undertaking to be carried out in an orderly fashion.
Other machinery or clarifying amendments that will be of interest to honourable members are as follows: Clause 60 would extend the provision~

Housing Assistance Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2921

in the National Health Act which presently permit the Minister to exempt an organisation from paying medical benefits for certain services also to cover benefits for hospitalisation in respect of these same services. The provision clearly defines the limits or exemptions and protects the rights of contributors who seek to transfer to another organisation because of the exemption.. Clause 47 will put beyond doubt that under section 43A of the National Health Act the Director-General may request the balance sheets and related statements of nursing homes, as well as other relevant accounts such as profit and loss statements.
Conclusion The Bill now before the House will give effect to the health policy changes I announced on 29 April 1981. The overriding aim of the Government's new health policy is to ensure that all Australians have access to high quality health care at a cost the individual and the community can afford. The Government believes that the most equitable and responsible means of achieving that goal is through a combination of special assistance schemes for those in genuine need and the fostering of private health insurance for other people. Honourable members should not lose sight of the fact that major Government subsidies, financed through the general taxation system, are provided to ensure that health insurance is available to the average family man at a reasonable cost. However, the Government rejects the concept of a tax financed universal health insurance scheme. The Government believes that such an approach to health financing does not contain sufficient inbuilt checks and balances designed to prevent overuse and abuses of the health care delivery system and, therefore, that such a system would inevitably lead to an increase in the overall cost of providing health services to the detriment of all taxpayers. Proper health care is vital to all Australians. But the Government's health policy must be both equitable and responsible. The Bill now before the House will achieve these dual goals. I commend the Bill to the House. Debate (onmotion by Dr Blewett) adjourned.
HOUSING ASSISTANCE BILL 1981 Second Reading
Debate resumed from 14 May, on motion by MrMcVeigh:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr UREN (Reid) (9.29)-At the outset I want to protest and place on record my opposition to the Government's attitude in regard to the agreement under which the Commonwealth

will make money available to the States over the next five years for housing purposes. This Government is not even prepared to debate this matter when an allocation is made each year. The Government Whip, on behalf of the Government, told us that one hour will be allocated to debate on this legislation. This state of affairs shows the complete incompetence of the Government. The Parliament has been sitting for months. Opposition members have been waiting to debate issues. We on this side of the House want to debate legislation. Now an enormous amount of legislation is being introduced. I move:

That all words after 'That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:

this House is of the opinion that-

(I) the Bill should be withdrawn and redrafted because it fails to provide sufficient funds to meet the neglected housing needs of the Australian people and signals a further withdrawal by the Federal Government from its-
(a) responsibility to ensure, in co-operation with the States, Territories and local government, security of housing for all Australians, and
(b) direct responsibility for the housing of Aboriginal people;

(2) the Bill should provide-

(a) sufficient funds to housing authorities to ensure a significant reduction of the backlog on their waiting lists;

(b) sufficient funds to ensure a permanent reduction in the number of persons who are homeless or living in sub-standard accommodation;

(c) sufficient funds to allow the housing authorities to undertake a comprehensive survey of housing needs and the adequacy of the existing housing stock;

(d) sufficient funds to enable the housing authorities, in co-operation with the Commonwealth, to develop a strategy as part of overall urban and regional policy planning to ensure a social mix in housing estates as well as ensuring that public housing tenants have access to social and cultural amenities and job opportunities;

(e) sufficient funds to allow the housing authorities to expand and improve the stock of public housing while enabling them to continue an effective program of home ownership assistance for low and middle income earners;

(f) that the interest rates charged for home pur-

chase account 'on lent' to other lending

authorities should rise in line with capacity

to pay;

.

(g) that the rents of public housing tenants be related to capacity to pay and otherwise determined on an averaged cost-rent basis, and

(h) an adequate forward commitment of real funds to enable the States and Territories to properly plan their h,ousing programs, and

2922 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Housing Assistance Bill

(3) the opportunity should be provided for the Commonwealth Parliament to debate annually the appropriation to determine progress made in relation to the objectives of the Agreement.
The Housing Assistance Bill is a disgrace, a national scandal. It represents a further withdrawal of the Federal Government from its national responsibilities to ensure security of housing for all Australians. It is an intolerable withdrawal of its special responsibility to provide for the welfare of the Aboriginal people. It signals increases in hardship for the growing number of low and middle income earners. Further, it neglects the hundreds of thousands of people in need of housing security. Never before in the history of the Commonwealth and State Housing Agreement has the Federal Government given so little. Never before has the Federal Government been so mean and miserable. The new agreement is to last for at least five years, and this Bill will be debated for one hour. It will not even be reviewed during those five years. It cannot properly be called an agreement. It is an imposition. The Fraser Government has imposed on the States and individuals the burden of its misguided economic policies. This imposition represents a further transfer of public funds away from the needs of the people and towards the corporate sector.
This Government has singled out the housing sector, both private and public, but particularly the public sector, to bear the brunt of its regressive policies of transferring wealth away from the working people of Australia and their dependants. Next to unemployment, housing security is the most serious social problem in Australia. It is aggravated by the contractionary and regressive policies of the Fraser Government. The full extent of housing needs in Australia has been obscured by this Government's failure to provide regular detailed housing information. We are told that across Australia there are 80,000 families on the waiting lists of the housing authorities, but that is a real under-estimation of the real number of people in need of rental accommodation and home purchase assistance. In New South Wales alone, at the end of December 1980 over 36,000 families were on the New South Wales Housing Commission list. The New South Wales Minister of Housing has already made a clear statement that if the new Agreement goes through 40,000 families will be on the waiting list by the end of this year. Of course, the Agreement will be forced through by the Government with its numbers.
The Victorian Minister of Housing has estimated conservatively that at least 100,000 households are in need in that State. On the basis of the 1974-75 data, he has estimated that the

public housing stock in Victoria can cater for only 34 per cent of apparent needs. Since that time the number of people below the poverty line has increased as has the number of unemployed and homeless. The Victorian Housing Minister places due blame on the Federal Government's cutback in public sector housing outlays foJ' the deterioration in the Victorian housing position. Even the Victorian Government's irresponsible selling off of too large a proportion of its public housing stock has left it with mostly high rise units or houses in unsuitable areas. The waiting time in Victoria is some cases exceeds four years. It is a wonder that hundreds of thousands of people are not on the waiting list. People know that they hardly have a chance, no matter how long they wait. They know that what they will be offered probably will not be suitable for them in the long term.
Some 80,000 families on the waiting lists of the State public housing authorities around the country is an unacceptable number, but even that number does not indicate the full extent of the housing needs. Across Australia at present about 150,000 people are homeless. A quarter of a million people are living in caravans or temporary makeshift accommodation. In the city of Gladstone about one-quarter of the population is now living in caravans, and those figures do not take into account the new growth in Gladstone. They represent the backlog from the 1960s and 1970s. At the start of the so-called resource development about 25 per cent of people in Gladstone already are living in caravans and temporary accommodation. This will happen across the country wherever resource development occurs. People will live in squalor. This Government cannot and will not provide more detailed statistics, yet it will encourage the Department of Housing and Construction to develop a prototype of a mobile home, the size of a caravan, to be used to house workers and their families in the regions where resource development is taking place. Honourable members should have nO doubt that later the Government will put forward this type of accommodation as an alternative in our cities. Instead of providing decent public housing it will try to put people into temporary accommodation.
The problem of housing involves both the public and private sectors. Private rents are increasing beyond the means of the low income families, which are vulnerable to acute poverty as higher and higher proportions of their declining disposable incomes go in rent. I give one example, and I have witnessed this myself. In a terrace in the

Housing Assistance Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2923

inner city suburb of Stanmore in Sydney six rooms are rented for between $35 and $60 a week each. Most of the tenants are pensioners. The terrace has one toilet, with outside access for 10 people. Few of the rooms have hot running water. One male pensioner uses a methylated spirit lighter to cook his meals. The place has not been painted for 10 years. The ceiling leaks in a number of places and basic repairs have not been carried out despite the tenant's complaints. Unfortunately, this case is not an exception. There are thousands of over priced, overcrowded, sub-standard dwellings on the Sydney, Melbourne and Gladstone and other city rental markets.
A growing number of people have no choice but to accept what they can get. They dare not complain for fear they will be evicted. That is their problem. There are just too many people in fear they will be evicted if they complain and seek justice. It is not possible to get justice under this type of conservative government. it would not be difficult for me and for other members of the House to describe case by case moral neglect and exploitation in the private rental market. I am sure that if honourable members on the other side cared to look they would find case after case of intolerable hardship for pensioners, single parents, unemployed youth and other groups, but particularly for the young seeking homes. These people deserve more just and humane housing security.
Let us consider the home ownership side of the private housing market. After all this Government seems to be very proud that it is a government of private enterprise. This Government is content to parade figures from a few years back to show that the level of home ownership in Australia, by international standards, is high. The Government neglects to mention that home ownership is declining dramatically, particularly for people between the ages of 20 and 30 years. We have been given figures to suggest that the rate of lending for home purchase is increasing, but we are not told the whole truth. The rate of lending
of money to first home buyers is declining. An

increasing proportion of loans is going to people who already have homes and who arc seeking to renovate them, move into a higher priced home or acquire a second, third, fourth or fifth horne. We are not told of the effect of speculation in the housing market which is pushing up prices in the sought after areas of the capital cities, thus putting home ownership outside the reach of a growing number of people.
Some wealthy people are well housed. There are those smart people, who can afford to do it, who can get finance for a second home. They are paying high interest rates to get that second home, but in buying and renting out that second home they can get the interest rate as a tax deduction. Consequently, many people in Sydney, the smarties and the wealthy, instead of investing in gold are investing in real estate. People are suffering as a result because they cannot afford to pay the rent. This Government will not come clean about the factors which have increased the home loan interest rates which have been brought about by its obsession to hold down the domestic and public sector activities in order to allow the foreign corporations free rein in the Australian resource economy. The broad policies in the Australian resource economy are really the Achilles heel of this housing policy.
The Government is not prepared to face up to the fact that persons earning less than $15,000 a year-that accounts for around three-quarters of the people in the work force-cannot obtain sufficient funds to purchase a home. This Government will not face up to the fact that access to home ownership has declined from nine out of ten wage earners ten years ago to two out of ten wage earners today. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard several tables showing the levels of income required to service a housing loan of $30,000 at different interest rates and a deposit gap for home owners on average earnings trying to buy a home priced at $45,000, $55,000 and $65,000.

Mr UREN-I thank the House. The tables show that the person seeking a housing loan of
$30,000 over a 30-year term at 11 t per cent
interest rate requires a weekly income of $275. That excludes 71 per cent of the male work force in this country. With a one per cent increase to 12+ per cent-the rise likely under the policies of this Government-the weekly income required to service that loan would be $296 a week. That excludes 76 per cent of the male work force. It is shown that a person on average earnings who wishes to buy a house for $55,000, which is almost $30,000 below the medium price in Sydney, has a borrowing limit of $30,140. At an 1it per cent interest rate, that leaves a deposit gap of $19,360. If the interest rate is 12t per cent the deposit gap grows to $21,350. Where can a person find the remainder to make up that deposit gap? That is the impossible dream. That is the situation in regard to housing under private ownership in this country.
A couple could rely on their joint income to obtain a large loan or to secure a second mortgage of around 16 per cent and upwards. For most people such a decision would mean deferring a

family. For many it would involve a risk of overcommitment in the event of a partner losing a job. The point is that the pressures of the private home
ownership market are creating a higher demand
for rental accommodation. In the private rental market those on relatively high incomes are pushing the lower income tenants out as landlords see the opportunity to push up rents so that low income tenants are forced to grab what they can. As I have ~aid, we would see a much higher level of demand for public housing if there were sufficient suitable public housing stock available. The situation is similar to that existing in the labour market. If betler jobs were available the unemployment statistics would rise in the immediate term
as the hidden unemploment. Those too discouraged to worry about being included in the labour force statistics would come out of hiding. So it is in the public housing sector that present statistics underestimate by a factor of at least ten
the real number of people in this country waiting for homes. What has been the Government's re-
sponse? I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard several tables showing outlays by the Federal Government on housing since 1974-75.

Mr UREN-I thank the House. The tables bear close inspection. The Bill provides for a forward commitment in money terms, not in real terms, of $200m each year for the next five years in federal payments to the States and the Northern Territory for housing assistance. In today's prices, the real outlay on housing payments to the States and the Northern Territory, including Aboriginal housing, pensioner housing and assistance to other groups of people in need, equals $850m. In other words, taking the figures in real terms in 1974-75-the last full year Labor was in government-Labor made $850m available, and this Government is making $200m available this year. If we look at the inflation figure and take it as an average of 10 per cent over the next 5 years if the Government keeps this base rate of $200m then to get the same value the figure should be $292.8m at the end of the 5year term.
This Bill provides for less than one-quarter of the amount which Labor gave 5 years ago. The allocation will be eroded more and more by inflationary pressures under this Government. The Minister has told us that the allocation gives the States and the Northern Territory a chance to enable them properly to plan their housing programs. It gives them a forward commitment to increase stringency. It directs them to cut back on levels of housing services. It entices them to pull out of the Commonwealth-State Agreement. Let us consider some specifics. I was intrigued by the Minister's ignorance, if I may say so, in his statement on Tuesday, 26 May, in answer to a question by the honourable member for Bendigo (Mr Bourchier), the Government Whip, who professes to be very interested in housing. On behalf of the Government he has limited this housing debate to one hour. In effect the Minister said that claims of some States having to pay more back to the Commonwealth in capital and interest repayment than they were getting from the Commonwealth under this agreement were scurrilous. What are the facts? I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a table showing the real net payments to the States for housing from 1974-75 to 1980-81.

Mr UREN-I thank the House. The table net amount after both the interest repayment and the
capital repayment have been repaid. The net amount of Federal payments has fallen from $515min 1974-75to$75.7min 1980-81. This year
the States can use only one-seventh of the amount of funds that they had 6 years ago. This year the States are paying back $200.3m to the Commonwealth. A question was asked of the Minister by the honourable member for Grayndler (Mr Leo McLeay) this morning during Question Time. Because it was not a Dorothy Dix question the Minister could not answer. I had to take a point of order to say: 'You are only offering $200m a year
but, in fact, the States are paying $200.3m back to you'. Of course, this will continue each y, ear. The

housing situation is getting so bad that the Minister, like Pontius Pilate, wants to wash his hands of any of the real responsibilities for housing problems. In other words, what we are really looking at is a very acute position.
The $200m that has been made available this year will consist of $54m in a direct grant for Aboriginals and pensioners and $l46m of loan money. Next year, New South Wales will receive 31.1 per cent of the $146m of loan funds made available to the States and the Northern Territory; that is, $45m of which $32m will go to the Housing Commission of New South Wales and the remaining $13.5m to the building societies.

Housing Assistance Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2927

The New South Wales Housing Commission estimates that it will be paying back to the Commonwealth $56m in both interest and capital repayments. The New South Wales Government does not know how much of the $54m of grants for Aboriginal housing, pensioners and other persons in need it will receive next year. Under this Bill giving 'autonomy' to the States this is a matter for the Minister to determine on his assessment of need. How can the Minister make such an assessment when he does not have the necessary information? How can he argue that the States can plan their programs properly when they do not know what grants they will receive and whether they will receive topping up funds once the program is under way?
In South Australia the position is similar. South Australia will receive a 14.2 per cent share of the $146m loans, which is about $21m this year-a ~ut of over $4m on the previous year and about the same amount that South Australia will be paying back to the Commonwealth next year in interest repayments and capital repayments. South Australia has had a progressive housing policy under the Playford non-Labor Government and under Labor governments of building up its public housing stock. But that possibility in the future may well be undermined by the shift to per capita allocation of housing assistance which will erode the payments to South Australia even further. South Australia faces a future of falling Commonwealth payments and rising State repayments. It is the people of South Australia in need of housing assistance who truly will be the meat in the sandwich.
A special feature of this Bill is the inclusion of Aboriginal housing as a State responsibility. The Australian Government has reneged on its special responsibilities to the Aboriginal people and reduced the funds available for Aboriginal housing in real terms from approximately $81m in 1974-75 to less than $62.4m in 1980-81. Next year even less will be made available as only $54m is earmarked for special assistance. This includes not only Aboriginals but also pensioners and other groups in need. All will suffer as a result.
Public housing tenants will face further rent increases as the new agreement moves away from the inequitable concept of market related rents to the outrageous proposal for market rents in the public sector. In some magical way this Bill provides for a uniform national system of establishing market rents. How in heaven's name the Government can do that I do not know. The cost of housing differs so much from city to city and from State to State. In Victoria the sheltered group has

called this move a conspiracy against public housing tenants. It is a sad case of policy based on the backward theory that the tail wags the dog. In 1979 over 40 per cent of public housing tenants in Victoria were in rental arrears. The number has increased since. An increasing proportion are more than 10 weeks in arrears. The rate of increase in rental rebates exceeds the rate of increase in rents received in all States. The sad thing about this is that the people getting into arrears are facing insecurity and the fear that has been created because of the enormous debt burden thrown on them by the policies of this Government. Under these conditions the States have smaller rather than larger surpluses with which to finance public housing improvements and expansion. All the policy of market rents means is that the needy and the poor are subsidising the very poor and the very needy. There remains no evidence that middle income tenants have departed from public housing or that eligible low income earners have increased their entry into this area. All the evidence points to the increasing fear and insecurity on the part of public housing tenants-an effect which is contrary to the objective of providing housing security for people in need.
This Bill also provides for rental assistance to be given to private sector tenants. I say with due respect to those people that subsidising public housing rentals is no real solution. The major solution is that we have to make sure that we make more money available to build public sector housing so that form of rental is available. It represents a waste of public expenditures to invest taxpayers' monies in the pockets of existing multiple home owners without the public gaining any tangible return of the investment through an increase in the size and quality of public housing sector stock. Already we have seen the public housing sector reduced to a minimal proportion of the total housing stock in Australia. In 1974-75 public dwelling commencements totalled 19,000. In 1979-80 they were down to almost half that number at 10,500. The public housing sector is a crucial element that provides balance, stability and security in the housing market.
Labor is committed to increasing the access of people to private home ownership. We are clearly committed to that policy. If we can ever get a person on the first rung of the ladder to private home ownership we want to do that. In conclusion I say, as I said at the beginning, that this Bill is a disgrace. This Commonwealth and State Housing Agreement is a scandal. The next Federal Labor Government will renegotiate this agreement and create a new agreement. We will do that after we

2928 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

New Business after 11 p.m.

have discussions with the States. The only way we will really begin to solve the problem is by discussion between the national government and the States, the private sector and local government. That is the only way that we really can decide the great social problem of housing in this country.

HOUSING ASSISTANCE BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed.
Mr CHAPMAN (Kingston) (10.10)-1 thank the Opposition for bringing so many honourable members into the House to listen to my remarks this evening, I certainly welcome this legislation, the Housing Assistance Bill 1981. The Bill is an affirmation that the present Federal Government consistently recognises those most in need and is always working to improve their situation, whether in regard to housing or other needs. It helps to provide housing to those who are unable or cannot afford to purchase their own homes. The Bill also strongly reinforces the Federal Government's policy of minimum interference in State government affairs. The majority of funds are provided by way of untied moneys. This is a positive demonstration of the Government's commitment to federalism.
I strongly reject the comments made earlier in this debate by the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren). They lacked both substance and logic. I recall the debate in 1978 on the Housing Assistance Bill 1978, which introduced the housing agreement that is due to expire on 30 June this year. That legislation gave effect to what was then a new three-year housing agreement. I took the

honourable member for Reid to task for his comments during that debate. His comments tonight show he has not learnt anything during the last three years. It is worthwhile referring briefly to that debate. The honourable member for Reid claimed that our 1978 legislation would not reduce the backlog of families waiting for housing commission or housing trust homes. The backlog was then around 100,000 homes. However, under that legislation, over the last three years of this Government's administration the backlog has been reduced to 85,000 homes. So the predictions of doom and gloom made by the honourable member for Reid were not fulfilled over that period. Therefore, the comments he has made this evening concerning this legislation should similarly be taken with a grain of salt.
In his remarks tonight, the honourable member for Reid claimed that no detailed information is provided concerning housing in Australia. But since 1978 this Government has ensured that an annual report on housing has been provided to the Parliament. Each year we have provided the most comprehensive report on welfare housing produced in Australia. He claimed that no provision was made for those who find themselves in a homeless situation. But the State housing authorities are able to provide urgently rental housing on a priority basis to those shown to be homeless.
The Australian Labor Party does not support the present Government's positive attitude to housing. It takes the view that the more money the Government spends the better off all members of the community will be, including those most in need. That is a complete fallacy. The experience of the last decade clearly shows that the great bulk of the community is better off if government spending, and therefore taxation, is kept to a minimum so that people are left to spend their own incomes according to their own preferences, free of bureaucratic interference.
Mr Porter-Hear, hear!
Dr Edwards--Hear, hear!
Mr CHAPMAN-I thank the honourable member for Barker and the honourable member for Berowra for their support. Government spending can then be directed to those in real need, who are unable to provide for themselves in any way. The detrimental impact of the housing policy of the Labor Party is reflected in its miserable record in the era it was in office. Housing interest rates soared as high as 11 per cent, which was a 4 per cent increase over the three years it was in office. In marked contrast, the Fraser Government has largely stabilised interest rates

2930 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Housing Assistance Bill

over the last three years, the highest rate being 12.5 per cent, an increase of only 1.5 per cent in the five years it has been in office. This compares with the 4 per cent increase during just three years that Labor was in office.
Under the Labor Administration, the cost of building materials rose by over 60 per cent. Waiting lists for welfare housing increased to 104,000 families. The Labor Party also discriminates very clearly against home ownership for single income families. In its policy for home ownership put to the people before the last election, it stated that a means test of only $16,000 would apply to single income families seeking assistance. However, the means test for two-income families would be $24,000. That is clear discrimination. A single income family with any number of children would not have been able to receive a grant if its total household income from that one breadwinner was over $16,000, but a family with two breadwinners would have been able to receive assistance, whether it had children or not, if its income was under $24,000. The honourable member for Reid has left the chamber, so his concern about housing, as I said earlier, can be taken with a grain of salt.
Mr Porter -He can't take it.
Mr CHAPMAN-He certainly cannot take it. The electorate quite correctly rejected such an iniquitous proposal with its resounding defeat of the Labor Party at the last election. I might ask whether the Labor Party has rethought its policy on those matters as a result of its defeat.
This Bill is the major instrument for the implementation of the present Government's welfare housing policy. The Commonwealth is providing $285m to the States and the Northern Territory for the purpose of welfare housing in 1980-81. The Commonwealth has also offered to guarantee to provide a base level of $1 ,000m over the five years from July 1981 to assist the States and the Northern Territory in the provision of welfare housing. Additional funds will be allocated each year under this proposed arrangement as part of normal budgetary procedures. So, contrary to the comments of the honourable member for Reid, the $200m to be provided each year is a minimum amount to be provided and not the maximum, as he perhaps would have had us believe. The Bill is an effective demonstration of the Fraser Government's commitment to the provision of adequate housing for the people of Australia and of encouraging the widest possible enjoyment of home ownership.
Mr Robert Brown-That is not so.

MI' CHAPMAN-It is so. Such a program of Government assistance as is provided in this Bill continues to be an important requirement. At the present time some 85,000 people or families throughout Australia are on the waiting list for government-funded rental accommodation. Of these, some 19,000 live in my own State of South Australia. However, the present Government has made significant progress in reducing the massive backlog which developed during the era of the Whitlam Labor Government. From a backlog of something less than 80,000 when the Labor Party came to office, the backlog soared to 104,000 when it left office. This was a direct result of its disastrous economic management, which had a dual effect. More people became dependent on welfare and the cost of housing soared. To reduce this backlog of 85,000 over five years is a creditable performance by this Government. Similarly, over the same period this Government has increased the stock of lettable government housing from 194,000 to 210,000. In South Australia the backlog of applications has been reduced from 23,000 to 19,000, while the stocks of lettable dwellings have increased from 38,000 to 42,000. Despite a continuing need, the Government's policies are commendable. This legislation will continue that favourable trend.
While encouraging home ownership, the Government recognises that there are those who, because of their circumstances, cannot afford to buy a house. However, some 72 per cent of homes are owned or are being purchased by their occupants. Australia has one of the highest levels of home ownership in the world. It used to be even higher. It is a tragedy that the percentage fell during the period that Labor was in office. The success of the Fraser Government in containing inflation and interest rates in comparison with the way they soared between 1972 and 1975, and also by way of current comparison with most industrialised countries around the world, has helped home ownership grow again. Over the last five years an extra 600,0000 Australian families have acquired their own homes.
Home ownership is a stabilising influence in the community, so it is vital that home purchase assistance be maintained and strengthened by this Government. The 1978 housing agreement was a progressive step forward towards this. When it expires in June, the new arrangements this Bill provides will be more beneficial to needy people than was the case before. Whether people are able to purchase their own homes or must continue to rent, the Government has an obligation to help them. The Fraser Government is fulfilling that obligation. Mr DUFFY (Holt) (1O.23)-The amendment moved by the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren) goes to the heart of the problem of welfare housing in Australia. The Housing Assistance Bill does not provide sufficient funds to meet the neglected housing needs of the Australian people and signals further withdrawal by the Federal Government from its responsibility in this area. Federal expenditure on housing peaked in 1974-75, and real expenditure has been cut back each year since. As a result, public housing construction rates continue to decline and waiting lists and waiting times continue to increase. The problem is exacerbated by the inability of the private sector to assist, as indicated by a survey conducted by the Real Estate Institute of Australia this year which showed a high demand for private rental housing and flats, with low vacancy rates. The main features of the 1978-79 Commonwealth-State Housing Agreement and the present Bill are statements of principle, but the basic requirement of adequate funds has been ignored.
The statements by this Government that its policy is directed at giving scope for a wide range of innovations in policy and, in the words of the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) 'The current Bill increases the flexibility given to the States and continues the process of ensuring that assistance is directed to people in need' are absolute nonsense as those so called statements of principle are stated simultaneously with drastic cutbacks in funding. This, as was stated so clearly by the honourable member for Reid, is the core of the problem. The lack of appreciation by this Government of the grave problems in welfare housing is clearly demonstrated from the Victorian Liberal Party document 'Federalism-Is It Working?' There are two particularly relevant quotes from that document. Firstly, if one supports the need for restraint on government expenditure there is still the question why housing has been singled out to

receive such an unprecedented attack. Secondly, public housing is the only category to have suffered a fall in Commonwealth outlays in real terms. It is difficult to understand why this should occur, as housing directly stimulates the economy and provides capital works for the private sector. The Government has been slated in dramatic terms by a Victorian government of its own failed, outdated philosophy.
The facts must be spelled out in a plea to the Government to reverse its policy. On 26 March this year figures released by the Government showed that applications for rental accommodation in Australia through State authorities totalled 83,391 at 31 December 1980. This represented an increase of 11,479 over the 1978 figure and 10,927 over the 1979 figure. According to the Government's figures, the waiting list in Victoria was 13,861-an increase of 1,805 over the 1979 figure. To be more specific on the Victorian position, in 1980 the Westernport Regional Housing Council prepared a working paper for the Ministry of Housing. This research showed that in the Westernport area rental stocks of houses and flats totalled 2,045 for a population of 388,000-that is, rental houses and fiats as a percentage of population was 0.5 per cent. The consequences for the Westernport area have been devastating. The waiting list for 17 of 22 estates with family dwellings is indefinite; for two estates, three years; for a further two estates, one year; and the other estate is reserved for armed services. The waiting lists for 21 of 27 estates with elderly persons' dwellings are indefinite; for three estates, four years, and for the further three estates, 12 to 18 months.
This tragic situation is reflected in other specific areas in Victoria such as the Ministry of Housing figures for the Dandenong district where over 3,000 people are waiting for home purchase. There is a 4-year to 5-year wait for rental of a twobedroomed home, over 3 years for a threebedroomed home and 4 years to 5 years for a ground floor fiat. In April 1981 the Westernport Youth Refuge prepared a further submission to the Ministry of Housing. This allowed that in all the municipalities in the Westernport region a desperate need existed for housing for young people on low incomes. The submission concentrated On the two areas of greatest need-Dandenong and Frankston. The term 'youth homelessness' covers a wide range of needs, and the age of young people involved ranges from 14 to 25 years. The submission by the Westernport Youth Refuge focused on young people seeking long term individual accommodation. The emergency housing service which the refuge has been

Housing Assistance Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2933

forced to provide is having great difficulty in achieving a quick turnover of residents because there is no long term housing for them to move into. Stays of 9 to 12 weeks are becoming common, and these young people are in need of secure, low cost housing.
The facts outlined by me tonight clearly show a desperate need for welfare housing in Victoria for young people, those in the middle age group and the elderly. Unfortunately, it is necessary to spell out to this Government some specific case studies which were conducted by the Westernport Youth Refuge to emphasise the tragedy over which this Government and its Liberal counterpart in Victoria presided. Some examples from the Westernport Youth Refuge submission are as follows: Firstly, a 19-year-old girl left Tasmania to look for work. She went to stay with a family friend in Lang Lang. This is only a temporary home and is unsuitable in any case because of lack of public transport and employment opportunities. She would like to live by herself in a one-bedroomed flat or in a bungalow. Her income is $53 a week, and as she has been paying board she has no savings. Secondly, a 16 year-old unemployed male, forced to leave home, is receiving $36 a week. His weekly budget does not and could not leave any money over for rent. Thirdly, a 23-yearold male, sleeping in railway stations around Melbourne for 2 weeks prior to residing at the youth refuge, was not receiving benefits at that time but now receives $53 a week. Fourthly, a 20-year-old female living in Sydney decided to travel to Melbourne with a male friend. She was living with him. She was forced to leave because he beat her. She went to the Commonwealth Employment Service and then to a local Community Youth Support Scheme and was referred to the refuge. She is planning to move into a two-bedroomed flat with a friend she met at the refuge. The refuge is being forced to pay the bond and the rent in advance because she cannot afford to do so. Fifthly, a 16-year-old female, forced to leave home after severe beatings from her father, arrived at the refuge with two black eyes and severe body bruising. Her sisters and mother had been subject to similar beatings over several years. She left home and her mother was sympathetic to the girl living in a private board situation. Once again, it is impossible for her to afford to do that.
These are actual case histories. Instead of the Federal Government making adequate finance available to the State of Victoria and the Victorian Government properly applying such funds, supplemented by its own funding, this human tragedy will continue. This must be the case with

reductions in funding by the Federal Government, coupled with the incompetence of the Victorian Government, clearly demonstrated by the appalling housing conditions in Victoria and highlighted by the commission of inquiry into the Victorian Housing Commission land deals. Instead of facing the issue, the Federal Minister for Housing and Construction and the State Minister have engaged in public abuse, the State Minister suggesting that the Federal Government should abolish the Department of Housing and sack its Minister. Not to be denied, the Federal Minister, in his reply, accused the State Minister of unwarranted Canberra bashing and unreasonable bellyaching. While the two Ministers engage in an argument about how much Victoria contributes to welfare housing, the people who are suffering are treated as pawns in the dispute. The shadow Minister for Housing in Victoria, Mr Ian Cathie, has requested both Ministers to justify their claims on the figures contributed by Victoria to welfare housing. Clearly both Ministers cannot be correct and the time has come to put up or shut up. We eagerly await their replies to Mr Cathie on this matter.
Never before in the history of Australia has a Federal government given so little to the crucial area of housing. This agreement fixes total Federal housing expenditure at $200m for each of the next five years. That figure, in itself, is bad enough but inflation will erode the real value of such funds year by year. The allocation of the same level of funding in money terms for each of the five years of the agreement guarantees that the real value of Commonwealth assistance will decline in each year of the agreement. Assuming an inflation rate of 10 per cent in each year of the Agreement, to maintain the same real value in 1985-86 the allocation for that year would have to be $292.8m. Under this agreement the amount from the Commonwealth in 1985-86 will remain at $200m, that is, only 70 per cent of what is needed to maintain real value using 1981-82 as the base year. In fact, by the next year some States will be paying more to the Commonwealth in capital and interest than they will receive. Not only are Australians facing insecurity in employment but, because of the policies of this mean and miserable Government, they are also faced with the tragedy of an appalling lack of housing for those most in need. The answer to the question 'Federalism-is It Working?' is clearly no. This is an issue which even the Victorian Liberal Party is capable of understanding. This Government has proved itself incapable of developing a housing policy.
