Housing Assistance Bill

It has been necessary to go through those matters very quickly because of the time allocated for this debate. Any government that seriously puts a Bill such as this before the House and then allows the amount of time to debate it that it has allowed, when one considers that the Bill involves a housing agreement for the next five years, is guilty of a contemptible performance and clearly indicates its contempt for people in desperate need in the area of housing.
Mr EWEN CAMERON (Indi) (10.34) The Housing Assistance Bill endorses the concept that welfare housing should promote the principles of energy conservation and concern for the every day needs of handicapped persons living in that housing. It is to these particular aspects of the legislation that I would like to address my remarks. In the International Year of Disabled Persons it is fitting indeed that this Government should pay particular attention to the needs of the disabled. There are 1.4 million people in Australia who suffer some form of disability affecting their mobility. Many of these people are occupants of welfare housing, and it is to this group that the innovations encouraged by this legislation are relevant. People who are handicapped but mobile, using wheelchairs or walking frames, or who suffer from bad arthritic conditions or other physical disabilities, have an important basic need for easy access to their home. Steps or stairs should not make access to the home or within the home too difficult. Ramps can be used instead of steps. This provision would also benefit mothers and children with pushers and prams. Rooms should be easily accessible, doors should be wide enough for wheelchairs to pass through and multiple level and high rise buildings should be discouraged when we are providing housing for the handicapped. People in wheelchairs or using walking frames require ready access to public transport. It is most important for public housing, whenever possible, to be conveniently sited near public transport and shopping centres.
Specific design characteristics in houses can greatly improve the standard of living for handicapped or disabled people. Light switches often create difficulties for the handicapped person. They should be placed at a suitable level and consist of a large rocker-type switch which could be operated by any appropriate limb, or part of a limb such as an elbow. Lower switch levels would also be useful for young children who find it very difficult to reach and operate the common type of light switch. Power points are usually located on skirting boards, making them very difficult to reach from a wheelchair, difficult for the aged to reach and almost impossible for a person using a

walking frame to reach. This difficulty could be easily overcome by raising power points to waist level, a simple and inexpensive change in any housing design. Cistern buttons and taps in bathrooms and toilets can cause difficulty for disabled people. They should be easily accessible and of careful design. Rails in bathrooms for the elderly and arthritic are a small additional attachment which could be installed in all houses.
The structural design of stoves is another area where simple changes could make a big difference to disabled persons. Usually stove controls are located at the rear of the stove and at a level which is too high for a person in a wheelchair to operate. The design and installation of a stove with controls at the front or side could overcome this inconvenience. Levers instead of knobs to operate doors would be a great advantage, especially for people disadvantaged by arthritis. A paralysed person is not usually able to turn a door knob; however, such a person is able to move a lever or push through a swinging door arrangement. Few of these design suggestions are expensive, and this legislation gives every encouragement for public housing authorities to implement them. Suitable access into and within houses will enable the disabled in our community to live in their own homes rather than face the possibility of having to move into special accommodation. Families will be able to remain together with full encouragement being given to disabled people to live as nearly as possible a normal life.
The second area in this Bill which I would like to discuss concerns measures to reduce energy consumption in housing. The second reading speech of the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) refers to the States having regard for energy savings which are appropriate in an energy conscious era. Public housing authorities have the responsibility for including cost efficient, energy-conscious features in their buildings or alternatively providing financial incentives to people who install energy saving devices. To facilitate this energy consciousness it is essential for occupants to be allowed maximum discretion over their own use of energy. They should be able to control thermostat settings or close off sections of the building which are not in use. That means that individual occupants would be responsible for the cost of energy consumed in their dwellings, thus encouraging more energy consciousness. This also supports the principle of the user paying for the services provided.
Siting and site planning are important parts of the total concept of energy conservation. Trees and plants should be planted to provide maximum shade during hot summer conditions

Housing Assistance Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2935

and minimum shade during cool winter conditions. To illustrate this point, deciduous vines planted on the northern and western sides of a house provide shade and protection from the sun during the hot summer whilst in the winter, with the loss of foliage, the house is allowed the benefit of the winter sun. In cool climates buildings should be set back from the northern site boundary so as to minimise overshadowing from trees, buildings and other structures on adjacent sites. Buildings designed for temperate climates should have the long elevation of the building facing in a northerly direction. Large glass areas facing north allow for the sun to warm the house in winter and can be protected from the hot summer sun by blinds or other means. It is desirable to plan living rooms and kitchens to be on the northern side so that they receive maximum benefit from the winter sun. The ideal house should have a large amount of its living area facing north with a great deal of natural light entering the house through large windows.
Insulation is another area of house construction which deserves special consideration by public housing authorities. Appropriate ceiling and wall insulation retains warmth in the winter and helps keep the house interior cool in the summer. It is an added cost in construction but the benefits in terms of energy savings far outweigh the initial cost. Floors constructed of concrete provide better insulation and allow for heat retention and, when covered with a heavy carpet or rug, play an important part in maintaining an even temperature throughout the dwelling. Windows fitted with opaque, closely woven, close fitting drapes or blinds help to retain heat in the house during the winter and provide protection from the summer sun.
Consideration should be given to the provision of cost and energy efficient appliances such as solar hot water units. If housing authorities avoid the installation of heating appliances which rely solely on petroleum based fuels cost savings are achieved and the limited reserves of fossil fuels are conserved. These few suggestions give some indication of the possibilities that exist to conserve energy if sensible planning is employed in housing design. All dwellings in Australia should be looked at in terms of conserving energy. It is particularly significant that the Commonwealth Government in its assistance to welfare housing through legislation places so much emphasis on the disadvantaged person and the conservation of energy. I commend the Bill to the House.
Mrs CHILD (Henty) (l0.42)-Over the last few Budgets the Federal Government has effected a redistribution of housing subsidies away from

lower income groups. It has introduced matching arrangements to cover up its withdrawal from public housing expenditure. If we look at the Federal allocations for public housing in all States over recent years we will see that $400m was allocated in 1977-78; $330m in 1978-79; and $260m in 1979-80. In 1980-81 the amount went up to $285m but in that year for the first time an amount for the Northern Territory was included. In 1981-82 we see a further reduction to $200m. Worse still, we are debating a 5-year agreement. The Parliament should debate this important issue annually and should provide a forward commitment of real funds so that the States can plan their housing programs instead of having this stop-go racket. This legislation makes a mockery of the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh), who in his second reading speech on the 1981 Housing Assistance Bill said:
OUf policy is to ensure that every household in Australia is able to obtain adequate housing within its capacity to pay.
This takes one back to 1977 when the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) stated:
Once the election is over, we will start to move to the consummation of a 2 per cent reduction in interest rates-and that means about $500 a year to someone on an average home loan.
We are aware of what happened to interest rates. They continue to go up. Our expectation in respect of a policy which will ensure that every household in Australia obtains adequate housing is certainly going down.
The poverty inquiry established that 25 per cent of one's income should be the maximum allowed for rent in order for families to meet their costs adequately. What is there in the 1981 Bill of the Minister for Housing and Construction which will help families to overcome the problem of high rents, high interest and the resultant misery from inadequate housing? Where is the provision for families on low incomes to buy their own home? I tell the Minister for his information that the average cost of renting a housing in the Melbourne surburbs of Northcote, Blackburn, Windsor, Oakleigh, Frankston-in fact every suburb in Melbourne; I would think this situation is probably duplicated throughout every State in Australia-is between $60 and $70 a week. So families are paying between 45 per cent and 52 per cent of their income in rent in the private sector, not the 25 per cent as recommended by Professor Henderson. It is estimated by Shelter, a group in Victoria, that there are at least three times as many households with incomes below the poverty line living in private rented houses as there are in total Victoria Housing Commission rental dwellings. The $200m provided in this Bill

2936 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Housing Assistance Bill

will not do anything even to touch this tremendous gap. There is a crisis in public housing in Victoria which I am sure applies in other States. Public housing rental units in Victoria in 1980 numbered just over 40,000. In the same period Department of Social Security figures showed that 95,241 Victorian pensioners were receiving the $5 a week supplementary rent allowanceand that is a pitiful amount. A further 89,960 people were benefit recipients not eligible for rent assistance but obviously on very low incomes. Many pensioners and beneficiaries therefore must be renting in the private sector, which further indicates that public rental houses and fiats are in desperately short supply. All in all, about 145,201 people need low cost housing in Victoria alone. A real increase was needed to subsidise low cost housing. Once again, the Government has opted out of the public sector as it does consistently.
Those renting in the private sector who are already on or below the poverty line are further disadvantaged by the high rents. There are no figures available on the number of families on low wages who are also in need of low rent housing. This is probably the greatest social problem of the 1980s. The demand for low cost housing is increasing at a time when the Federal Government is trying to get out from under all or any responsibility to the people of Australia, not just in housing but in health and education as well. It is doing so under the guise of fine words-I quote the Minister-such as:
The States will have complete freedom of choice in allocating all untied advances and grants between home purchase and rental housing assistance.
What choice do they have when they are starved of money? What hypocrisy the Minister speaks. On page 3 of the Minister's second reading speech we see that money for Aboriginal housing will be integrated into the Commonwealth-State Housing Agreement. We all know that this is just one more way in which the Federal Government opts out of its responsibilities. The attitude of the Federal Government is: Pass it over to the States and forget it. Perhaps the Minister hopes the problem will go away just as the Government hopes that the problems of community health centres, the women's refuges and the rape crisis centres will go away. I support the amendment moved by the honourable member for Reid (Mr Dren) which states that the Commonwealth must take direct responsibility for the housing of Aboriginal people. No one can say that this Minister lacks humour. On the second page of his second reading speech he said:
. the design, style and siting of public housing will . . . reflect the needs of the handicapped. This

objective is particularly appropriate in this International Year of Disabled Persons . . .
Does the Minister not know that disabled persons are still waiting for the $700,000 that the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar) has been talking about for the past nine months? This amount is to be used, among other things, to modify their homes. They know, as we on this side of the House know, that they cannot have any faith in promises from this Government. Government members seem to feel that if they mention the International Year of Disabled Persons they have done their share. It is all words and not action.
Low and moderate income earners in Victoria are facing a housing crisis and high rents are forcing many of them into financial difficulties. This is exacerbated by the growing incidence of homelessness among youth. The Victorian Housing Commission is in need of substantial new allocations of funds to assist the large numbers of people who are unable to find adequate accommodation in the private rental market. I would also say it is in need of a new Minister and some new administration so that the funds that are available can be allocated where they are needed and not disbursed on such scandals as we had over the land deals some years ago.
The present waiting list for public housing is long. Yet there is a great underestimation of the need. Housing officers can attest to the great number of people in dire housing need who do not apply to the Commission because the housing that is available is not appropriate to their needs, be-' cause of the long delays in obtaining it, because of the enormous distances that would be involved in travelling to and from work daily or because mostly high rise housing is available. Who wants to bring up one's children in high rise fiats? Because of the severe Budget cuts, the amount of housing provided over past years has been greatly reduced. The Budget cuts must be reversed if the urgent housing needs of the people are to be met. In fact, I suggest that the public housing sector has to be stabilised and given, by this Government, the type of attention it got during the three years of the Whitlam Government, when the needs of the people on low incomes and the dreams of the people of Australia of owning their own homes were recognised by a government which believed in Australia for the people.
Mr WEST (Cunningham) (lO.SO)-Since the Second World War the Federal Government has awarded money to the States for welfare housing. In 1978 we saw an agreement which was to last for three years. That agreement had a number of anti-social features. The agreement which has been put forward in 1981 is even worse than the

Housing Assistance Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2937

1978 agreement, and it is expected to last another five years. The financial cuts imposed upon the States in the area of welfare housing are savage and unprincipled. They are in line with the general anti-social attack of this Government upon the poor and upon people who are not able to afford private housing. Since 1975, when $371m was made available, the situation has deteriorated. Last year only $276m was made available. The Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) has now told us that the Government will make available only $200m as a base payment. The situation is even worse when we take into account the effects of inflation and repayments from the States to the Federal Government.
Firstly, let us look at the effects of inflation. When we convert to 1981 prices, we find a steady and constant deterioration in Federal funds to the States for public housing. In 1975, $584m was provided. I will not read out all the figures, but in each year, in 1981 prices, there was a fall in real terms. In 1978, only $411m was provided; in 1979, $296m was provided; in 1980, $276m was provided. The base payment next financial year will be $200m. The Minister has said that interest rates, repayment provisions and terms are fairly generous. An interest rate of 4.5 per cent over a period of 53 years in low interest advances for rental assistance, home purchase assistance, and now a rental subsidy in the private sector is fairly generous. Of course, the Minister neglected to say that those terms should be generous. Nevertheless, even though the terms are generous, the repayment provisions affect the situation adversely. As Federal assistance declines in monetary terms, the responsibility of the States to repay previous loans increases. In 1974, $515m was made available to the States on a net basis. The amount fell to $191m in 1978. It decreased further to the extremely meagre sum of $75.7m in 1980. The breakdown of the figure for 1980 indicates that the total payment was $276m, repayment for advances was $35.6m, repayment of interest was $164.7m, and total repayments were a massive $200m. In effect, only $75.7m out of the sum of $276m was made available to the States.
Mr Milton-Shameful.
Mr WEST-It is shameful, I agree. In 1974 total repayments were only 27 per cent of the total made available to the States and 73 per cent was actually spent in the area of public housing. Today that position has exactly reversed. Of the payments made to the States, 73 per cent has to be repaid to finance previous debts. The Minister said that this year from the base payment of $200m $54m will be available for disadvantaged

persons such as pensioners and Aboriginals and $146m will be available for housing assistance fqr other people. That is the sum that has to be repaid.
I refer to the Aboriginal situation. The Minister said in his second reading speech that, from now, grants to the States for Aboriginal housing will be integrated into the Commonwealth-State Housing Agreement. We learn that $54m has been earmarked for Aboriginals, pensioners and other disadvantaged people. I put on the record, as someone who went into this area fairly deeply when I had shadow ministerial responsibility, that the Department of Aboriginal Affairs last year made $22m available for grants-in-aid to Aboriginal housing associations, $10m in State grants and $7.3m for Aboriginal hostels, a total of $39.3n:i. This was a cut of 43 per cent in real terms since 1975-76, but a further $22.1m was earmarked for Aboriginal housing from the general welfare housing grant. This year what will the position be? I would like an answer from the Minister, who said that he would integrate State grants for Aboriginal housing into the general welfare hous" ing grants. I repeat that last year $lOm in State grants and $22m in welfare housing grants wa:;; made available for Aboriginal housing, a total of $32m, and $31m was made available for pensioners. In 1980-81 a total of $63m was made available to the States for housing for Aboriginals and pensioners. Next financial year the figure will be $54m. Quite frankly, it needs to be at least $70m to be commensurate in real terms with the allocation made last year. I am very puzzled on this point. The Government needs to increase considerably the amount made available in top-up payments.
I hope I have demonstrated that these payments are completely unacceptable. The cuts are too great. They are despicably mean and they will contribute to unemployment. The Government acts in this way, firstly, for ideological reasons. It is committed to the so-called private sector. Secondly, the Government wishes to make cut-backs in Federal payments for the provision of social infrastructure to allow for the expansion of public financing of State infrastructure to provide the ports, power stations, railway lines, gas pipelines and so on to service the so-called resource development boom. Power stations alone in New South Wales and Victoria are supposed to require $10 billion, according to Commonwealth estimates, over the next seven or eight years. In New South Wales $2.7 billion is needed for three power stations in the Hunter Valley and in Victoria $3.2 billion is needed for the Loy Yang A and B power stations over the next five years. These massive

2938 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Housing Assistance Bill

sums will be borrowed mostly through the Loan Council. Yet this year, after taking repayments into account, the States will be given less than $70m to spend on public housing.
Let me refer to the position in boom centres like Gladstone. In Gladstone now 8,000 people out of a total of 24,000 people are living in caravans. New parks are booked out even before they are completed. I was in Gladstone recently. People are paying private residents to park their vans in backyards. No wonder that Housing Commission construction has fallen in this terrible

situation. For example, the cuts that have occurred, despite the need in places like Gladstone, must be taken into account. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a table prepared by the Parliamentary Library showing the number of housing commission houses and fiats completed by State housing authorities year by year and State by State since 1950.

Mr WESrI thank the House. Honourable members will see that the percentage of State housing commission dwelling completions as a percentage of total dwellings completed has fallen from 22 per cent in 1955 to 5.2 per cent in 1980. That is in total. In several States the position is far worse. As a percentage of total completions of houses and fiats, in Queensland the completion rate by the Housing Commission was only 1.5 per
cent. It was 4.5 per cent in New South Wales and 4.5 per cent in Victoria. It is no wonder that

80,000 families are on waiting lists in Australia for housing commission accommodation, despite the artifically reduced nature of the list due to the imposition of a severe means test for eligibility. People practically have to be pauperised before they are accepted for inclusion on the housing commission lists. There are 40,000 such people in New South Wales, including 1,700 people in my own electorate of Cunningham in the Illawarra. In New South Wales in 1980 there were only

Housing Assistance Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2939

1,900 completions which was 2.3 per cent of existing total stock in New South Wales. In 1979-80 18,000 people were admitted to the waiting list for homes and 9,800 people were allocated dwellings. Of course, many of these dwellings had already been erected for many years. There is little chance that these homeless and poorly housed people will get public housing or housing in the private sector under this Government.
Land and construction costs in the private sector are just too high in a situation of declining Federal finance to the States for public housing. In Sydney and my own town of Wollongong it is not possible even to look at a place for less than $70,000 or $80,000. Even a middle or high-middle income earner cannot afford that amount.
Mr MeVeigh--That is a State Government responsibility.
Mr WEST-It is this Government's responsibility. It is not only the State Government's responsibility but also the Federal Government's responsibility. What chance have these people got?
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member will address the Chair.
Mr WEST-Thank you, Mr Deputy Speaker. Perhaps the Minister will restrain himself from making inane interjections. What chance have people got of saving $80,000 for a modest threebedroom house, with interest rates from banks at 11 per cent or 12 per cent and interest rates for building society loans at 12 per cent to 13 per cent? People who are lucky enough to be able to afford and get the savings bank loan would have to pay $240 a month. With the high interest rates under this coalition on a loan of $30,000 a person would have to pay $240 a month. What chance have these people got? They would have to earn at least $1,000 a month before they qualified or were considered for a loan.
I want to mention some further matters and then I will conclude. I do not want to take up the time of the House for too much longer. I think I am entitled to raise these points seeing that it will be another five weary years before the Housing Agreement comes before the House again.
Mr Uren-We will do something about it in 1983.
Mr WEST--l am assured by my colleague the shadow Minister that we will do something about this tatty agreement in 1983 as a matter of priority. There are several matters in the Agreement itself that we are dissatisfied with. Firstly, I refer to the policy of market rents, with the system of special rebates. I ask: Who determines the market

that the Government forces the States to adhere to? The real estate agents? The shortage of housing itself is the main contributing factor. In effect this Government is contributing to the housing shortage by horrendous cuts in the public housing sector. When rents go up as a result of these cuts the Government demands that the States charge the new thus induced market rents. The Government then further allows the States in this Agreement to subsidise people forced to rent at these exorbitant rentals. It will finish up that not one single house will be built in the States. That further contributes to unemployment.
The Opposition urges that the amendment be passed forthwith by this House to ensure that adequate funds are allocated to the States for public housing including, and not forgetting, housing for pensioners and that the Commonwealth accept its responsibility to fund Aboriginal housing by Federal grants to Aboriginal housing associations and not to bureaucratic State organisations which do not know what the Aboriginal people want. In conclusion we demand that this Bill be withdrawn. This absolutely horrific Bill starves the States in the great area of public housing. The Opposition cannot tolerate the fact that this Agreement is to serve the needs of the States for the next five years. The Bill should be immediately withdrawn and redrafted to take into account those sentiments expressed by my colleague the shadow Minister.
Mr MILTON (La Trobe) (11.7)-In supporting the amendment to the Housing Assistance Bill moved by the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren) I wish to draw attention to some matters which are of grave concern in my electorate and which are reflected in most other electorates in the outer suburbs of the capital cities of Australia. I have not much time so--
Mr Howe-It is outrageous too. It is a very important measure.
Mr MILTON-Indeed, as the honourable member said, the situation is outrageous. I have not time to go into the figures which I would like to have presented to the House. There has been a drastic cut in funds for housing resulting in appalling consequences for homeless people in the outer eastern region of Melbourne. Community workers in the region are experiencing great difficulties in satisfying the demands for accommodation, especially from single parent families and homeless youth. High rents and the limited amount of private accommodation available for rent in the region are forcing many low and moderate income earners into financial and housing difficulties. For unemployed youth the position is

2940 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Housing Assistance Bill

even more desperate. The chances of obtaining fiats in this area for all of these people are virtually nil. Most people seeking accommodation must look for houses to rent. This requires a $400 to $500 bond plus a $90 deposit for the electricity connection. For the young unemployed living on a pittance of $36 a week unemployment benefit such accommodation is completely unobtainable. I would like to refer to an example to demonstrate such circumstances, but that would take too long.
In consequence of these appalling policies the homeless are directing their anger against their local councils whereas their real enemies are the Liberal controlled State and Federal governments. Even the State housing Minister in Victoria has been so frustrated by the Commonwealth housing policy that he has called for the resignation of the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh). The Minister's only answer to the State housing Minister has been to suggest that the Victorian Government is ignoring its responsibilities to the poor and the needy. I agree with the Minister for Housing and Construction that the Liberal State Government of Victoria has placed the poor and needy at the bottom of its list of priorities. However, the fact cannot be escaped that the reduction in Commonwealth funds provided for welfare housing indicates that the Federal Government has also completely disregarded the problems of the homeless. The Age editorial of 19 May aptly described the recent slanging match between the Commonwealth and the Victorian housing Ministers. It stated:
Sometimes both sides of an argument are clearly wrong.
What is clear is that the 'user pays' philosophy of both Liberal led governments shows a callous disregard for poor and needy people. Another matter I wish to draw to the attention of the House tonight is the desperate financial situation which many home buyers in my electorate are facing. Over the past 15 months there has been a one per cent rise in interest rates, resulting in an increase of $60 a month in average mortgage payments for home buyers. Housing has been pushed into the commercial field. In consequence interest rates are rapidly escalating as financial institutions battle to win money from investors. The high costs of mortgage repayments have been forced on to the public by the Government's deregulation of bank interest rates. Four out of five residents in my electorate own or are purchasing their homes with the aid of mortgage agreements. Many have both first and second mortgages, the second mortgage being subject to very high interest rates. For some of my constituents there

has been a 25 per cent increase in mortgage repayments over the past 15 months. Their incomes are not sufficient to meet the additional costs. In consequence, they have been forced to place their homes on the market for sale. The responsibility for such desperate measures can be laid directly at the door of the Federal Government as a result of its irresponsible economic policies. Housing has to compete with resource projects and capital works programs for funds. There is a clear case for the introduction of a Commonwealth funded interest subsidy scheme for lower income familities.
Finally, I draw attention to the plight of women and children who suddenly find themselves homeless because of a breakup of marital relationships. In the vicinity of my electorate are located two women's refuges which carry out an excellent job in providing temporary accommodation for such homeless families. Unfortunately, there is virtually no welfare housing in the eastern suburbs of Melbourne. Because of their economic circumstances these women have to seek permanent accommodation through the Housing Commission. Thus, even if they are successful in reaching the top of the waiting list, they must then accept a high rise fiat, which social workers all agree is totally unsuitable for small children. What these homeless families need are welfare houses located within the outer suburban community. Only in such locations will they stand a chance of resuming a normal and meaningful life. As the amendment indicates, the Federal Government has a responsibility to provide sufficient funds to the States to ensure that the housing needs of these broken families are met.
While I am on my feet I would like to express my unqualified support for the demonstrations in King's Hall by representatives of the women's refuges. We are a people's House and this Government is treating--
Mr Ian Robinson-Shame on you.
Mr MILTON-It is shameful all right. We are a people's House.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member is no longer being relevant to the Bill. I ask him to address himself to the Bill.
Mr MILTON-This is a people's House. This Government is treating the homeless people with a callous unprecedented indifference. It is fitting that their protest should be heard inside this chamber. I strongly support the proposed amendment, which if adopted will ensure that help is provided for home buyers on low incomes and the homeless in their present desperate circumstances.

ENVIRONMENT PROTECTION (SEA DUMPING) BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 14 May, on motion by MrThomson:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr WEST (Cunningham) (11.38)-The Opposition, whilst believing that there must be some amendments, welcomes the introduction of the Environment Protection (Sea Dumping) Bill to ratify the 1972 Convention on the Prevention of Marine Pollution by Dumping of Wastes and Other Matter. However, we question why it has taken eight years to ratify the decisions which Australia signed in 1973. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard the definition of 'dumping' contained in that Convention.
Leave granted.
The definition read as jollows-
For the purposes of this Convention: 1. (a) "Dumping" means:
(i) any deliberate disposal at sea of wastes or other matter from vessels, aircraft, platforms or other man-made structures at sea;
(ii) any deliberate disposal at sea of vessels, aircraft, platforms or other man-made structures
at sea.
(b) "Dumping" does riot include:

(i) the disposal at sea of wastes or other matter incidental to, or derived from the normal operations of vessels, aircraft, platforms, or other man-made structures at sea and their equipment, other than wastes or other matter transported by or to vessels, aircraft, platforms or other man-made structures at sea, operating for the purpose of disposal of such matter or derived from.the treatment of such wastes or other matter on such vessels, aircraft, platforms or structures;
(ii) placement of matter for a purpose other than the mere disposal thereof, provided that such placement is not contrary to the aims of this Convention.
Mr WEST-I thank the House. A resolution made at the third consultation meeting in 1978 stated that incineration at sea was an interim measure for waste disposal pending development of environmentally better solutions and that the intention of the adoption of mandatory provisions for the control of incineration at sea is not to increase the amount of wastes incinerated at sea for which there are practical land-based methods. This point should be extended to all methods of waste disposal at sea. It is not the ideal receptacle for all wastes. We must examine alternative waste disposal methods, increased recycling of wastes, and more efficient resource utilisation. Whilst there have not been many horror stories on the results of dumping of toxic chemicals into the sea from ships, there have been many examples of shore-based dumping into the ocean, most notable being the dumping of mercury into the sea off Minamata Bay in Japan. The residents ate fish and other marine life that was heavily contaminated with mercury. A~ a result the acute mercury poisoning from which they suffered became known as Minamata Disease. By April 1977, 1,076 residents of Minamata were certified victims of the disease and 3,000 others were awaiting examination.
Needless to say mercury, as well as cadmiumanother environmental poison which became publicly prominent from massive health problems in Japanese waters-are both in Annex I and are totally prohibited from being disposed of in the ocean. Other metals such as arsenic and zinc are in Annex II and will require special permits for dumping. The zinc contaminated oysters of the Derwent Estuary in Tasmania gained notoriety early in the 1970s when one was analysed and found to contain 26,000 parts per million of zinc. It should be noted that the Electrolytic Zinc Co. of (Australasia) Ltd is the only company in Australia regularly to dump wastes into the sea-in 1,500 metres of water south of Hobart to be precise. It would like to move, no doubt, to shallower waters for cost reasons. Toxicity tests, which are being carried out for that company on

2944 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Environment Protection (Sea Dumping) Bill

its wastes, are being done in the United States, on an American species of fish, not those normally found in the dump area. Any research carried out to determine the suitability of the application for dumping permits should be relevant to Australian conditions.
This legislation will apply to all vessels, platforms and planes in Australian waters and to all Australian vessels and planes in any part of the sea. Of course the definition 'Australian waters' means territorial seas, that is, three miles seawards from a given base line. Dumping or incineration of certain substances is prohibited whilst the disposal of other toxic materials needs a special permit and disposal of an other types of material requires a general dumping permit. I think it is important that we get details of the prohibited list on the record. I ask for permission of the House for Annex I of the Convention, which bans dumping of substances such as mercury, cadmium and high level radioactive wastes into the sea, to be incorporated in Hansard.
Leave granted.
The Annex read as follows-
ANNEX I
1. Organohalogen compounds.
2. Mercury and mercury compounds.
3. Cadmium and cadmium compounds.
4. Persistent plastics and other persistent synthetic materials, for example, netting and ropes, which may float or may remain in suspension in the sea in such a manner as to interfere materially with fishing, navigation or other legitimate uses of the sea.
5. Crude oil, fuel oil, heavy diesel oil, and lubricating oils, hydraulic fluids, and any mixtures containing any of these, taken on board for the purpose of dumping.
6. High-level radio-active wastes or other high-level radioactive matter, defined on public health, biological or other grounds, by the competent international body in this field, at present the International Atomic Energy Agency, as unsuitable for dumping at sea.
7. Materials in whatever form (e.g. solids, liquids, semiliquids, gases or in a living state) produced for biological and chemical warefare.
8. The preceding paragraphs of this Annex do not apply to substances which are rapidly rendered harmless by physical, chemical or biological processes in the sea provided they do not:
(i) make edible marine organisms unpalatable, or
(ii) endanger human health or that of domestic animals.
The consultative procedure provided for under Article XIV should be followed by a Party if there is doubt about the harmlessness of the substance.
9. This Annex does not apply to wastes or other materials (e.g. sewage sludges and dredged spoils) containing the matters referred to in paragraphs 1-5 above as trace contaminants. Such wastes shall be subject to the provisions of Annexes IT and III as appropriate.

Mr WEST-I thank the House. A second list requiring special permits includes substances such as arsenic, lead, zinc and so on and low level radioactive wastes. I seek the leave of the House for Annex II to be incorporated in Hansard.

The Convention referred to in this Bill allow the dumping into the sea of materials such as arsenic and low and medium level radioactive wastes. We do not and will not accept this practice.

complicated when I get into it. Clause 9 provides for the States to pass mirror legislation if they so wish and to assume full responsibility for enforcing the laws of an international convention. Mr WEST-What I was trying to doMr Ruddock-Incorporate the whole lot. That way the speech will not be so long. Mr WEST-If the honourable member opposite wants to stop here all night I will comply with exactly what he is saying and go through the whole business again clause by clause in the Committee stage. I am concerned at a situation which excludes coverage of the disposal of wastes from off-shore mining operations. Whilst the Petroleum (Submerged Lands-Miscellaneous Amendments) Bill and Minerals (Submerged Lands) Bill purport to have a common mining

the owner and the person in charge of the vessel, aircraft or platform and the owner of the wastes or other matter are each guilty of an offence against this section.
The effect of clauses lO, 11, 12 and so on is to require foreign ships registered with nations which are signatory to the Convention to acquire a permit from the Australian designated authority to dump in Australian waters; that is, the territorial sea for 3 miles. But the Bill only requires those ships to obtain a permit from their own designated authority before dumping on the Australian continental shelf outside the 3-mile territorial sea. Similarly, because of what is set out in clauses 10 and 11, foreign ships-I think the Minister for

2946 REPRESENTATIVES 2 June 1981

Environment Protection (Sea Dumping) Bill

Home Affairs and Environment (Mr Wilson) will understand this-registered with nations not party to this Convention must, under the Convention rules, obtain an Australian permit to dump in our territorial sea. But, they are not under any control on the continental shelf outside the 3-mile zone. It is essential that all acts of dumpingwastes into the waters of the Australian continental shelf are regulated by Australian law. There is adequate precedent for Australia to assert its jurisdiction over dumping above the continental shelf. In 1972 at the London dumping convention Australia and New Zealand issued a joint statement which, in part, said:
It is the understanding of Australia and New Zealand that this Convention in no way detracts from the rights of a coastal State to take action in areas under its jurisdiction, and in particular from the rights of the coastal State to take action for the purpose of protecting the resources of its continental shelf.
We have asserted our sovereign rights over the continental shelf and have declared the 200-mile wide fishing zone. Article VII (1) 1 (c) of the Convention was intended to provide for coastal state jurisdiction in a zone adjacent to the coast. At the time, agreement on the extent of this zone could not be reached but Article 13 in the Convention on the Law of the Sea referred to this point. Articles 210 (5) and 216 (1) (a) of the draft Law of the Sea Convention, recognise the right of a coastal State to control dumping in waters above its continental shelf. Article 210 (5) states:
Dumping within the territorial sea and the exclusive economic zone or onto the continental shelf shall not be carried out without the express prior approval of the coastal State, which has the right to permit, regulate and control such dumping after due consideration of the matter with other States which by reason of their geographical situation may be adversely affected thereby.
Article 216 (1) states:
Laws and regulations adopted in accordance with this Convention and appJicable international rules and standards established through competent organisations or diplomatic conference for the prevention, reduction and control of pollution of the marine environment from dumping shall be enforced~
(a) by the coastal State with regard to dumping within its territorial sea or its exclusive economic zone or onto its continental shelf.
This is not a contentious issue. We have ;rlways argued strongly for this right. The delay in finalising the Law of the Sea Convention, directly attributable to the delaying tactics of the new United States Reagan Administration, should not prevent Australia from asserting jurisdiction over dumping on our continental shelf. It is a matter clearly consistent with the proposed Law of the Sea Convention as outlined in the articles I have read to this House. Only last week the Australian Government asserted this jurisdiction in making

its regulations regarding the Capricornia section of the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park. The regulations prohibit the disposal of wastes in the sea from ships without permission from the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Authority in the Capricornia section, both within and beyond the territorial sea. Clearly the Government needs to protect our fisheries as well as the seabed, which we claim to be Australia's, from pollution resulting from sea dumping. There is no justification for not asserting jurisdiction over all dumping on Australia's continental shelf.
The Government projects a curious inconsistency when implementing legislation to control pollution of the sea. Its recent Protection of the Sea (Discharge of Oil from Ships) Bill bans oil discharge within 50 miles from land and then limits discharges to 60 litres per mile. Yet, under last week's amendments to the Petroleum (Submerged Lands) Act, oil exploration and drilling will be allowed 30 miles from the Great Barrier Reef, and probably a lot closer if the Minister for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony) has his way. Under this legislation the Government will allow foreign ships to discharge arsenic, zinc, radioactive wastes and so on onto our continental shelf. So long as it is further than 3 miles from Australian shores it can be done, in the case of nonsignatory nations, without a permit from anyone and, in the case of fellow signatory nations, with a permit from their own designated authority. This Bill must be amended to extend Australian jurisdiction over all dumping at sea to the edge of our continental shelf.
I want now to refer to a provision in this Bill which deals with the, dumping of radioactive materials into the sea. There should be no such sea dumping of nuclear wastes and the relevant clause should be replaced with another clause which prohibits sea dumping of low and medium level radioactive waste as if they were listed in the same category as those substances in Annex 1 of the Convention. The Australian Labor Party is opposed to the dumping of any radioactive material into the world's oceans. This Government had a chance to prohibit this but it is showing its true colours andlooking to the day when it will bring nuclear power plants and enrichment plants to Australia and will be able to drop the consequent radioactive rubbish into the seas off Australia. Article IV sub-section 3 of the Conventionon the prevention of Marine Pollution by Dumping of W!}stes and Other Matter states:
No provision of this Convention is to be interpreted as preventing a Contracting Party from prohibiting, insofar as that Party is concerned, the dumping of wastes or other matter not mentioned in Annex I. That party shall notify such measures to the Organisation.

Environment Protection (Sea Dumping) Bill

2 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2947

So, what I propose in the amendments I am foreshadowing are entirely feasible and acceptable to all nations that are signatories to this Convention. At present, only high level radioactive wastes, usually those wastes which come from the reactor cone or from the reprocessing of spent fuel, are listed in Annex 1 and are prohibited from being dumped into the ocean. Are we to follow Japan's lead? Japan is preparing to dump 45,000 barrels of radioactive waste into the Pacific north of the Mariania Islands. American scientists claim deep ocean currents and migrating marine life could spread accumulated radioactivity to both Japan and Micronesia. Clearly Australia is taking this line in not opposing sea dumping of radioactive wastes. In a letter written in answer to my representation to the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, the Minister stated that Australian officials talking to the Japanese mission which came to Australia to placate fears about the dumping of radioactive wastes into the Pacific advised the mission that 'Australia considered it essential that the disposal of nuclear waste in any environment, and in particular the Pacific Ocean, should be undertaken only in compliance with the strictest safety measures and in full accordance with the relevant international agreements on the subject'. The Minister's letter is in answer to my representations regarding opposition to the dumping of Japanese nuclear waste in the seas off the Mariania Islands, and I seek leave to have it incorporated in Hansard.

Leave granted.

Mr WEST-I thank the House. For those honourable members who did not follow the information in the letter, it said that Australia feels it is all right for the Japanese to dump their wastes in the ocean. Of course, the Japanese have been inadvertently doing so, close to home, from their own nuclear reactor at Tsuruga. The United States of America has dumped radioactive cannisters in the north-east Pacific from California to the ~awaiian Islands and up to Canada, and is looking to the seabed as a permanent resting place for its decommissioned nuclear submarines. I ask all honourable members on the other side of the House to consider the implications of nuclear waste being dumped into the oceans of Australia. This legislation is not being amended to prohibit dumping of all nuclear wastes in the oceans. It may be that honourable members opposite will not be affected. But have no doubt that future generations will be affected and will pay the price of the negligence. There is as yet no solution to the permanent disposal of radioactive wastes. There may never be. Perhaps the solution is physically and scientifically impossible. The oceans, therefore, must not be used as an alternative dump.

The Opposition supports this legislation but ~ill move amendments at the Committee stage to Improve and strengthen the Bill. It is unacceptable for the Australian Government to propose legislation 'which will allow the dumping of radioactive wastes into the sea. People over the centuries have used the oceans of the world as a sink for their wastes and poisons. They are now realising that it is not a bottomless pit in which rubbish can be dumped without any ill effect. Over 3,500 million years ago the seas spawned the first living cell from which all life on earthdinosaurs, mammals and, in the last mere 500,000 years, man himself-has evolved. In the last 50 to 100 years the seas have suffered more pollution directly attributable to human folly than they have suffered in all of the previous four billion years of their existence. The convention measures are supportable. But Australians and this House can give a further lead to the world by passing the Opposition amendments in the Committee stage of this Bill tonight.

Clauses 1 to 3-by leave-taken together, and agreed to.
Clause 4 (Interpretation).
Mr WILSON (Sturt-Minister for Home Affairs and Environment) (12.3 a.m.)-I move:
(\) Clause 4, page 2, lines 27 to 33, omit the definition of "Australian waters", substitute the following definition:
" 'Australian waters' means-
(a) the territorial sea of Australia and any sea that is on the landward side of the territorial sea of Australia, other than any part of the sea that is within the limits of a State or of the Northern Territory; or
(b) the territorial sea of an external Territory and any sea that is on the landward side of that territorial sea,
and includes any area of sea that is declared by the regulations to be included in Australian waters for the purposes of this Act;".
A new definition of 'Australian waters' is provided for in the legislation. The seaward side of the territorial sea of Australia or of an external territory may be declared by regulation to be Australian waters for the purposes of the Bill. I want to point out that some reference was made in the second reading debate to the fact that certain dumping could take place beyond the outer boundary of the territorial sea. Australian vessels and aircraft which might seek to dump in or over the sea anywhere in the world will come under the legislation. The legislation also controls out to the outer edge of the continental shelf the dumping from an Australian platform.
The legislation restricts the capacity of foreign vessels to dump and requires the operation of this legislation out to the outer boundary of the territorial sea. Some concern was expressed by the Opposition during the second reading stage of the Bill about the fact that foreign vessels could dump between the outer boundary of the territorial sea and the outer edge of the continental shelf. Firstly, I want to draw the attention of the Committee to the fact that the ships and aircraft of the 41 countries that are parties to the London dumping convention would not be permitted to dump in that area unless they had the permission of their own governments under legislation which complied with the convention. It is understood that under the present accepted international law,

nations which are parties to the London dumping convention are required to legislate in the form of the Bill which is now before the Committee.
There could come a time when the circumstances will change and, as a result of very difficult negotiations, a new international law of the sea regime will operate. Recognising that circumstances may change, the Government proposes that a new definition be added to the Bill as originally introduced so that the Bill may be expanded by regulation to cover the dumping operations of foreign vessels and aircraft in the area between the outer edge of the territorial sea and the outer edge of the continental shelf. I am aware that the Opposition is proposing to move an amendment under which the legislation would cover right out to the outer edge of the continental shelf. I can indicate at this stage that the Government is not prepared to accept that amendment. However, it is responding to the concern that has been expressed by changing the definition in the Bill to enable adjustment to be made by regulation to extend at some later stage the operation of the legislation beyond the edge of the territorial sea in relation to foreign vessels and aircraft.
Amendment agreed to.
Dr JENKINS (Scullin) (12.8 a.m.)-Clause 4 deals with a number of definitions which clearly identify what this Bill applies to. Perhaps one of the most important of these is the description of the convention which Australia is implementing. We welcome the ratification of another international convention. This gives me the opportunity to make the criticism that one of the problems members of national parliaments have is keeping in touch with international conventions that have been ratified and implemented by their parliaments. The Executive Government holds such conventions pretty close to its collective chest. It is very difficult for us to know what response there has been to conventions, such as the one involved in this Bill, which are decided in international forums. One of the matters raised at a weekly meeting of an informal group of parliamentarians attending the United Nations, which I chaired, referred to conventions such as the one before us. Mention was made of the difficulties that members of national parliaments have in finding out what national governments were doing about conventions. Each year the United Nations produces an account of conventions which have been ratified and conventions which have yet to be ratified by various countries. The
list is a long one. The delays in ratification of conventions are long. Yet parliamentary consciousness of their existence is abysmally low and public awareness is practically nil. I think executive

Environment Protection (Sea Dumping) Bill 2 and 3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2949

governments have a responsibility to improve this situation.
However, the definitions in this clause illustrate some of the problems that my colleague the honourable member for Cunningham (Mr West) has outlined so well. The ships of countries not party to the Convention can dump substances prohibited by the Convention outside Australia's territorial sea. The Australian permit as it applies to foreign ships, whether their countries are party to the Convention or not, is necessary for dumping only in Australia's territorial waters. Further, included in the details is the permitting of the dumping of low level or medium level radioactive waste. One can query the adequacy of this provision and express concern at the possible concentration of such substances through the ecological chain, with adverse effects at the end of the chain or even earlier.
It has been necessary to define the types of enforcement and monitoring procedures that will operate. It is obvious from the definition of an inspector that, whilst clause 26 provides for persons to be appointed as inspectors, members of the police force will be used. We know that the extent of Australia's coastline and territorial seas causes many problems of inspection and monitoring in other areas such as drug smuggling and illegal immigration. My colleague the honourable member for St George (Mr Morrison) the other day raised the matter of defence. It is difficult to believe that the same sorts of problems will not arise here. I have no confidence that inspectors will be available in appropriate numbers or with appropriate facilities to enforce the proposals.
How will the permit which has to be granted operate? At the moment neither the Federal Government nor the State governments have effective information-gathering bodies giving a real picture of the volume or weight of the substances listed in Schedules 1 and 2 to this Bill; neither are their sources of production or storage details adequately known. We cannot provide adequately for the identification and disposal of substances on land in Australia, let alone at sea. What will the permit system involve? What will be its mechanics? What checking will there be? Will it be as simple to bypass the system as it is now to bypass the permit system for the disposal of hazardous wastes on land in the various States? We do not know how much has been done in regional efforts, which were referred to at the Stockholm Conference. Paragraph 86 (c) of the report of the Stockholm Conference refers to the need for regional efforts to bring all significant sources of pollution within the marine environment, including radioactive pollution from nuclear surface

ships and submarines and, in particular, in enclosed and semi-enclosed seas, under appropriate controls. It recommends the complete elimination of deliberate pollution of oil from ships. We have dealt with that matter before. How much progress has been made with this Convention in the regional sense, or are we acting in isolation? What negotiations have there been with countries such as New Zealand, the various Pacific Island nations, Papua New Guinea, Indonesia, and so on? Has that task been effectively carried out?
In the same area, there should be national control over land-based sources of marine pollution, to which my colleague referred. Do we place too much emphasis on the fact that we are an island continent? Have we effectively checked land base sources or do they hold the possibility of a concentration in an ecological chain that I referred to previously? Earlier I referred to the problem of information about the source of production of toxic substances, their storage in Australia and the lack of effective and up to date data. Yet in Stockholm so many years ago this matter was referred to quite bluntly and clearly. I quote paragraph 87 of the Stockholm conference report, which states:
It is recommended that Governments:
Provide to the UN, FAO and UNCTAD, as appropriate to the data-gathering activities of each, statistics on the production and use of toxic or dangerous substances that are potential marine pollutants, especially if they are persistent; . . .
From an inquiry that the Standing Committee on Environment and Conservation is carrying out, it is obvious that the data on the source of production, the volumes, the weights and where they are stored, are thoroughly inadequate; just as the method of by-passing the permit system is so simple in any State. To talk about the permit system here without adequate provision being made for inspectors or for monitoring, will completely beg the question. If we are to be fair dinkum about it we also have to consider that part of the London dumping convention is included in this Bill. Schedule 1, Annex III, C, 4 states:
The practical availability of alternative land-based methods of treatment, disposal or elimination, or of treatment to render the matter less harmful for dumping at sea.
I want to ensure that this will not be forgotten. It would be tragic if the implementation of the Convention which this Bill carries out was to be used as a device to excuse the Australian Government from action along these lines. Yet what mechanism exists to carry it out? In the matter of waste disposal in Australia, the cry is predominantly: 'Land fill, land fill, land fill'. If the waste is the least bit unpleasant, the Government says it must be dumped out of sight. State governments cannot

2950 REPRESENTATIVES 2 and 3 June 1981 EnvironmentProtection(SeaDumping)BilI

handle it. I remind honourable members of the controversy about using the Darghile forest as a toxic waste dump in Victoria, which was abandoned, and of the present public agitation over the toxic waste site suggested at Avalon.

Clause, as amended, agreed to. Clauses 5 to 8-by leave-taken together, and agreed to. Clause 9 (Declaration by Minister in relation to coastal waters of a State, et cetera) . Mr WEST (Cunningham) (12.18 a.m.)-The Opposition opposes clause 9. I do not want to take up too much time on this because in my speech at the second reading stage, I outlined in a broad sense many of the reasons for these amendments. Obviously, the reason why clause 9 should go is that the Government has been foolish enough to betray our national heritage and to betray the histor~c decision of the High Court of Australia, WhiCh ruled that the Commonwealth had power over the seas and what lay beneath them from the low water mark to the edge of the continental shelf. It followed that the Government made that historic sell out deal with the State Premiers, cul~ minating in the passing of that so-called off-shore package on 1 May last year. I am sorry to say that all State Premiers and governments collaborated against the High Court decision after 10 years of Australian Labor Party struggle on this matter. As a result of all that has transpired we have all the problems that now arise in Queensland in regard to the declaration of the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park. Mr Bjelke-Petersen and his Government can see that if they can only frustrate the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment (Mr Wilson) about the declaration of the Park and the Cairns section, the Coastal Waters (State Powers) Act 1980 will be declared. It provides for possibly at least 3 miles out to sea for a given base line. At that time the Minister will be in all sorts of trouble because the State Government will continue to say that the boundary of the Marine Park ought to be at least 3 miles out to sea rather than the low water mark so it can have control of all the coast along Queensland and perhaps allow oil drilling there. These are some of the problems that have arisen from that historic sellout of May Day 12 months ago. Just because the Government did that does not mean that every time legislation that has something to do with off-shore matters comes up in this House the Government has to continue this folly and say: 'Let us mirror the legislation

now even though it is about the ratification of an international convention on dumping at sea', Why the devil do we have to mirror that sort of thing which is clearly a national responsibility? Why do we say to the States: 'If you deal with the legislation we will get out of the area altogether and rely on you to police it'? The international convention of London on dumping at sea does not expect the Federal Government to say to the States: 'Here is the Bill. If you say you will police our international obligations we will get out of the area altogether and forget it'. That is not the position as it should be at all in regard to clause 9. Clause 9 should go. I urge the Government to vote for my amendment.
Mr WILSON (Sturt-Minister for Home Affairs and Environment) (12.23 a.m.)-I draw the attention of the Committee to the point that this is an historic Bill implementing an historic agreement which is a demonstration of cooperative federalism. It demonstrates that the States and the Commonwealth can reach an agreement to bring into operation a regime of law which protects coastal waters and enables the States to exercise control in certain ways subject to the terms of the agreement entered into. The control of marine pollution through dumping and the ratification of the London dumping convention has been the subject of detailed discussions with the States and the Northern Territory as part of the overall discussions on off-shore arrangements. In line with the general provisions of the off-shore constitutional settlement to give each State powers with respect to adjacent territorial sea it was agreed that the Commonwealth legislation would apply in respect of dumping outside the territorial sea and that it would also apply in respect of dumping inside the territorial sea if the State does not wish to legislate.
Time will tell whether any State or whether all States wish to legislate. If they wish to legislate their legislation will apply, but it will apply only so long as the legislation complies with the London dumping convention. What this Parliament is doing is legislating to cover all the seas from low water mark to the limits that we have discussed in earlier debate on this Bill. Clause 9 gives effect to' the arrangements entered into between the Commonwealth and the States. It is for the States to determine whether, for their own purposes, they wish to legislate. Their legislation must comply with the conditions of the London dumping convention otherwise the Commonwealth legislation will be brought into effect again.
Mr JACOBI (Hawker) (12.25 a.m.)-I ask the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment (Mr Wilson) a very simple and blunt question.

Environment Protection (Sea Dumping) Bill 2 and 3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2951

There is a contradiction between international law and municipal law. I think the Committee ought to address itself to the following question: Irrespective of agreements between the States and the Commonwealth, is the .Commonwealth in fact responsible for the implementation of the agreement in compliance with the London convention on dumping? I assume that it is, because international jurisdiction does not recognise States per se. It recognises a State in the sense of the Commonwealth of Australia. That is the conflict between the two sections ofIaw.

Mr WILSON (Sturt-Minister for Home Affairs and Environment) (12.26 a.m.)-It is recognised in international law that States in an international sense often have a Federal system of government. There is acceptance and understanding of the manner in which Federal clauses can be involved in the implementation of international conventions. Having said that, I emphasise that the Federal legislation will operate to comply with the London dumping convention and that legislation will be pushed back, as it were, only if the State legislation itself complies with that convention. Otherwise, the Commonwealth's legislation will move back to the low water mark, as it will do when this Bill first comes into operation.

Clauses 10to 17-by leave-taken together.

Mr WEST (Cunningham) (12.32 a.m.)-by leave-I move:

(2) Clause 10, page 5, line 5, at the beginning of the clause insert 'Subject to section 22,'.

(3) Clause 10, page 5, lines 7 and 8, omit paragraph (a), substitute the following paragraph:

'(a) are, or is, dumped into Australian waters or into the sea above the continental shelf of Australia or of an external Territory from any vessel, platform or aircraft; or'.

(4) Clause 11, page S,line 16, after 'waters' insert 'or into the sea above the continental shelf of Australia or of an external Territory'.

(5) Clause 12, page 5, sub-clause (I), at the beginning of the sub-clause insert 'Subject to section 22,'.

(6) Clause 12, page 5,lines 28-30, omit paragraph (a), substitute the following paragraph:

'(a) is loaded on any vessel, platform or aircraft in Australia or in Australian waters or in the sea above the continental shelf of Australia or of an external Territory for the purpose of being dumped into the sea or being incinerated at sea; or'.

(7) Clause 12, page 5,line 31, omit 'or Australian aircraft', substitute ',Australian aircraft or Australian platform'.

(8) Clause 14, page 6, sub-clause (I), at the beginning of the sub-clause insert 'Subject to ~ction 22, '.

(9) Clause 14, page 6,line 14, omit paragraph (a), substitute the following paragraph:

2952 REPRESENTATIVES 2 and 3 June 1981 Environment Protection (Sea Dumping) Bill

'(a) a vessel or platform in Australian waters or in the sea above the continental shelf of Australia or of an external Territory;'.
(10) Clause 17, page 8, line 40, at the end of paragraph (b) add 'or in the sea above the continental shelf of Australia or of an external Territory'.
Clause 10, which is the key to all this, states:
Where, otherwise than in accordance with a permit, any wastes or other matter-
(a) are, or is, dumped into Australian waters from any vessel or aircraft; or
(b) are, or is, dumped into any part of the sea from any Australian vessel, Australian aircraft or Australian platform . . .
I move the amendments to this clause because the effects of the clause are abundantly clear. Even ships of foreign nations which are signatories to the Convention on the Prevention of Marine Pollution by Dumping of Wastes and Other Matter only need an Australian permit from our designated authority to dump in territorial waters, that is, within three miles from a base line. Once they get outside that area they only require a permit from their own designated authority. That means a ship from France, the United States of America or any other nation could dump, according to its own permit system, four or five miles off the Australian coast. The situation is even worse for ships registered in a port of a foreign nation that is not a signatory to this Convention because outside the 3-mile limit they do not need a permit from anyone. They can simply dump what they like.
This Government has, as I have said, a curious ambivalence when it comes to controlling our offshore waters. It finds no contradiction at all in moving amendments to the Seas and Submerged Lands Act which claim one million square miles of territory in the Coral Sea to the outermost edge of the continental shelf, not for the purpose of preventing dumping at sea by foreign ships but for the purpose of issuing oil exploration permits and, if oil is found, drilling licences, and then we are told by the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) that he will protect the Great Barrier Reef by not giving a commitment to drill within 30 miles of it. Thanks for nothing! The point with regard to clause 10 is that we find no difficulties whatsoever in claiming the sea out to the continental shelf for fishing purposes, or oil drilling, but when it comes to asserting our national sovereignty as far as preventing the dumping of waste is concerned the Government is not interested.
I conclude by saying, as I outlined in the debate on the motion for the second reading of this Bill, that there is absolutely no contradiction in what I am proposing and what is proposed in the draft law of the sea. If it were not for the belligerence of the incoming Reagan Administration, which

jammed up this draft at the last possible moment, as outlined by one of my colleagues the other day, perhaps we would already have a clause to back up my assertion that this Government ought to be claiming that this Convention law operates to the outermost edge of the continental shelf. I am fully aware that the Minister has foreshadowed an amendment to clause 41 that, in effect, picks up and accepts what I am saying. Later he will say that an amendment to clause 41 will set out that the Government can do in future by regulation exactly what I am proposing tonight when it suits
its purpose.
Mr WILSON (Sturt-Minister for Home Affairs and Environment) (12.38 a.m.)-It is not true to say that the Government is not interested in this question of the responsibilities of foreign ships and aircraft beyond the outer boundary of the territorial sea. The amendment assumes that the draft law of the sea is the law of the sea. It is not yet the law of the sea and this legislation takes account of the international law as it is today. The main purpose of this Bill is to enable Australia to control marine pollution through dumping and incineration by giving effect to the London dumping convention. The intention of the Bill is not to assert to the maximum permissible degree the right of Australia to control dumping and incineration by foreign vessels beyond our territorial
waters.
The assertion of such jurisdiction raises issues not directly connected with the convention but which relate to the larger question of coastal state jurisdiction over the sea and seabed and the current state of development of the Law of the Sea. Any assertion by Australia of dumping jurisdiction over foreign vessels beyond the territorial sea could be challenged at international law as the extent of the outer limits of the Australian continental shelf for the purposes of the 1958 convention on the continental shelf is not a matter that can be regarded as free from controversy. It should be noted that whilst the 1958 convention on the continental shelf recognises certain rights of coastal states over the continental shelf, namely the right to control the exploration and exploitation of the resources of the continental shelf, it expressly preserves the 'high seas' status of superjacent waters. It is a potentially controversial question whether and in what circumstances the exercise of a claimed high seas right by foreign vessels would be subject to control by the coastal state under the 1958 convention. It is therefore considered by the Government that the proposed amendments to the definition of 'Australian waters' provide a valid alternative to the amendments moved by the Opposition in that

Environment Protection (Sea Dumping) Bill 2 and 3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2953

they foreshadow an assertion of dumping jurisdiction beyond the territorial sea and will allow the Government to move swiftly to extend the jurisdiction of the Bill to take account of future international developments.

In those circumstances, as the honourable member for Scullin (Dr Jenkins) pointed out, where solutions simply do not exist-
