Clause 22 (Radioactive wastes or other matter).

Mr WEST (Cunningham) (12.50 a.m.)-I move:

Clause 22, pages 13 and 14, omit the clause, substitute the following clause:

Radioactive wastes or other matter

22. (1) The dumping at sea of any radioactive wastes or other radioactive matter, being dumping to which this section applies, is prohibited.
(2) The incineration at sea of any radioactive wastes or other radioactive matter, being incineration to which this section applies, is prohibited.
(3) The loading of any radioactive wastes or other radioactive matter for the purpose of being dumped at sea or incinerated at sea, being loading to which this section applies, is prohibited.
(4) This section applies to dumping at sea and incineration at sea carried out from or by-
(a) any vessel, platform or aircraft in or into Australian waters or in or into the sea above the continental shelf of Australia or of an external Territory;
(b) any Australian vessel, Australian aircraft or Australian platform in or into any part of the sea.
(5) This section applies to loading on or onto
(a) any vessel, aircraft or platform in Australia or in Australian waters or in

2954 REPRESENTATIVES 2 and 3 June 1981 Environment Protection (Sea Dumping) Bill

the sea above the continental shelf of Australia or of an external Territory; (b) any Australian vessel, Australian aircraft or Australian platform in, or in the territorial sea, of a country that is not a party to the Convention. (6) The owner and person in charge of the vessel, platform or aircraft on or from which loading, dumping at sea or incineration at sea in contravention of this section is carried out and the owner of the wastes .or other matter so loaded, dumped or incinerated are each guilty of an offence against this section, punishable on conviction by a fine not exceeding (a) if the person is a natural person- $50,000; or (b) if the person is a body corporate- $100,000.
I have already drawn attention to the problems that have arisen from clauses 10, 11 and 12 regarding signatory nations needing only their own permits to dump radioactive materials within three miles of our shores and the fact that under this legislation non-signatory nations do not require any permits at all to dump within three miles of our shores. It is obvious then that, in the case of signatory nations, low level radioactive wastes could be dumped under their permits within four miles of our shores. Non-signatory nations could dump anything, including high level radioactive waste, within four miles of our shores. Japan is obviously a signatory nation, yet it has already indicated that it will dump 45,000 drums of low level or medium level radioactive waste in the Pacific Ocean. I am absolutely amazed that the Government will not accept my amendment, considering the actions that are going forward.
I say to the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment (Mr Wilson) that I am amazed that the Government has not firmly told the Japanese Government that Australia will not stand by and accept that it is Japan's right to dump radioactive wastes of any sort in the ocean. Of all the nations, one would expect Japan to have some extra sensitivity in this matter. When the Minister was asked questions about the Government's attitude to the Japanese proposal for dumping off the Mariani Islands he said, and I will read from a letter which I advise all honourable members to read in Hansard tomorrow because it was incorporated in my speech on the second reading of the Bill:
Officials advised the Japanese mission that Australia considered it essential that the disposal of nuclear waste in any environment, and in particular the Pacific Ocean, should be undertaken only in compliance with the strictest safety measures and in full accordance with the relevant international agreements on the subject. Australia has taken a position to this effect at meetings of the South Pacific Forum.

is no safe method known to science for the disposal of nuclear wastes. I might add that plutonium, which is a derivative of high level radioactive waste from spent uranium oxide, can exist and be harmful to man for up to half a million years. There is no way we can get around that. There is no way we can get around the fact that no nation has yet been able to decommission a nuclear power station. A period of 100 years has to elapse before the core of these stations can be touched. In the United States nobody has yet been successful in cleaning up the results of the Harrisburg disaster. When they finally get around to cleaning it up, it will cost at least $2 billion. They cannot work out who should be responsible for cleaning it up and who should pay the price. How can we be sure that nuclear nations, committed to nuclear power stations because of the inability of their scientists to work out a way to dispose of nuclear waste, will never dump waste, even of a high level into the ocean? At the very least, Australia should declare that in no circumstances will we ever dump radioactive waste into the seas anywhere and that those nations which will not comply with that declaration will be prevented from disposing of such waste on our continental shelf.
Mr WILSON (Sturt-Minister for Home Affairs and Environment) (12.56 a.m.)-The London dumping convention prohibits the disposal of high level radioactive material listed in Annexe I of the convention and places strict controls on the disposal of other radioactive material listed in Annexe II of the convention. This Bill gives effect to these provisions. The effect can be seen in clauses 19 (2) and 19 (5) together with the first Schedule to the Bill. In the issue of permits for the disposal of radioactive material not listed in Annexe I, the convention contracting parties are to take full account of stringent guidelines developed by the International Atomic Energy Agency which is the recognised international authority in this field. Clause 19 (6) (a) of the Bill specifically provides for consideration of these guidelines. These provisions in the Bill indicate Australia's willingness to comply with the strictest international measures laid down for the control of sea dumping of radioactive materials. In view of that fact and the fact that this Bill aims to enable Australia to comply with the London dumping convention, the amendment just moved by the Opposition is not acceptable to the Government.

The Government has given the Japanese a virtual Mrs DARLING (Lilley) (12.57 a.m.)~The carte-blanche to dump the stuff by not telling it amendment to clause 22 of the Bill, as proposed firmly that we disapprove of the dumping. There by the Opposition, suggests a course of action

Environment Protection (Sea Dumping) Bill 2 and 3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2955

which only a completely irresponsible government would reject. I therefore hope that Govern-
ment members in this House are not just conscientious enough but also are realistic enough to see
that to allow the dumping of radioactive matter as
close as three miles off the Australian coastline is entirely against the national interest and, in fact,
could have disastrous effects on us all. The exist-
ing clause 22, as proposed by the Government, gives the Minister the power to issue a permit for
dumping or loading radioactive matter, stating
special conditions to be adhered to if he deems the matter to be 'so radioactive as to require special
precautions to be taken'.

Mrs DARLING (Lilley) (1.10 a.m.)-This clause highlights, as do the other clauses we have been dealing with, the complete inadequacy of this Bill to protect the people of Australia. The Bill allows the dumping of low and medium level radioactive wastes in waters within three miles of the Australian coast. This Parliament should be legislating to make dumping of such waste by persons from any country in Australian coastal waters an offence.

Mr DEPUTY CHAIRMAN (Mr Giles)- Order! I cannot hear what the honourable member for Lilley is saying. However, I have doubts about whether she is referring to the amendment. I invite her to get quickly on to it, considering the hour.

Mrs DARLING-I feel that this is so important to the Australian people that it should be considered even at this late hour.

Mr DEPUTY CHAIRMAN. The honourable member will not raise matters unless they are related to the amendment, or I will have to rule her out of order.

Mrs DARLING-I point out that the proposed amendment was in keeping with the Convention for the Prevention of Marine Pollution by Dumping of wastes and other matter, to which Australia

is a signatory, along with 44 other nations. Article IV, paragraph 3 of that Convention states:
No provision of this Convention is to be interpreted as preventing a Contracting Party from prohibiting, insofar as that Party is concerned, the dumping of wastes or other matter not mentioned in Annex 1.
The article allows countries to prohibit the dumping of substances as long as the organisation is notified of such prohibitions. The prohibition of sea dumping of nuclear matter is of particular importance to me, as a Queenslander. The unique reef off the east coast of Queensland must be protected. The Great Barrier Reef Marine Park (Capricornia Section) Regulations make dumping of wastes from a vessel or an aircraft, an island or fixed structure within the Capricornia section an offence. Under the Act, dumping of waste by Australians is regulated, but not that of foreign countries.
As the honourable member for Cunningham (Mr West) has pointed out the reef should be protected to the extent of the continental shelf, but it is of even greater importance that people be protected. Protection should be given to all Australian coastal waters. The radioactive pollution of Australian waters has horrific implications for us and for future generations. The probability of such pollution should not be underrated. The Queensland Premier, for one, is feverishly in favour of the extension of the uranium industry in Queensland but his enthusiasm goes beyond the bounds of reason. His determination to proceed with the project, despite the decision by a mining warden's court to disallow expansion of a uranium mining project in Townsville for safety reasons, typifies the situations which could arise in Queensland coastal waters. The Charters Towers mining warden, Mr Eric Lendich, made his decision because of 'grave doubts as to the suitability and safety of proposals by the French company Minatome, for a treatment plant, evaporation ponds and tailing ponds'. In relation to the Premier's response to the warden's decision, I quote from the Hobart Mercury of 28 April 1981. It states:
The Queensland Premier, Mr Joh Bjelke-Petersen-

Mr WILSON (Sturt-Minister for Home Affairs and Environment) (1.15 a.m.) -by leave-l move:

Clause 29, page 16, add ai the end of sub-clause (4) 'to

which it is safe and practicable to bring the vessel'.

.

Clause 29, page 17, line 16, omit '$1,000', substitute '$2,000'.

These amendments have arisen from consultations with the States. The Premier of Tasmania drew to the attention of the Government the fact that the clause, as presently drawn, may require an inspector directing a ship to enter a port to enter the nearest port. The purpose of the first amendment is to cover the possibility that travel to the nearest port by a vessel may be unsafe and impracticable. It would enable a direction to be given that the ship go to the nearest safe or practicable port. The second amendment is technical in nature and increases from $1,000 to $2,000 penalty which may be imposed.

Amendments agreed to.

Clause, as amended, agreed to.

We now see what the Government is about. Having accepted the relevance and validity of our argument, it is now saying that it may want to do this at some future point by way of regulation. We would have much preferred that the Government accept our proposed course of action, but will not oppose the amendment. We will merely regard it as a victory for the Opposition.

COMMONWEALTH LEGAL AID COMMISSION AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Second Reading Debate resumed from 27 May, on motion by MrVinel':
That the Bill be now read a second time.
MI' LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (1.21 a.m.)-The Opposition opposes this Bill. I move:
That all words after 'That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not declining io give this Bill a second reading, the House is of the opinion thai--
(I) the Commonwealth has a continuing national responsibility for the funding of legal aid, the States having amply demonstrated in the past that they have neither the resources nor the inclination to ensure equal access to the law;
(2) the Commonwealth has an indispensable national role in the formulation of legal aid policy so as to ensure that legal services are delivered on a co-ordinated, rational and equitable basis;
(3) the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission has a central national role to play in research, evaluating, advising upon, co-ordinating and monitoring the funding and delivery oflegal aid services;
(4) the present functions of the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission cannot be performed as adequately or effectively by a part time advisory Council and an Attorney-General's Department already subject to severe staffing constraints, and

2958 REPRESENTATIVES 2 and 3 June 1981 Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Bill

(5) the proposals to abolish the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission, to curtail the functions and limit the independence of its successor advisory Council and to limit the number of full time officials working on legal aid policy and funding, are all to be deplored as marking the first step towards the Commonwealth's ultimate abandonment of responsibility for legal aid funding'.
The Opposition has set out in the five paragraphs of the amendment why it suggests that this Bill should be opposed. The purpose of the Bill is to abolish the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission and to create another body called the Commonwealth Legal Aid Council. Honourable members will notice in the preamble to the Bill a prpposal to transfer Commonwealth employees. In other words, Commonwealth employees will be made redundant or transferred. This brings us to the point of what the Bill is all about. This is one of the first of the package of Bills which has resulted from the recommendations of what is known as the Lynch committee. That committee is anxious to cut costs and it proposes to do so in a most stupid and ineffective way. It is proposed to transfer people from-
Mr Coleman-That is a harsh statement.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-I know that the hour is late and that honourable members are a bit tired. But all the Government is doing is transferring unfortunate officers out to some other jurisdiction where I think they would still be paid. One wonders how the razor gang can justify this cost cutting without any idea or analysis of what it is about from the point of view of Commonwealth programs or State programs. If these people are to be transferred to some other area someone else has to bear the burden either in another area of Commonwealth responsibility or another area of State responsibility. It is really unsound to suggest that the legislation is doing anything at all. I remind the House that the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) made a promise in 1975 that no person would be denied legal aid because of lack of means. This is another example of the Prime Minister's again reneging on a promise.
Mr John Brown-Don't tell me he broke a promise!
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-Oh, he has broken many of them. There are many problems in legal aid in Australia, particularly in Victoria and Tasmania. We see the complete abandonment of Commonw~alth involvement. There is a view that the Commonwealth getting out of legal aid would not affect the Australian people. We should bear in mind that very many people are affected by Commonwealth laws. Many people are obliged to go to litigation or to courts because of Commonwealth legislation. We recognise that in a number

of areas, particularly in administrative law. We have had to set up a number of tribunals to assist people, even in the welfare field. Now, in the really difficult area of what one would call adversery legislation, many people will be denied any help at all.
It is also significant that the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission, which was created by the Government, is now being abolished by it. In introducing legislation in 1977 Attorney-General Ellicott said that the Bill was an important part of the framework within which the partnership between the Australian and State governments would be conducted. So much for the partnership! We are now abolishing the Commission. It is clear that no partnership will exist any longer. Again, it would appear that the Government objects to the fact that the Commission was doing effective work and was able to prove it was dynamic. Sir John Nimmo was critical of Commonwealth funding and I suppose that might well have caused the demise of this Commission. In 1978-79 he said:
Since the change in the Federal Government in December 1975 we have seen a 'freeze' in the provision of federal legal aid through the Australian Legal Aid Office while the Government negotiated with the States for the introduction of its new comprehensive scheme. The 'freeze' has been marked, on the one hand, by the imposition of restrictive staff ceilings, and, on the other hand, by failure to adjust or index the monetary commitment limit for referrals to private practitioners to take account of inflation. The commitment limit was fixed at $12m per annum in August 1975. Since then, there has only been one minor increase-to $12.6m-- -in 1977-78.
A distinction needs to be drawn between the annual monetary commitment limit for referrals and the annual provision in the Budget for expenditure on payment of accounts for referrals completed. Expenditure inevitably increases each year because of the time lag between commitment and expenditure and because of cost increases after the referrals are made. However, the resulting annual increases in expenditure are not a measure of the reallevcl of legal aid which is governed by the annual commitment limit.
In other words, what has been happening for years is that the commitment has been limiting the amount of legal aid but has not been able to cope with the arrears that have had to be met because of the actual taking up of cases. Whilst the commitment was limited to, say, $12m per annum, the Government was actually spending some $16.6m in the Budget merely to pick up arrears and not to provide any additional legal aid.
The Commission also complained of the serious rundown in the provision of federal legal aid and recommended the expenditure of $500,000 on voluntary and community legal aid services. Yet only half that amount has been provided in 1981, two years after the Commission recommended that $500,000 ought to be spent. It is important that we look at what was admitted in the Senate

CUSTOMS TARIFF VALIDATION BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 28 May, on motion by MrMoore:
That the Bill be now read a second time.

Mr HURFORD (Adelaide) (1.46 a.m,)-Mr Deputy Speaker, the purpose of this Bill-and I am going to stay here until the end of my specch~-is to validate the collection of customs duties until 31 December 1981 in respect of tariffs imposed on imports of silicone products and wheelchairs. Each of these industries is relatively small, the silicone industry having about 110 employees in Australia and the wheelchair industry having about 55 employees, However, for quite different reasons, these are very important industries and the assistance arrangements afforded to them by the Government will have an important impact on particular sections of the community, Because the Government is mismanaging the affairs of this House so that we have to debate this Bill at 1.45 in the morning is no reason why I am not going to talk on this Bill. The silicone industry is a branch of the chemical industry, and involves high technologies which have had rapidly increasing application since the Second World War, I warn Government. members that I have an amendment on this Bill and it is in their best interests that they stay here until the death to vote on it,
The industry is characterised by large scale, integrated production processes, and as is common in these circumstances, major multinational corporations dominate the industry, Australian production began in 1966 and 70 per cent of production occurs at Dow Corning's plant. The value of production has rapidly grown in the face of expanding demand for products based on silicones such as sealants, greases, adhesives and resins, The industry is now principally supplied by local producers, and export sales have become increasingly important. I am delighted that the honourable member for Hughes (Mr Les Johnson) is wide awake at this early hour of the morning and noting how interesting this speech is, The companies involved have become quite export conscious, and rapid growth in sales to South East Asian countries is expected. The Industries Assistance Commission recommended that a duty of 10 per cent apply for the intermediate and finished silicone products, and minimum duties of 2 per cent apply to raw materials. These rates significantly reduce the nominal levels of assistance afforded to the industry. However, because the cost of raw materials will fall, the effective rates of assistance for many of the products will remain similar to current levels. The Opposition has no objection to these decisions.
The second matter dealt with by this Bill relates to tariff assistance for producers of wheelchairs in Australia. Tariff Proposal No.7 of 1980, which is validated by this Bill, provides for a duty rate to

2962 REPRESENTATIVES 2 and 3 June 1981

Customs Tariff Validation Bill

be applied to imports of wheelchairs of 30 per cent for one year, 25 per cent in the following year, and 20 per cent thereafter. Prior to these changes the rate applicable to mechanically propelled wheelchairs was 34 per cent and 36 per cent for manually operated wheelchairs. These high rates increased the cost of wheelchairs by up to $300. They applied from 1 January 1980, before which time the rate was only 15 per cent on imports from the United Kingdom, a significant source of supply. I repeat the facts. On 1 January 1980 the rate of duty and thus the cost of wheelchairs went up because of an action of the Government in getting rid of the United Kingdom preferences.
Mr Scott-Why do they hate invalids?
Mr HURFORD-Exactly. Why does the Government hate invalids? Why on earth would it allow the cost of wheelchairs to go up, as the honourable member for Hindmarsh has pointed out?
Mr Scott-It is the Year ofDisabled Persons on top of it.
Mr HURFORD-Exactly, in the Year ofDis1 abled Persons. The majority of wheelchairs sold in
Australia are imported. It is quite incredible that a Customs duty of 34.36 per cent should have been applied to those imports, to the disadvantage of the disabled, for the past 16 months. After extra ordinary delays, the Government has decided to reduce the duties, but they will still be a high 30 per cent for one year, and the ultimate rate of 20 per cent will still be higher than the rate applying to many imports of wheelchairs before 1 January 1980. It is not possible for the Government to excuse its delays in making this decision. The lAC, as requested, forwarded the final report to the Government on 23December 1980, about five months ago. This delay, I repeat, is inexcusable. How does the Government excuse the decision to maintain high tariff duties on wheelchairs, par ticularly as this is the Year ofDisabled Persons. In the lAC draft report, a minority of commissioners preferred the payment of a bounty for local pro ducers. This proposal is very sensible.
Mr Scholes-It pays a bounty on tractors. Why not on wheelchairs?
Mr HURFORD-As the honourable member for Corio says, bounties are paid on tractors, and sensibly bounties are paid on many other prod ucts. Why should they not be paid on wheelchairs? The industry provides only a.min ority share of the market and employs only 55 people. A bounty would not be expensive, but it would greatly benefit the disadvantaged people

who must use wheelchairs. However, the Government announced in the 1980 Budget that it would introduce a program of aids for the disabled. The scheme was intended to commence on 1 April this year and $700,000 was allocated to it. But the plain fact is that the scheme has not been implemented, and it is not likely to be implemented for some time to come. That is a measure of this Government's lack of concern for the disadvantaged in our society. It is also a measure of its lack' of commitment to the objec tives of the International Year of Disabled Per sons. Every member of the Australian community also knows that it is a typical example of what happens when this Government makes a promise. Having failed to implement the program of aids to daily living for the disabled, the Minister for Busi ness and Consumer Affairs (Mr Moore) had the temerity in his second reading speech to refer to the program on apparent justification of the decision to retain high rates of duty.
It is inexcusable for this Government to com mence a new regime of tariff duties on one date while breaking its promises in relation to offset ting assistance. Indeed, we have been given no new commencement date for the aids program of $700,000 promised to the community in this Year of Disabled Persons. In the intervening period, about 500 wheelchairs are sold every month at an inflated cost because of the high rate of duties. It is not merely this delay that should concern us. The Government has not indicated who will be eligible for assistance under the aids program. The program relates not only to wheelchairs but also to a wide range of other aids to daily living.
The Government allocated $700,000 for a three-month period. This implies an annual cost of $2.8m. Yet annual sales of wheelchairs exceed $4m. So there is no way in the wide world, that that particular program would offset the cost of wheelchairs to those poor unfortunate people who are obliged to use that form of transport. It is virtually certain, therefore, that the tariff duties on wheelchairs will have to be paid by many dis abled persons, even if the aid program is introduced. This is quite scandalous.
The Government should be condemned for its pathetic efforts in relation to the disabled. It makes promises during election campaigns and then breaks or delays them immediately after the election. The only further promise that the dis abled in our community should seek from the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar) is his prom ise to resign. The Minister for Business and Con sumer Affairs should be ashamed to have the car riage of a Bill such as this which maintains high rates of duty on wheelchairs. Not only should he

Customs Tariff Validation Bill

2 and 3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2963

be ashamed but also he should ensure that something is done very soon to alter the present situation. So that we may focus on this situation more clearly, I move:
That all words after 'That' be omitted with a view to substituting the following words:
'whilst not opposing this Bill, the House-
(1) expresses its concern that the Customs duties validated by the Bill in relation to wheelchairs will maintain the high cost of these necessary aids for the disabled;
(2) calls upon the Government immediately to review its decision in relation to wheelchairs with a view to replacing the tariff arrangements provided in the Bill with a bounty scheme for local producers, and
(3) condemns the Government for its failure to implement its promise for a Programme of Aids for Disabled People including the free provision of wheelchairs' .
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)-Is the amendment seconded?
Mrs CHILD (Henty) (1.56 a.m.)-I second the amendment moved by my colleague the honourable member for Adelaide (Mf Hurford) as I believe a bounty would be in the best interests of disabled people. On April 29 I brought this matter before the Parliament and asked the Treasurer (Mr Howard) to waive all duty on wheelchairs, both in justice and as a gesture from the Government towards the International Year of Disabled Persons. The Treasurer came into the House to say that he appreciated my concern and would certainly discuss the matter with the Minister for Social Security (Senator Chaney) and with the Cabinet because he agreed that there was some justice in what I had to say.
I do not really call it justice when the Commonwealth Government announced that tariffs will remain on wheelchairs. Disabled people are currently paying a tariff of 34 per cent on imported electric wheelchairs. Thirty-four per cent is equivalent to approximately $1,000 a chair. This is an enourmous burden to place on people who are already disadvantaged through disability. On top of the cost of $3,000 for an electric wheelchair, it costs a disabled person $20 to $30 a week to maintain it. An electric wheelchair in constant use lasts for only a few years. The Government has indicated that the tariff will reduce to 20 per cent over two years, but it would still be 5 per cent higher than the tariff which existed prior to I January 1980.
I think the two basic convictions on which the Government should approach this matter, are firstly, that the needs of the wheelchair user are of basiC importance, as a wheelchair is a necessary utility, not a luxury, to many disabled people and, secondly, that the existence of a viable Australian

wheelchair manufacturing industry is necessary to supply both standard and non-standard units to cater for specific disabilities, because many of the wheelchairs are tailored to the disability a person has and to provide an efficient maintenance and parts supply service. We must maintain our own industry. In doing this a bounty would be much more beneficial to the end user and would provide greater assistance and more likelihood of the preservation of a viable local industry.
Wheelchair users are a special group of consumers. It would not be appropriate for them to have to bear the cost of protecting the local industry themselves. Again, I stress that wheelchairs are a necessity, not a luxury. The information available on the economic circumstances of the disabled seems to indicate that as a group they are financially disadvantaged because of the expenses incurred through their disability. It appears that most have reduced ability to earn a normal income. Some people may well ask why wheelchair users, as distinct from other disabled people, should receive preferential treatment from the Government. This group, because of its particular type of disability, often requires much more assistance in everyday living than other disabled people.
In addition to the general maintenance costs involved in owning a wheelchair-that is, for wheel replacements, brake adjustments and seat repairs-those confined to a wheelchair often require assistance in bathing, dressing, movement to and from the wheelchair and mobility outside their home. If no family member is available to do these things nursing attendants must be employed. All this costs money and the money comes out of the pockets of the disabled. Yet they are expected to pay this heavy duty on imported wheelchairs. Assistance is also required for house and garden maintenance for those not living in institutions. This type of assistance is not a luxury; it is a necessity if the wheelchair-bound are to live in our society and make a contribution to it. If payment of a bountry is good enough to help farmers to buy tractors and superphosphate, it is good enough to help the disabled to buy wheelchairs.
The Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs (Mr Moore) and the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip Lynch) indicated that disabled people would be able to obtain wheelchairs through the aids scheme which was announced in August 1980. We keep getting this red herring dragged across. The aids scheme was announced in August of last year. Nine months have passed and not one disabled person has received an aid. Disabled people are asking the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar) and the

2964 REPRESENTATIVES 2 and 3 June 1981

Customs TariJfValidation Bill

State Health Ministers what they are doing with the money. In nine months not one aid has been prescribed. Unless action occurs immediately disabled people will lost $700,000 worth of aids. The Government obviously does not understand the problems faced by disabled people in obtaining aids for daily living. The decision made on the tariff issue ignores the fact that there was a thriving industry in Australia when the tariff was only 15 per cent. I am appalled that after nine months disabled people still have not received their aids and appliances. I am very disappointed at what the Government has brought down. I asked for better from the Treasurer and expected it, which perhaps was a bit naive of me. I totally support the amendment moved by my colleague the honourable member for Adelaide-which is supported by every member on this side of the chamber-who really does believe that disabled people have a right to live in this community and should be subsidised to give them that right.
Mrs KELLY (Canberra) (2.3 a.m.)-I rise at this late hour to speak in support of the proposed amendment because of the importance of this amendment to the handicapped people in Australia, and to point out-to emphasise further-the hypocritical attitude of this Government. On the one hand it is espousing the cause of the disabled in this the International Year of Disabled Persons but, on the other, it is bringing about legislation which will cause severe hardship to users of wheelchairs in Australia. I believe most Australians will be appalled by this callous decision. Currently the tariff on an imported electric wheelchair is 34 per cent, or about $1,000. We should all remember the importance of this equipment to a disabled person. The wheelchair is his or her only source of mobility. In many cases the wheelchair is a life-line which allows a disabled person to participate in a normal lifestyle, to gain employment, to attend social functions and generally to enjoy life's opportunities as much as possible. That is what will be taken from the handicapped people in Australia by the Customs Tariff Validation Bill which is before the House tonight.
Electric wheelchairs, in particular, are vital to those people unfortunate enough to suffer debilitating diseases, especially mUltiple sclerosis and other such diseases. These people rely upon electric wheelchairs as they do not have sufficient muscular strength to operate ordinary wheelchairs. These people are already seriously disadvantaged by their disability. Yet this Government wishes to add an enormous financial burden as well. The cost of an electric wheelchair is about $3,000 and a further $20 to $30 a week is

required for maintenance. A wheelchair in constant use would last only a few years. It is iniquitous that disabled people are being charged high tariffs on their essential items of equipment. It is particularly unjust when we remember that each year this Government hands out hundreds of millions of dollars of taxpayers' money in subsidies to industry, yet it is too mean to assist the small number of people in our community who are disabled.
The Australian Council for Rehabilitation of the Disabled in a submission to the Industries Assistance Commission in 1980 recommended a bounty of 20 per cent on wheelchairs. Members of the Government may be interested in the comments of the Executive Director of ACROD who said that if a bounty is good enough to help farmers to buy tractors and superphosphate, it should be good enough for disabled people to buy wheelchairs. It has become apparent that this Government has little practical concern for the disabled people, despite its rhetoric. Both the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs (Mr Moore) and the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip Lynch) in their announcement in August 1980, indicated that disabled people would be able to obtain wheelchairs through the aids scheme. Nine months later no disabled person has received an aid. So much for the rhetoric.
I am particularly aware of the Government's breach of commitment to giving practical assistance to disabled people through my efforts to assist a young inventor in my electorate. This young man has invented a microprocessor controlled foldable electric wheelchair. This very lightweight wheelchair was the result of the inventor's knowledge of the debilitating effects experienced by his brother and other disabled people when using heavy, conventional wheelchairs. This project is well advanced with patent applications pending and considerable commercial interest being shown in the manufacturing side of the project. This enterprise would be particularly suitable for commercial development in the Australian Capital Territory and would be most appropriate in this International Year of Disabled Persons. The inventor requires funding of between $8,000 and $10,000 to continue the project and develop a prototype. He has spent considerable time and effort to get assistance but so far he has been unsuccessful. He has approached the assistance to inventors scheme but this scheme has no funds available. In any case, the future of this scheme is clouded following the razor gang review and this is yet another case of inefficient and ad hoc decision making. The Department of Productivity apparently has

Answers to Questions

The following answers to questions were circulated:

Australian Equity Participation in New Investment Projects
(Question No. 584)
Mr Holding asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 2S February 1981:
(l) Has his attention been drawn to the article headed 'FIRB gets up a $500m natural resources shopping list' in the Australian Financial Review,19 February 1981, page 30.
(2) If so, (a) what steps does the Foreign Investment Review Board take to work towards a high level of Australian equity participation in new investment projects and (b) do these steps include regular notification to Australian Stock Exchanges of projects requiring increased Australian equity participation; if so, what are the details.
(3) What foreign investment projects have been approved on second or subsequent applications, following direct FIRB involvement, to increase Australian equity participation, for each year from 1975-76 to 1979-80.
(4) With respect to Chapter 2, Foreign investment in industry sectors,.of the FIRB Report 1980, pages 9 to 18, (a) what (i) are the names of the 127 manufacturing companies where payment for assets by foreign investors led to a reduction in Australian ownership in 1978-79, and (ii) was the percentage of Australian ownership before and after that payment in each case (page 9), (b) what (i) are the names of the 145 manufacturing companies where payment for assets by foreign investors led to a reduction in Australian ownership in 1979-80 and (ii) was the percentage of Australian ownership before and after that payment in each case (page 9); (c) what are the names of the (i) 67 businesses in 1978-79 and (ii) 76 businesses in 1979-80 where Australian control was lost through acquisition, giving in each case the name both before and after acquisition (page 9); (d) how many proposals in the manufacturing sector were approved subject to conditions in (i) 1978-79 and (ii) 1979-80, giving in each case (A) the names of the company or companies concerned, (B) the conditions that were applied, (C) whether the conditions have been met and (D) what changes have occurred where conditions have not been met, (page 9); (e) has the FIRB completed its review of the activities of some overseas-owned companies, especially in the food industry, which have made a considerable number of small business acquisitions; if so, (i) has the FIRB made a report to the Government; if so, when and (ii) will he make a statement to the Parliament regarding the companies involved, the reasons for the review and any Government action which has resulted from that ~eview (page 10); (f) (i) what were the conditions established by the Government in July 1978, in order for its approval to the Iwasaki Sangyo Co. for its tourist project at Yeppoon, Queensland, to be granted, (ii) what form has the FIRB's monitoring of the project taken to date, (iii) for how much longer will the FIRB be required to monitor the project, (iv) which of the Government's conditions have not been met, in whole or part, and what action has the Government taken as a result, (v) has the Government reconsidered its 1979 decision not to allow further land acquisitions for the Iwasaki project; if so, has further land acquisition been approved; if that approval has been given, what is the area of extra acquisition and when was it acquired, (vi) what is the percentage.of Australian equity in the Iwasaki project, as at 24 February 1981, and (vii) what is the nature of the Australian participation in other ways in the Iwasaki project (page 12); (g) what are the names of the United States of America firms which have

lodged the 19 successful applications to invest in private hospitals in Australia and what are the locations of the hospitals which were the subject of those applications; (h) when were the applications referred to in part 4 (g) approved and what was the percentage of local equity in each of the companies concerned, as at 24 February 1981, (j) what are the names, before and after acquisition, of the II Australian owned advertising agencies taken over in the last 2 years by foreign firms (page 13) and (k) what methods does the FIRB employ in seeking to establish that Australians have been given an opportunity to buy rural land when there is a proposal before the FIRB for the sale of that land to foreign persons (page 14).
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) Yes.
(2) (a) The Foreign Investment Review Board discusses with the parties to foreign investment proposals the Australian equity participation requirements that may apply to the proposed investments under the foreign investment policy. Such discussions are an important means of ensuring that the proposed equity structures of projects will satisfy the relevant Australian participation requirements. The discussions are held as early as possible in the planning of projects so that the formulation of the proposal can be undertaken with a knowledge of the Government's policy objectives.
In the examination of individual proposals, the Board considers the proposals against the Government's objective to seek Australian participation to the extent appropriate to the particular circumstances of each proposal, and in the light of the specific guidelines for Australian involvement in new natural resource projects. In all proposals for the acquisition of a substantial interest in an existing Australian business, the board considers very carefully the opportunities that have been provided for Australian investors to sustain or increase the level of Australian participation in the enterprises.
(b) No. Arrangement by which wholly or partly overseasowned companies seek increased Australian participation are a commercial matter to be undertaken by the companies concerned through commercial channels of their own choice. From time to time, Australian companies and institutions approach the FIRB to make known their interest in participating in natural resource development. If the foreign participants in projects requiring additional Australian equity participation so request, they are supplied with the names of those Australian companies and institutions (subject to their concurrence) which have indicated an interest in investing in natural resources.
(3) The FIRB processes referrcd to in 2 (a) frequently reo sult in increases being made in the proposed level of Australian equity during the course of initial consideration of applications. Accordingly, only a small proportion of applications is rejected. Of the 45 proposals rejected between I April 1976 and 31 December 1980,22 were declined, at least in part, because of insufficient provision for Australian equity. Four of these were latcr approved in the light of increased provision for Australian equity participation, including:
the acquisition by Brooke Bond Australia of Bushells;
a proposal by Iwasaki Sangyo Co. (Australia) Pty Ltd to establish a tourist development project at Yeppoon, Queensland; and

Answers to Questions

2 and 3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2969

a proposal involving the acquisition by Robin Holdings Pty Ltd of Singapore of an interest in a property known as 'Merrimac',located on the Queensland Gold Coast.
Details of the other case have not been made public by the Government.
(4) (a), (b) and (c) Because of the commercial sensitivity attaching to much of the information provided to the Board in connection with foreign investment proposals, the Government and the Board treat all such information in strict confidence and do not as a matter of practice disclose details of individual proposals, except in special circumstances where the parties' consent is first obtained. It is not, therefore, possible to provide details of the sort that have been requested.
(d) See answer to 4 (a), (b) and (c). The following information on all manufacturing sector proposals conditionally approved in 1978-79 and 1979-80 is available:

U) Seeanswert04 (a), (b) and (c).
(k) In the examination of proposals for foreign acquisition of rural land the FIRB seeks to ensure that Australians have had an opportunity to purchase the land if they wished to do so. The Board inquires whether and over what period of time the relevant property has been advertised for sale or listed with agents in the manner normally employed in the industry, and seeks information on any Australian bids Jor the land. In cases where the land has not been offered to Australians or the exposure to the market has been regarded as inadequate, the parties have been ,invited to advertise the property on the understanding that the Government would complete its examination of the proposal in the light of the outcome from the advertisement.

Subsidised Medical Equipment

(Question No. 764)

Mrs Kelly asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 11 March 1981:

Is there provision for subsidising medical equipment, such as wheelchairs, for disabled persons either by direct grants or by taxation concessions; if so, what are the details.

Mr Howard-The, answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

As regards direct assistance to disabled persons, in the context of the 1980-81 Federal Budget it was announced that the Government would introduce a new program under which aids to daily living would be provided to disabled people not eligible under existing Government assistance programs. The new program is in recognition of the needs of disabled people, and is a Commonwealth initiative in the International Year of Disabled Persons.

Under the Program of Aids for Disabled People, a range of aids to daily living, including home modifications, wheelchairs, orthoses, oxygen, electrolarynxes, aids for incontinence, walking aids, and personal aids (eating and cooking utensils, toilet articles, etc) will be provided on medical prescription to eligible disabled people, without cost to the recipient. Assistance under this program is to be by way of the provision of aids only, and will not include subsidising the cost of medical equipment purchased by individual disabled people.

I understand that the Department of Health is currently

developing administrative procedures with a view to introduc-

ing the new program later this year.

'

Turning now to taxation concessions for disabled persons, I

note that medical and surgical appliances, including

wheelchairs, prescribed by a legally qualified medical prac-

titioner qualify as rebatable expenditure under the-Income

Tax Assessment Act. A rebate is not, however, allowable un-

less the total of all classes of rebatable expenditure, including

life insurance premiums, superannuation, medical expenses,

rates, education expenses and self-education expenses,

exceeds $1,590. The rebate is allowable on the amount of

expenditure over $1,590 at the standard tax rate of 3~ cents in

the dollar.

.

2970 REPRESENTATIVES 2 and 3 June 1981

Answers to Questions

Item 42 (10) in the First Schedule to the Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Act exempts from sales tax a range of medical equipment, including wheelchairs, used by disabled persons.
States Borrowing for Power Stations
(Question No. 786)
Mr Howe asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 11 March 1981:
(1) In approving infrastructure borrowing by the States through the Loan Council for power stations, what information is available to the Commonwealth to evaluate the economic viability of the proposed works.
(2) Does his Department undertake any assessment of the pricing policies of the various State instrumentalities.
(3) If not, how does the Commonwealth determine whether the proposed borrowing is consistent with Commonwealth policy on national resource allocation priorities.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
. (1), (2) and (3) The Loan Council has adopted a procedure of referring proposals submitted to it for consideration under the infrastructure borrowing program to a working party of Commonwealth and State Treasury officers for evaluation and report before taking decisions on them. The proposals are evaluated with regard to guidelines which have been agreed by the Loan Council. Reference to those guidelines is to be found in Budget Paper No 7 for 1980-81, page 33. The working party reports are confidential to the Loan Council.
Resource Development
(Question No. 843)
Mr Willis asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 24 March 1981:
(1) Has his attention been drawn to a statement allegedly made by the Deputy Prime Minister and reported in the Australian Financial Review of 9 March 1981, in which the Deputy Prime Minister is reported to have said that in the last 2 years a number of resources companies had, amongst them, raised over $2 billion on the Australian capital market.
(2) If so, is this figure correct.
(3) If the figure is correct, what sum has been raised by (a) foreign-owned or controlled corporations and (b) Australian companies.
(4) What proportion of the funds has been raised (a) in the form of debt capital and (b) as equity capital.
(5) To what extent has the (a) level oflocal ownership of Australia's resource development been increased and (b) access of other borrowers to funds been restricted by this fund raising.
(6) Has the fund raising had any effect on the level of domestic interest rates; if so, what effect.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable m,ember's question is as follows:
(1) Yes. However, the report in the Australian Financial Review of 9 March 1981 quoted the Deputy Prime Minister as having said that the group of companies had, over the past two years, raised over $1 billion on the Australian capital market, not over $2 billion as stated in the honourable member's question.
(2) The figure mentioned by the Deputy Frime Minister refers to some resources companies, not to all resources

companies. Australian Bureau of Statistics (ABS) data on new capital raisings by all companies listed on Australian Stock Exchanges show that new money raised by listed companies in 1979 and 1980 totalled $l.641 million and $3.006 million respectively, i.e. $4.647 million over the twoyear period.
(3) All but one of the companies involved in the capital raisings referred to by the Deputy Prime Minister are predominantly Australian-owned companies. The exception, CRA Ltd, is a naturalising company for the purposes of the Government's foreign investment policy.
(4) Approximately four-fifths of the capital raisings to which the Deputy Prime Minister referred were in the form of equity capital. The remainder were debt capital raisings. The ABS data referred to in part (2) above show that of the $4.647 million of new capital raised by all listed companies over the two years to December 1980, $2.032 million or 44 per cent was in the form of share issues.
These data are published on an industry basis but do not enable figures to be derived on capital raising by resource companies as such. The data by industry show that 49 per cent of new capital raised by manufacturing industry was by share issues while 94 per cent of the 'other' industry group (which excludes the manufacturing, commerce, finance and property industry groups but includes primary production, mining and quarrying) was by share issues.
(5) (a) It is not possible to quantify the effect of particular capital raisings by selected companies on the overall level of Australian ownership of resource development. Nevertheless, the need to ensure an appropriate level of Australian. equity participation in natural resource projects is recognised by the Australian equity participation guidelines in the Government's foreign investment policy. In the 18 months to December 1980, 9 of the 11 new natural resource development projects that were approved incorporated equity structures that satisfied the guideline level of Australian participation. The other two projects were required, as a condition of approval, to meet the guideline level before production commenced.
(b) In the present Australian context of a very high demand for finance coupled with monetary policies directed to fllstraining that demand within appropriate bounds, it is axiomatic that capital raisings by anyone group'of borrowers can restrict the fund raisings of other groups. It is not possible to measure the extent to which borrowers are being restricted or to identify clearly which groups are being restricted; it is possible, for example, that the companies referred to in this question were themselves restricted in their borrowing plans by the activities of other borrowers including governments and their authorities.
(6) If there is a significant increase in the demand for finance, for example, associated with strengthening investment and economic activities as is occurring in the Australian economy at present, then it is inevitable that there will be some upward pressure on interest rates. The upward pressure on interest rates is one indication of excess demand; it is also one mechanism by which funds are channelled to the more economic projects and resources allocated efficiently. Such pressures, however, are only one of several determinants of interest rates.
Numerical Multifador AssessmJnt System
(Question No. 890)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 25 March 1981:
(1) How many tradesmen with qualifications acceptable in Australia have migrated to Australia under the Numerical

Answers to Questions

2 and 3 June 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2971

Multifactor Assessment System (NUMAS) scheme since its inception in January 1979.
(2) What is the country of origin of each of these tradesmen.
(3) What is the numerical breakdown of these tradesmen by industry.
Mr Macpbee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) 12,753 skilled tradesmen entered Australia between January 1979 and December 1980. Before April 1979, migrants selected under NUMAS were not enumerated separately from those selected under pre-NUMAS criteria, many of whom continued to arrive well into 1980. Also, other tradesmen have entered under Family Reunion, Refugee and Special Eligibility categories, not subject to full NUMAS assessment. However of the number entering since April 1979, 6,289 were in the General Eligibility category of migrants and subject to full NUMAS assessment.
(2) Statistics for skilled tradesmen assessed under NUMAS, by country of last residence, are not available. The following table shows all skilled tradesmen by country of last residence from January 1979 to December 1980.
